Validity of Pledge by Implicit Words
Ruling on the validity of a pledge by implicit words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a pledge created by the buyer's implicit words 'Hold this until I pay the price' and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ambiguity of the term 'Hold'.

Core Idea
A pledge is valid if words indicate its meaning, even without explicit 'pledge'.

In this lesson, we examine a scenario where a buyer, after a sale, gives the seller an item and says 'Hold this until I give you the price.' The Hanafi school, based on the principle that consideration is given to meanings, rules this as a valid pledge. However, Imam Zufar and Abu Yusuf in one narration disagree, arguing that the word 'Hold' is ambiguous and deposit is the lesser obligation. We will analyze the proofs and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify that the buyer's statement 'Hold this until I give you the price' creates a valid pledge according to the Hanafi school.
The ruling is from Abu Hanifa, the relied-upon position.
2
Identify the proof
The proof is the principle that consideration is for meanings, not literal words. The passage also provides analogies from hawala/kafala and sale.
The phrase 'until I give you the price' indicates a pledge.
3
Analyze the opposing view
Imam Zufar and Abu Yusuf (in one narration) hold it is not a pledge because 'Hold' is ambiguous and could mean deposit, the lesser obligation.
They use the principle of presuming the lesser obligation.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the context 'until I give the price' specifies the intent is security, resolving ambiguity. Other contract examples support this.
The context resolves the ambiguity.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under the Hanafi view, the item becomes a pledge with all its rulings (e.g., right to sell on default). Under Zufar's view, it is only a deposit (trust).
This thamarah al-khilaf affects creditors' rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال لبائعه : أمسك هذا حتى أعطي ثمنك فهو رهن) : أي أعطى المشتري البائع شيئاً غير مبيعه وقال : أمسك هذا حتى أعطي ثمنك يكون رهناً ؛ لأنه تلفظ بما ينبئ عن الرهن، والعبرة للمعاني ، وعند زفر " لا يكون رهنا". قوله : وعند زفـر الله ؛ أي قال زفـر : لا يكون رهناً، ومثله عن أبي يوسف له ؛ لأن قوله : أمسك يحتمل الرهن والإيداع، والثاني: أقلهما، فيقضي بثبوته، بخلاف ما إذا قال : أمسكه بدينك أو بمالك علي ؛ لأنه لما قابله بالدين أو بالمال فقد عين الرهن. ولنا ما قاله الشارح الله بقوله : لأنه تلفظ بما ينبئ عن الرهن ، وهو الحبس إلى إيفاء الثمن، والعبرة في العقود للمعاني، حتى كانت الكفالة بشرط براءة الأصيل حوالة، والحوالة بشرط عدم براءة المحيل كفالة. ألا ترى أنه لو قال : ملكتك هذا بكذا يكون بيعاً ؛ للتصريح بموجب البيع، كأنه قال له : بعتك هكذا أو أطلق في قوله هذا ، فشمل الثوب المبيع وغيره، إذ لا فرق بين أن يكون ذلك الثوب هو المشترى أو لم يكن بعد أن كان بعد القبض ؛ لأنَّ البيع بعد القبض يصلح أن يكون رهناً بثمنه، حتى يثبت فيه حكم الرهن ، بخلاف ما إذا كان قبل القبض ؛ لأنه محبوس بالثمن وضمانه.
So if he says to his seller: 'Hold this until I give you the price', then it is a pledge. That is, the buyer gives the seller something other than his purchase and says 'Hold this until I give you the price', it becomes a pledge because he uttered something that indicates the pledge, and consideration is for meanings. And according to Zufar: it is not a pledge. He said: And according to Zufar — may Allah have mercy on him — i.e., Zufar said: it is not a pledge, and similarly from Abu Yusuf, because his statement 'Hold' can mean pledge or deposit, and the latter is the lesser of the two, so it is presumed to be established, unlike if he says 'Hold it for your debt' or 'for what you have against me', because when he connects it to the debt or money, he specifies the pledge. Our argument is what the commentator said: because he uttered something that indicates the pledge, which is detention until payment of the price, and consideration in contracts is for meanings, such that a guarantee with the condition of the original's discharge is a transfer (hawala), and a transfer with the condition that the transferor is not discharged is a guarantee (kafala). Do you not see that if he said 'I give you ownership of this for such', it is a sale, due to explicit mention of the requisite of sale, as if he said to him 'I sell you' thus, or he used the word 'this' generally, so it includes the garment sold or other, for there is no difference between that garment being the purchased one or not, after it is after delivery, because a sale after delivery is suitable to be a pledge for its price, so the ruling of pledge is established, unlike if it is before delivery, because it is detained for the price and its liability.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and includes a dispute. It addresses the case where a buyer, after purchasing an item, gives the seller another object and says 'Hold this until I give you the price.' According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa), this creates a valid pledge because the words indicate the meaning of a pledge, even without using the word 'pledge'. This is based on the principle that consideration is given to meanings and intentions, not literal words. The school cites analogies: if a guarantee is made with the condition of discharging the original debtor, it becomes a hawala (transfer), and vice versa. Also, if someone says 'I give you ownership of this for such', it is a sale. Imam Zufar and, according to one narration, Abu Yusuf, disagree: they say 'Hold' could also mean a deposit (wadi'ah), and since deposit is the lesser of the two (lesser obligation), it should be presumed. However, the Hanafi response is that when the buyer says 'until I give you the price', the context specifies that the intent is pledge. Furthermore, the commentary clarifies that this ruling applies whether the item given is the purchased item itself (after delivery) or a different item, because after delivery, the purchased item can be held as a pledge for its price. Before delivery, the purchased item is already held for the price, so the buyer's statement then would not create an additional pledge.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge; a contract where property is held as security for a debt.
إيداع (īdā'): A deposit; a contract where property is left for safekeeping.
عبرة للمعاني (‘ibrah li-l-ma‘ānī): Consideration is given to meanings and intentions, not just literal words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer says to the seller 'Hold this until I pay you the price', what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is a valid pledge.
B) It is a deposit only.
C) It is void due to ambiguity.
D) It is a sale.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is valid if the words used indicate the meaning of a pledge, even without the term 'rahn', based on the principle of considering meanings.

Pledging One Item to Two Creditors: Validity and Effect
Ruling on the validity and effect of a multi-creditor pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for pledging one item to two creditors and explain that the entire item serves as security for each debt, not merely half.

Core Idea
One item pledged to two creditors is valid; the whole secures each debt.

This lesson presents a concise ruling from the Hanafi school on a common transaction: can a single item be used as collateral for two separate debts? The answer is yes, and the key point is that the entire item stands as security for each creditor, not just a portion. This rule highlights the nature of rahn as a security contract where the pledged item is detained collectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pledging one specific tangible item ('ayn) to two creditors for separate debts. The ruling is that this pledge is valid (sahih).
2
Understand the legal effect
The phrase 'kulluhu rahn min kull minhuma' means the entire item becomes detained (mahbus) for the debt of each creditor. This is the legal effect: each creditor has a security right over the whole item.
3
Distinguish from the misconception
The passage explicitly negates the misunderstanding that half the item is pledged to each creditor. Instead, the whole item is subject to both debts. Upon default, the item is sold and the proceeds are divided proportionally among the creditors.
This is crucial because it affects the creditors' rights in case of default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن رهن عيناً من رجلين بدين كل منهما صح، وكله رهن من كل منهما : أي يصير كله محبوساً بدين كل واحد ، لا أنّ نصفه يكون رهناً عند هذا
And if he pledges one item to two men for a debt due to each, it is valid, and the entire item is a pledge from each of them: meaning the whole becomes detained for the debt of each, not that half is a pledge with this one.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This is a concise ruling (memorization anchor). It states that if a person pledges a single specific item (ayn) to two creditors, each for a separate debt, the pledge is valid. The legal effect is that the entire pledged item is considered detained (mahbus) for the debt of each creditor. That is, each creditor has a claim against the whole item, not just a half share. This means that upon default, the item can be sold to satisfy both debts proportionally. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the entire item serves as security for each debt, which is a unique feature of rahn as a security contract. This ruling is straightforward and is taken from the relied-upon texts.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge; a contract where property is held as security for a debt.
عين (‘ayn): A specific, tangible item (not a debt or benefit).
كله رهن من كل منهما (kulluhu rahn min kull minhumā): The entire item is a pledge with each of them; each creditor has a security right over the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man pledges one cow to two creditors for separate debts, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The pledge is invalid because one item cannot secure two debts.
B) It is valid, and half the cow is pledged to each creditor.
C) It is valid, and the entire cow is pledged to both creditors.
D) It is valid only if the debts are equal.

Key Takeaway
Pledging one specific item to two creditors is valid; the entire item secures each debt proportionally.

Implied Pledge by Expression of Detention
Ruling that a buyer's statement to hold item constitutes a pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers a buyer's statement 'Hold this until I give you your price' as a valid pledge, and identify the opposing position of Zufar and Abu Yusuf, including their proofs and the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
In Hanafi contracts, meanings override words—so a buyer's 'Hold this until I pay' creates a valid pledge, not a deposit.

This passage addresses a subtle question: when a buyer hands over an item to the seller and says 'Hold this until I pay you,' does that form a pledge (rahn) or a deposit (wadi'ah)? The matn rules it as a valid pledge, and the commentary expands on the reasoning, invoking the fundamental Hanafi principle that consideration in contracts is given to meanings, not literal words. However, Zufar and Abu Yusuf differ, arguing that the word 'hold' is ambiguous and the lesser meaning (deposit) should prevail. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify when an implied expression is legally effective.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling of the matn
Identify what the matn (base text) states regarding the buyer's statement. It declares that such a statement makes the item a pledge (rahn).
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that Zufar and Abu Yusuf hold a different opinion: the statement does not create a pledge but rather a deposit (wadi'ah).
This is an intra-Hanafi difference.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The commentary provides the proof: the buyer's utterance ('Hold this until I give you your price') indicates the meaning of detention for payment of the price, which is the essence of a pledge. The maxim 'al-ibrah li-l-ma'ani' (consideration is given to meanings) is applied.
Additionally, the commentary analogizes to other contracts like sale where similar implied expressions are effective.
4
Analyze the opposing proof
Zufar and Abu Yusuf argue that the word 'hold' is ambiguous between pledge and deposit. Since deposit is the lesser (i.e., more basic) meaning, it should be assumed unless specified otherwise.
They also point out that if the context is specific (e.g., 'Hold it for your debt'), then it is clearly a pledge.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: if it is a pledge, the seller can sell the item upon default; if a deposit, it is merely a trust and cannot be sold. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is a valid pledge, as stated in the matn and supported by the commentary's strong proof.
The tarjih is based on the principle that context removes ambiguity and the meaning is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال لبائعه : أمسك هذا حتى أعطي ثمنك فهو رهن) : أي أعطى المشتري البائع شيئاً غير مبيعه وقال : أمسك هذا حتى أعطي ثمنك يكون رهناً ؛ لأنه تلفظ بما ينبئ عن الرهن، والعبرة للمعاني ، وعند زفر " لا يكون رهنا". قوله : وعند زفـر الله ؛ أي قال زفـر : لا يكون رهناً، ومثله عن أبي يوسف له ؛ لأن قوله : أمسك يحتمل الرهن والإيداع، والثاني: أقلهما، فيقضي بثبوته، بخلاف ما إذا قال : أمسكه بدينك أو بمالك علي ؛ لأنه لما قابله بالدين أو بالمال فقد عين الرهن. ولنا ما قاله الشارح الله بقوله : لأنه تلفظ بما ينبئ عن الرهن ، وهو الحبس إلى إيفاء الثمن، والعبرة في العقود للمعاني، حتى كانت الكفالة بشرط براءة الأصيل حوالة، والحوالة بشرط عدم براءة المحيل كفالة. ألا ترى أنه لو قال : ملكتك هذا بكذا يكون بيعاً ؛ للتصريح بموجب البيع، كأنه قال له : بعتك هكذا أو أطلق في قوله هذا ، فشمل الثوب المبيع وغيره، إذ لا فرق بين أن يكون ذلك الثوب هو المشترى أو لم يكن بعد أن كان بعد القبض ؛ لأنَّ البيع بعد القبض يصلح أن يكون رهناً بثمنه، حتى يثبت فيه حكم الرهن ، بخلاف ما إذا كان قبل القبض ؛ لأنه محبوس بالثمن وضمانه.
If he says to his seller: 'Hold this until I give you your price' then it is a pledge. Meaning: the buyer gives the seller something other than the purchased item and says 'Hold this until I give you your price' then it is a pledge because he uttered something that indicates a pledge, and consideration is given to meanings. According to Zufar, it is not a pledge. His statement: According to Zufar; i.e., Zufar said it is not a pledge, and similar is narrated from Abu Yusuf, because his statement 'Hold' could mean pledge or deposit, and the latter is the lesser of the two, so it is taken as established. Unlike if he says 'Hold it for your debt' or 'for what you have against me' because by linking it to the debt he specified a pledge. Our proof is what the commentator said: because he uttered something indicating a pledge, which is detention until payment of the price, and consideration in contracts is given to meanings; such that surety with the condition of freeing the principal is considered a transfer (hawala), and a transfer with the condition of non-freedom of the transferor is considered a surety (kafala). Do you not see that if he says 'I give you ownership of this for such amount' it is a sale due to explicit expression of the consequence of sale, as if he said 'I sell to you'. And if he says this generally, it includes the sold garment and other things; there is no difference whether that garment is the purchased one or not, as long as it is after possession, because the purchased item after possession is suitable to be a pledge for its price, such that the ruling of pledge is established for it. Unlike if it is before possession because it is detained by the price and its guarantee.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of a pledge when the buyer says to the seller 'Hold this until I pay you the price' without explicitly saying 'pledge'. The matn states it is a valid pledge. The commentator, Sharh al-Wiqaya, explains that the buyer's statement indicates the meaning of pledge, and the legal maxim is that consideration is given to meanings, not just words. Zufar and according to one narration Abu Yusuf hold that it is not a pledge because the word 'hold' is ambiguous between pledge (rahn) and deposit (wadi‘ah), and the lesser meaning (deposit) is preferred, so it is taken as a deposit. The author of the sharh refutes this by arguing that the context of the statement (after purchase and referencing the price) clearly indicates the intention of pledge. He supports his argument by analogizing to other contracts where the meaning prevails over the wording, such as surety/transfer and the phrase 'I give you ownership' which indicates sale even without the word 'sell'. Furthermore, he distinguishes between a case where the item is handed over after possession (which can be a pledge) versus before possession (where it is already detained by the price and guarantee). Thus, the correct Hanafi position is that the verbal expression creates a valid pledge. The relied-upon fatwa is that it is a pledge, as the text indicates.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is detained as security for a debt, which may be sold if the debt is not paid.
إيداع (īdāʿ): Deposit; a contract where one party leaves property with another for safekeeping.
العبرة للمعاني (al-‘ibrah li-l-ma‘ānī): The legal principle that consideration is given to the meanings and intentions behind expressions, not just the literal words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a buyer says to the seller 'Hold this until I pay you the price' while handing over an item, what is the ruling?
A) It is a valid pledge (rahn).
B) It is considered a deposit (wadi‘ah).
C) It is invalid because the word 'rahn' was not used.
D) It is a sale (bay‘).

Key Takeaway
An implied expression in a contract is legally effective if it conveys the intended meaning, especially when context removes ambiguity.

Pledging One Item to Multiple Creditors: The Whole for Each Debt
Ruling that one item can be pledged fully to each of two creditors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging a single item to two creditors, explain the distinction from sale/gift regarding shuyu', and apply the rules for turning and destruction.

Core Idea
In pledge, the whole item is detained for each creditor's debt; ownership transfer would require division.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a key distinction: when a single item is pledged to two creditors, the Hanafi school holds that the entire item serves as security for each debt. This ruling is based on the nature of pledge (rahn) as a contract of detention, not ownership transfer. In contrast, a gift or sale to two persons would require division of the item because ownership cannot be complete for both. Let's examine the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the act: pledging one item to two creditors for separate debts. The ruling is sahih (valid) and the whole item is a pledge for each debt, not half for each.
2
Understand the legal basis (talil)
The ruling is based on the nature of rahn: it is a contract of detention (حبس), not ownership transfer. Therefore, the same item can be detained for multiple rights without requiring division.
3
Compare with sale/gift (qiyas rebuttal)
An objection arises: sale and gift to two persons require shuyu' (joint ownership) according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Why not in pledge? The answer: because sale/gift transfer ownership, which cannot be held in full by two persons; pledge only detains, and full detention for each is possible. Also, division would prevent valid possession (qabd).
This is the key distinction that upholds the ruling.
4
Apply the ruling to sub-scenarios
If creditors take turns, each is a trustee (adl) for the other. If the item perishes, each is considered to have taken his share (istifa') and liability is apportioned. If one debt is paid, the whole item remains a pledge for the other debt.
5
Identify the Hanafi position's authority
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and is the mufta bihi position. It is supported by clear reasoning that distinguishes pledge from ownership contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن رهن عيناً من رجلين بدين لكلِّ منهما صح، وكله رهن من كل منهما ) : أي يصير كله محبوساً بدين كل واحد ، لا أنّ نصفه يكون رهناً عند هذا ونصفه عند ذلك، وهذا بخلاف الهبة من رجلين ، حيث لا يصح عند أبي حنيفة ه ، فإنّ الأول لا يقبلُ الوصف بالتجزؤ بخلاف الهبة. (وإذا تهايئا فكل في نوبته كالعدل في حق الآخر (١) ، ولو هلكَ ضَمِنَ كل حصته)، فإنّ عند الهلاك يصيرُ كل مستوفياً حصته، والإستيفاء مما يتجزأ، فإن قضى دين أحدهما، فكله رهن للآخر) ؛ لما مر (١) [۱] واحدة، ولا شيوع فيه، قال صاحب «العناية» (۳) أخذاً من «البناية»(۳) قيل: هو منقوض بما إذا باع من رجلين أو وهب من رجلين على قول أبي يوسف له ومحمد ه ، فإنّ العقد فيهما أضيف إلى جميع الدين في صفقة واحدة، وفيه الشيوع، حتى كان المبيع والمرهون بينهما نصفين، كما لو نص على المناصفة. والجواب : إنّ إضافة العقدِ إلى اثنين توجب الشيوع فيما يكون العقد مفيداً للملك ؛ كالهبة والبيع ، فإنّ العين الواحدة لا يمكن أن تكون مملوكة لشخصين على الكمال، فتجعل شائعة، فتنقسم عليهما للجواز، والرهن غير مفيد للملك، وإنما يفيد الاحتباس ، ويجوز أن تكون العين الواحدة محتبسة بحقين على الكمال، فيمتنعُ الشيوع فيه تحرياً للجواز ؛ لكون القبض لا بدَّ منه في الرهن ، والشيوع يمنع عنه. إلى هنا كلامه. أقول : بخلاف الهبة من رجلين حيث لا يجوز عند الإمام ؛ لأنَّ العين تنقسم عليهما لاستحالة ثبوت الملك لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما في الكلّ ، فيثبت الشيوع ضرورة، وقد تقدَّمَ بيانه في «كتاب الهبة»، وكلّ واحد منهما في نوبته كالعدل في حق الآخر، هذا إذا كان مما لا يتجزأ ظاهر، وإن كان مما يتجزّاً وجب أن يحبس كل واحد منهما النصف، فإن دفع أحدهما كله إلى الآخر، وجب أن يضمن الدافع عند الإمام خلافاً لهما. [1] قوله : لما مرّ أنّ كلّه رهن ؛ يعني أنّ جميع العين رهن في يد كلِّ واحدٍ منهما
And if one pledges an item to two men for a debt owed to each, it is valid, and the whole is a pledge from each of them. Meaning: the whole becomes detained for the debt of each, not that half is a pledge with this one and half with that one. And this differs from gifting to two men, which is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, because the former (pledge) does not admit of division by nature, unlike gift. And if they take turns, then each during his turn is like a trustee with respect to the other; if it perishes, each guarantees his share. Because at the time of destruction, each becomes one who has taken his share, and taking is divisible. If the debt of one is paid, then the whole is a pledge for the other; as was mentioned (that it is all a pledge). [Note: The commentary continues discussing the issue of shuyu' (joint ownership) and the difference from gift.]
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on pledging a single item to two creditors. The matn states it is valid and the entire item is a pledge for each debt, not half for each. The commentator explains that this is because the nature of a pledge (rahn) is to detain the entire item as security, and it does not require division (tajazzu). This is different from a gift (hiba) to two persons, which is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because the ownership of a single item cannot be held by two persons in entirety without division; necessity forces division (shuyu‘) in gifts, but in pledges, since it is not about ownership but detention, the entire item can be detained for each right. The text also discusses the scenario where the two creditors take turns keeping the pledged item: each acts as a trustee (adl) for the other during his turn. If the item is destroyed while in one's possession, that person is liable for the share of the other because destruction is considered taking of one's share (istifa'). If one debt is paid, the whole item remains a pledge for the other debt. The commentary further addresses an objection: if the contract with two persons necessitates shuyu‘ in sale and gift according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, why not in pledge? The answer is that because sale and gift transfer ownership, the item must be divided; pledge only detains, and it is permissible for the same item to be detained for multiple rights in entirety, and having shuyu‘ in a pledge would hinder valid possession (qabd), so it is avoided to uphold the contract. Thus, the Hanafi position is clear.

Key Terms
شيوع (shuyū‘): Joint ownership; when an item is owned by multiple persons in undivided shares.
تجزؤ (tajazzu’): Divisibility; the property can be split into parts without loss of function.
استيفاء (istīfā’): Taking or receiving one's due; here, when the pledged item is destroyed, each creditor is considered to have taken his share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if one item is pledged to two creditors for separate debts, what is the status of the pledge?
A) Valid, and each creditor has a claim to half of the item.
B) Invalid because the item cannot be held for two debts.
C) Valid, and the whole item is a pledge for both debts.
D) Valid only if the item is physically divided.

Key Takeaway
One item can be pledged fully to each of multiple creditors because pledge is detention, not ownership; thus no division (shuyu') is required.

Two Claimants of the Same Pledge: The Rule of Tahatur
Ruling on mutual nullification of conflicting pawn claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants prove a pledge of the same item and explain the reasoning based on impossibility of complete possession for two.

Core Idea
When two claimants each prove a pledge of the same item, both claims are nullified because one item cannot be wholly pledged to two.

This lesson addresses a unique case in Hanafi fiqh where two individuals each claim that the same item was pledged to them separately. The scholars rule that both claims are nullified (tahatur) due to logical impossibility. This ruling is based on the nature of pawn (rahn) as a complete detention (habs) that cannot be shared.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two claimants each assert that a specific item (a slave) was pledged to them by the same pawner. Both produce witnesses. Determine the core elements: the item, the claimants, the pawner.
The pawner is Zayd; the claimants are two men.
2
Analyze the conflict
Each claimant claims exclusive right to hold the item as a pawn. Since the item is one, it cannot be in complete possession of both simultaneously. Recognize that the evidence is contradictory and cannot both be accepted.
The Hanafi principle: one item cannot serve as a pawn for two separate debts in its entirety at the same time.
3
Apply the ruling of tahatur
Because both claims cannot be upheld, and no priority can be established, the only option is to nullify both claims. The pawn is returned to the pawner or his heirs. This is called tahatur (mutual nullification).
The judge does not split the item; splitting would create a share (shuyu') which invalidates the pawn contract's requirement of exclusive possession.
4
Consider the objection and answer
An objection arises: if one claimant later receives his debt, does his right to the pawn cease? The answer is that his right remains until the pawn is physically returned to the pawner, so any excess received must be returned.
This ensures justice and clarifies that the pawn is not considered divided even after partial satisfaction.

Source Text

[2] For each one... because it is impossible for a single thing to be a pledge wholly for this one and wholly for that one in one state. [3] For each half because it leads to sharing, so implementation of both becomes impossible, and mutual nullification is determined. Commentary
This passage deals with a peculiar case: two men each claim that a third person (Zayd) pledged the same slave to him separately. Each brings evidence. The ruling is that both claims are nullified (tahatur). The reasoning is that it is impossible for one item to be wholly pledged to two different people simultaneously. The judge cannot give preference to one nor divide it because that would create a share (shuyu') which is invalid in a pawn contract due to the requirement of exclusive possession for the purpose of detention (habs). The passage also addresses an objection about the case where one debtor's debt is paid and the pawn is later destroyed; the answer is that each pledgee still has a right in the pawn until it reaches the pawner, so if one received his debt, he might have taken more than his share and must return the excess. This is a Hanafi ruling based on istihsan, emphasizing that the pawn is considered as a whole for each creditor until the pawn is returned.

Key Terms
تهاتر (tahatur): باطل شدن متقابل دو دعوا به دلیل تعذر اثبات
رهن (rahn): عقدی که به موجب آن مالی برای وثیقه دین داده می‌شود

D) برده فروخته می‌شود و قیمت آن بین آنها تقسیم می‌شود

Key Takeaway
Conflicting claims to a pawn are mutually nullified because a pawn requires exclusive possession.

Death of the Pawner: Dividing the Pawn Between Two Claimants
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pawn division after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the division of a pawn after the pawner's death when two claimants prove their rights, and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
After death, the pawn is halved because the purpose shifts from detention to sale, making sharing harmless.

When a pawner dies and two people each hold the pawn as security for a debt, what happens? In life, each keeps the whole, but after death the purpose changes. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Hanifa and Muhammad use istihsan to divide the pawn, while Abu Yusuf sticks to qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the situation: the pawner dies, the pawn is with two claimants, and each proves their claim.
This is the precise case addressed.
2
Identify the dispute
Two positions: Abu Hanifa & Muhammad rule each gets half (istihsan). Abu Yusuf rules each keeps the whole (qiyas).
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the qiyas argument
Abu Yusuf argues the ruling should remain as in life because detention for fulfillment is the original purpose and sharing harms it.
Qiyas: life and death are irrelevant to the contractual purpose.
4
Understand the istihsan argument
After death, the purpose shifts from detention (habs) to sale (istifa). Sharing does not harm sale, so the pawn is halved.
Istihsan overrides qiyas due to change of purpose.
5
Apply the analogy
Analogous to conflicting marriage claims: in life they cancel, after death inheritance is divided because it can be shared.
Strengthens the istihsan reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو مات راهنة والرهن معهما ، فبرهن كل كذلك كان مع كل نصفه رهناً بحقه (ولو مات راهنه والرهن معهما ، فبرهن كل كذلك كان مع كل نصفه رهناً بحقه)، هذا قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وهو استحسان، وعند أبي يوسف له هذا ، وهو القياس " كما في الحياة، وجه الاستحسان : أنّ حكمَهُ في الحياةِ ) . الحبس، والشيوع يضره، وبعد الممات الاستيفاء بالبيع في الدين والشيوع لا باطل، [1] قوله : والرهن معهما ؛ قيّد بالمعيّة ؛ لأنه إذا كان في يد أحدهما كان صاحب اليد أسبق لدلالة يده عليه، وسبق التاريخ بمنزلة اليد في هذا الحكم، ولا فرق بين كونه معهما وكونه خارجاً عن يد كلّ واحدٍ منهما قاله الجلبي [2] قوله : هذا باطل، وهو القياس ... الخ ؛ لأنَّ الحبس للاستيفاء حكم أصلي مقصود بعقد الرهن، فيكون القضاء به قضاء بعقد الرهن، وإنه باطل الشيوع كما في حالة الحياة. [3]قوله : إن حكمه في الحياة ... الخ ؛ أي حالة الحياة هو الحبس، وهو المقصود، والشيوع يضره، وبعد الممات الاستيفاء بالبيع والشيوع لا يضره، وصار كما إذا ادعى الرجلان نكاح امرأة، أو ادّعت اختان النكاح على رجل ، وأقاموا البينة، تهاترت في حالة الحياة، ويقضي بالميراث بينهم بعد الممات ؛ لأنَّ الميراث يقبل الانقسام. حجامي (۱) إذ بعد الممات ليس له الحكم إلا الاستيفاء بأن يبيعه في الدين شاع أو لم يشع. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر» (٢ : ٥٩٩). (۲) في ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٩٧).
If the pawner dies and the pawn is with both of them, then if each proves likewise, then each has half of it as a pledge for his right. (If his pawner dies and the pawn is with both of them, and each proves likewise, then each has half of it as a pledge for his right), this is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and it is istihsan. According to Abu Yusuf, he has this (i.e., the whole), and it is qiyas as in life. The reason for istihsan: His ruling in life is detention, and sharing harms it, and after death the fulfillment is by sale in the debt and sharing does not harm it. [1] He said: 'and the pawn is with both of them' is conditioned by being together, because if it is in the hand of one, the possessor is prior due to his possession; and priority of date is like possession in this ruling; and there is no difference between it being with both or outside the hand of each of them, said al-Jalabi. [2] He said: 'this is void, and it is qiyas' because detention for fulfillment is an original purpose intended by the pawn contract, so judging by it is judging by the pawn contract, and it is void due to sharing as in the case of life. [3] He said: 'his ruling in life' means the state of life is detention, which is intended, and sharing harms it; after death, the fulfillment by sale does not harm it. It becomes like when two men claim marriage to a woman, or two sisters claim marriage to a man, and they bring evidence: they are mutually nullified during life, but after death inheritance is judged between them because inheritance can be divided. (1) Because after death, there is no ruling except fulfillment by way of selling it for the debt, whether it is shared or not. See Majma' al-Anhur (2:599). (2) In Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p. 597).
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a scenario where the pawner dies while the pawn is in the possession of two claimants who each prove their claim. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani rule (by istihsan) that each creditor gets half of the pawn as a pledge for his debt. Abu Yusuf rules (by qiyas) that each retains the entire pawn as in life, because the pawn contract remains. The reasoning for istihsan: during life, the purpose of pawn is detention (habs), which requires exclusive possession, and sharing is detrimental. However, after death, the purpose shifts to sale (istifa') to recover the debt, and sharing does not harm that. This is analogous to conflicting marriage claims: during life, they are nullified, but after death, inheritance is divided because it can be shared.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): عدول از قیاس به دلیل اقتضای مصلحت یا تغییر غرض
حبس (habs): نگهداری مال گرو برای وثیقه دین

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The pawner's death invalidates the contract.
B) The purpose of the pawn changes from detention to sale.
C) Both claimants have equal proof.
D) The pawn was originally owned half by each claimant.

Key Takeaway
After death, the pawn is divided among proven claimants because the purpose shifts from detention to sale, making sharing harmless.

Pledge with a Third Party: The Necessity of Possession (Rahn 'Inda 'Adl)
Inter-madhhab dispute on pledge possession by a third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledge with a third party, explain the dual-hand argument, and compare the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
The third party’s hand is both a trust and a liability — possession by him completes the pledge.

When a pledgor and mortgagee agree to deposit the pledged item with a neutral third party (‘adl), a dispute arises: does the pledge become binding only upon that third party’s possession? The Hanafi school affirms, citing the unique legal nature of the ‘adl’s hand. Imam Malik, in one narration, holds that possession is unnecessary because the ‘adl acts as the mortgagor’s agent. This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah unpacks the Hanafi position, its proof, and the practical consequences of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the dispute
What do the Hanafis say about possession by the third party, and what does Malik say? Note that the Hanafi position is the standard in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The passage begins: 'يتم الرهن بقبض عدل' — the pledge is completed by possession of a third party.
2
Parse the Hanafi argument
Understand the dual-nature analysis: the third party's hand is like the mortgagor's in form (trust) and like the mortgagee's in substance (liability).
The commentary explains: 'يده على الصورة يد المالك، وفي المالية يد المرتهن' — his hand in form is the owner's, in financial substance the mortgagee's.
3
Understand the Maliki counter-argument
Malik argues that the third party's hand is solely the mortgagor's, so possession by the third party does not change the mortgagor's control.
The proof: the third party has recourse against the mortgagor if he is held liable, indicating the mortgagor is the true possessor.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi response reconcile the apparent contradiction? The third party is treated as two persons: a trustee for the mortgagor and a guarantor for the mortgagee.
The commentary states: 'فنزَّل منزلة شخصين' — he is considered two persons.
5
Extract the practical consequences
What happens if the third party delivers the pledge to either party or if the pledge perishes? Determine liability and the thamarah al-khilaf.
If the third party wrongfully delivers, he is liable. If the pledge perishes with him, it is a loss to the mortgagee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرهن عند عدل يتم الرهن بقبض عدلٍ شُرِطَ وضعه عنده، هذا عندنا، وقال مالك لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ يدَ يد المالك؛ ولهذا يرجع عليه عند الاستحقاق، فانعدم القبض. [1] [2] [3] [4] يده على الصورة يد المالك، وفي المالية يد المرتهن؛ لأن يده يد ضمان والمضمون الماليَّة، فنزَّل منزلة شخصين، ولا أخذ لأحدهما منه وضمن بدفعه إلى أحدهما، وهلكه معه هلك رهن، فإن وكل العدلُ أو غيره ببيعه إذا حل أجله صح، فإن شرط في الرهن لا ينعزل. [1] قوله : يده على الصورة ... الخ؛ يعني أن قبض العدل كقبض المرتهن، فيتم به الرهن، وهذا لأن اليد في باب الرهن على الصورة أمانة، وعلى المعنى مضمون، فكانت يده ... الخ. «كفاية» [2] قوله : ولا أخذ لأحدهما منه؛ أي من العدل؛ لأنه تعلق به حقهما لأن حق الراهن تعلّق بالحفظ في يده وأمانته، وحق المرتهن في الاستيفاء، فلا يملك كل واحد منهما إبطال حق آخر، ولو شرطا أن يقبضه المرتهن ثم جعلاه على يد عدل جاز؛ لأن ما جاز للعدل أن يقوم مقام المرتهن في الابتداء، فكذلك في البقاء. [3] قوله : وضمن ... الخ؛ أي وإن دفع العدل الرهن إلى الراهن والمرتهن يضمن؛ لأنه متى دفع إلى المرتهن فقد دفع الأمانة بغير إذنه، كما لو دفع إلى أجنبي، ومتى دفع إلى الراهن فقد أبطل تلك اليد، والحبس على المرتهن؛ فإنه يثبت له ملك اليد والحبس بقبض العدل، وإبطال ملك السيّد كإبطال ملك العين في إيجاب الضمان، فإن من أتلف الرهن يضمن للمرتهن كما يضمن الراهن. [4] قوله : صح؛ لأن الراهن مالك، فله أن يوكل من شاء من الأهل ببيع ماله مطلقاً ومنجزاً؛ لأن الوكالة يجوز تعليقها بالشرط؛ لكونها من الإسقاطات؛ لأن المانع من التصرف حق المالك، وبالتسلط على بيعه أسقط حقه، والإسقاطات يجوز تعليقها بالشروط. [0] قوله : لا ينعزل ... الخ؛ لأنها لما شرطت في ضمن عقد الرهن صار وصفاً من أوصافه وحقاً من حقوقه، ألا ترى أنه لزيادة الوثيقة فيلزم بلزوم أصله؛ ولأنه تعلق.
Chapter of the pledge with a third party. The pledge is completed by possession of a third party with the condition that it be placed with him. This is according to us (Hanafis). Malik said: It is not permissible because his hand is the hand of the owner; therefore he (the third party) would have recourse against him (the mortgagor) upon the property being claimed (as owned by another). So possession is absent. [Commentary: The third party acts as deputy of the mortgagee. The third party cannot sell the pledge unless authorized. There is a dispute about Malik's position—some say he does not require possession for the contract to be binding, others say he does. The third party's hand is like the mortgagee's in effect (for liability) and like the mortgagor's in form (for trust). Therefore, neither party can take the pledge from him, and if he delivers it to either, he is liable. If the pledge perishes with him, it is a loss of a pledge. If the third party or someone else is authorized to sell it upon maturity, it is valid. If such authorization is stipulated in the pledge contract, the agent cannot be removed.]
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with the ruling on a pledge (rahn) placed in the custody of a neutral third party ('adl). The primary ruling—which is the Hanafi position—is that the pledge is only completed (yatimmu) by actual possession (qabd) of that third party, provided the contract stipulated his custody. This is contrary to Imam Malik, who, according to one narration, holds that the pledge is binding simply by the contract itself, without requiring possession. The Hanafi rebuttal is that the third party's hand has a dual nature: in form (surah) it is like the mortgagor's hand (thus a trust), but in substance (ma'nan) it is like the mortgagee's hand (thus subject to liability). Consequently, the third party is treated like two persons: he acts as a trustee for the mortgagor and as a guarantor for the mortgagee. Therefore, neither the mortgagor nor the mortgagee has the right to take the pledge from the third party without the other's consent. If the third party delivers the pledge to either one, he becomes liable for its value. If the pledge is destroyed while in his custody, it is considered to have perished as a pledge (i.e., the mortgagee loses his security). The passage also discusses the validity of appointing an agent to sell the pledge upon maturity: it is valid because the mortgagor has full ownership, and agency can be conditional. If such an authorization is stipulated in the pledge contract itself, it becomes an integral part of the contract and cannot be revoked unilaterally.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): A neutral third party (trustee) with whom a pledge is deposited, trusted by both mortgagor and mortgagee.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession or constructive possession that transfers control; here, possession by the third party is required for the pledge to be completed.
يد (yad): Hand/control; in this context, the third party's hand is analyzed as having both a form (surah) of trust and a substance (ma'nan) of liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the contract without possession
B) Possession by the third party
C) Possession by the mortgagee
D) Written documentation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires possession by the third party for a pledge with an ‘adl to be binding, based on the dual nature of the third party’s hand as both trust and liability.

Rulings on the Trustee and Agent for Sale in Pledge
Commentary on the position of the 'adl, agency for sale, and dismissal conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dual nature of the 'adl's possession, state the conditions under which the 'adl becomes liable, and apply the ruling that a stipulated agency in a pledge contract is irrevocable.

Core Idea
The 'adl's possession is both a trust and a guarantee; agency stipulated in the pledge contract cannot be revoked by the parties.

In this lesson, we explore the commentary on the role of the 'adl (trustee) and the agent appointed to sell a pledged property. The text clarifies that the 'adl's possession has a unique legal character: it is a trust in form but a guarantee in substance. We will also examine the validity and binding nature of agency when stipulated within the pledge contract, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the executor of the agent can step in after the agent's death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal nature of the 'adl's possession
The text describes the 'adl's hand as being a trust in form and a guarantee in meaning. Understand why it is considered guaranteed (because the pledgee's right of retention is established through it).
2
Determine the liability of the 'adl for delivery to one party
The 'adl is liable if he gives the pledge to either the pledgor or the pledgee without the other's consent. Note the basis: he nullifies the other's right.
3
Analyze the validity and revocability of agency stipulated in the pledge
The text rules that such agency is valid and becomes an integral part, making it irrevocable. Identify the reasoning: it is a right of the pledge contract and increases security.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the executor's authority
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ on whether the agent's executor can sell after the agent's death. Follow the proofs and the rebuttal.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario of sale without consent
Determine the outcome when a party sells without permission: the sale is suspended (mawquf) pending ratification.

And neither of the two may take it from him, and he is liable by giving it to one of them, and its destruction with him is the destruction of a pledge. If the 'adl or another appoints an agent to sell it when its term expires, it is valid. If he stipulates [agency] in the pledge, he is not dismissed. The pledgor's right is attached to safekeeping in his hand and his trust, and the pledgee's right is to recover the debt. So neither of them may nullify the right of the other. If they stipulate that the pledgee takes possession and then they place it in the hand of the 'adl, it is permissible, because what is permissible for the 'adl to represent the pledgee initially is likewise permissible in continuance. Nullifying the ownership of the master is like nullifying the ownership of the substance in imposing liability. Relinquishments may be suspended on conditions. His statement: 'But by the death of the agent...' etc.; because agency does not involve inheritance, and because the principal was satisfied with his opinion, not the opinion of another. And from Abu Yusuf, he may do so because the executor of the agent may sell it because the agency is binding, so the executor may do so like the mudarib when he dies, and the goods are merchandise that the executor of the mudarib may sell because it is binding after becoming merchandise. His statement: 'And he does not sell...' etc.; i.e., the pledgor may not sell the pledge except with the consent of the pledgee, because the pledgee has a greater right to its financial value than the pledgor, and the pledgor is unable to deliver it by sale. Likewise, the pledgee may not sell it except with the consent of the pledgor because it is his property and he has not consented to its sale. This is if he has not authorized its sale. [The pledgor] authorized him. But if he sells it without the permission of the pledgor or vice versa, it is suspended pending the ratification of the other party. If he ratifies it, it is valid, and the price becomes a pledge. If he does not ratify it, the sale is not valid, and he may nullify it, and it remains a pledge. This is the summary of what is in Ghayat al-Bayan. That is, it is suspended pending the ratification of the pledgor. If he ratifies, it is valid, and the price becomes a pledge. If he does not ratify, the sale is not valid, and he may nullify it and return it as a pledge.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses key rulings regarding the trustee ('adl) and the agent (wakil) in a pledge contract. The 'adl's possession is described as having a dual nature: in form it is a trust (amana), so the 'adl is not liable for loss without negligence, but in meaning it is a guarantee (daman) because the pledgee's right of retention is established through it. Consequently, neither the pledgor nor the pledgee can unilaterally take the pledge from the 'adl, as each has a distinct right: the pledgor to safekeeping and the pledgee to recovery of the debt. If the 'adl delivers the pledge to either party without the other's consent, he becomes liable for the full value. The text then discusses the validity of appointing an agent to sell the pledge when the debt matures. Such an appointment is valid because the pledgor is the owner and may delegate sale. If the agency is stipulated within the pledge contract itself, it becomes an inseparable part, and the agent cannot be dismissed by the pledgor or pledgee, nor does their death affect it; only the death of the agent terminates the agency. On the death of the agent, the dominant view (Abu Hanifa) is that the agency does not pass to heirs or the executor, while Abu Yusuf holds that the executor may sell, analogizing to the case of a mudarib (silent partner) whose executor may sell the merchandise. Finally, neither the pledgor nor the pledgee may sell the pledge without the other's consent. If a sale occurs without permission, it is suspended (mawquf) pending ratification. If ratified, it is valid and the sale proceeds become a pledge in place of the original item.

Key Terms
العدل (al-‘adl): The trustworthy person who holds the pledged property on behalf of both parties.
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract whereby property is given as security for a debt.
الوكيل (al-wakil): An agent appointed to sell the pledge upon maturity of the debt.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage; the 'adl becomes liable if he delivers the pledge to one party without the other's consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The 'adl's possession is a trust that carries a guarantee, and a stipulated agency in a pledge contract becomes an integral part, binding the parties until the agent dies.

Death of Agent Terminates Agency in Pledge: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on when agency for sale of pawn ends, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when agency for sale of a pawn ends, and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the agent's executor.

Core Idea
Agency in pledge ends only with the agent's death; his authority is personal and does not pass to heirs or executors.

In Hanafi law, agency is a contract of trust. When a pawn is given as security, the agent appointed to sell it holds a unique authority. This passage clarifies that the agency survives the pledgor's death and even a discharge by the pledgor, but it is extinguished by the agent's death. However, Imam Abu Yusuf raises a compelling analogy to the mudarib (sleeping partner), arguing that the agent's executor should be able to complete the sale. This intra-Hanafi dispute hinges on whether the agency is binding (lazim) or a mere trust. Understanding this distinction helps you grasp the Hanafi approach to agency and its limits.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on termination of agency in pawn sale
Identify the exact conditions under which the agency ends according to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Note the negative: it does not end by discharge or death of pledgor/mortgagee, only by death of agent.
2
Identify the opposing view of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that the agent's executor (wasi) may sell the pawn after the agent's death. This is the contested element.
3
Follow the opponent's proof: the mudarib analogy
Understand the qiyas: just as a mudarib's executor can sell partnership goods after the mudarib's death because the mudaraba becomes binding, so too the agent's executor should be able to sell the pawn because the agency is binding.
The opponent's dalil is aqli (analogical reasoning).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response rejecting the analogy
The majority rejects the analogy because agency in rahn is not binding like mudaraba; it is a contract of trust (amanah) that does not pass to heirs. The principal consented to the agent's judgment, not that of his executor.
The response distinguishes between the nature of agency and mudaraba.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the agent dies, the majority requires a new appointment; Abu Yusuf's view would allow the executor to proceed without delay. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
This affects whether a sale after the agent's death is valid without fresh authorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعزل ولا بموت الراهن أو المرتهن بل بموت الوكيل، وله بيعه بغيبة ورثته، سواء كان الوكيل المرتهن أو العدل أو غيرهما، وإذا مات الوكيل لا يقوم وارثه أو وصيه مقامه عندنا، وعند أبي يوسف إن وصي الوكيل يملك بيعه، وله بيعه بغيبة ورثته: أي للوكيل بيع المرهون بغيبة ورثة الراهن. به حق المرتهن، وفي العزل إتواء حقه، وصار كالوكيل بالخصومة بطلب «هداية». [1]قوله : بل بموت الوكيل ... الخ ; لأن الوكالة لا يجري فيها الإرث ; ولأن الموكل رضي برأيه لا برأي غيره، وعن أبي يوسف له ; لأن وصي الوكيل يملك بيعه ; لأن الوكالة لازمة، فيملك الوصي كالمضارب إذا مات، والمالية عروض يملك وصي المضارب بيعها لما أنه لازم بعدما صار عروضا.
By discharge and not by death of the pledgor or mortgagee, but by death of the agent, and he may sell it in the absence of his heirs, whether the agent is the mortgagee or the trustee or someone else. And when the agent dies, his heir or executor does not take his place in our view, but according to Abu Yusuf, the executor of the agent may sell it. And he may sell it in the absence of his heirs: meaning the agent may sell the pawn in the absence of the pledgor's heirs. The mortgagee's right is attached to it, and in discharge there is destruction of his right, and it becomes like an agent in litigation by request. [1] He said: Rather by death of the agent... etc.; because inheritance does not apply to agency; and because the principal was satisfied with his opinion not with the opinion of another, and from Abu Yusuf he has it, because the executor of the agent may sell it; because the agency is binding, so the executor possesses it like the mudarib when he dies, and the property is chattel, the executor of the mudarib may sell it because it became binding after it became chattel.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the termination of agency in a pledge contract. The original ruling states that the agency to sell the pawn is not terminated by the pledgor's discharge of the agent, nor by the death of the pledgor or mortgagee, but only by the death of the agent. This is because agency is a contract based on personal trust; the principal consented to the agent's judgment, not to that of his heirs or executors. Therefore, upon the agent's death, his representative does not step into his shoes. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds a different view: if the agent dies, his executor (wasi) may sell the pawn. Abu Yusuf argues by analogy to the mudarib (silent partnership manager): when a mudarib dies, his executor may sell the partnership goods because the mudarib's authority is binding (lazim) after he has started the work. Similarly, the agent in a pledge has a binding authority, so his executor may complete the sale. The text then clarifies that the agent may sell the pawn even if the pledgor's heirs are absent, analogizing to an agent for litigation who may proceed even if the principal is absent, because delay would harm the mortgagee's right.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakīl): The person authorized to sell the pawn on behalf of the pledgor, whether he is the mortgagee, a neutral trustee, or another party.
العزل (al-‘azl): Discharge or removal of the agent by the pledgor before the sale.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The executor appointed by the agent in his will, who according to Abu Yusuf may sell the pawn after the agent's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to the agency to sell a pawn when the agent dies?
A) The agent's heir automatically takes over the agency.
B) The agent's executor may sell the pawn.
C) The agency ends and a new agent must be appointed.
D) The mortgagee becomes the new agent.

Key Takeaway
Agency in a pawn sale is personal to the agent; it ends only with his death, and his authority does not pass to his heirs or executors.

Pledgor and Mortgagee Cannot Sell Without Mutual Consent
Ruling on sale of pawn by either party without the other's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of a pawn without mutual consent and explain the effect of suspension (tawaqquf) and ratification (ijazah).

Core Idea
An unauthorized sale of a pawn is suspended; it becomes valid only if the other party ratifies it, and the price then substitutes the pawn as security.

In a pawn contract (rahn), both the pledgor and the mortgagee have rights over the pawned property. The pledgor owns it, but the mortgagee holds a lien. This lesson explains that neither party can sell the pawn without the other's consent, and the legal status of such a sale if it occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize who the pledgor (rahin) and mortgagee (murtahin) are and the rights each holds over the pawn.
The mortgagee has a right to retain the pawn until the debt is paid.
2
Understand the general prohibition
Learn that neither party may sell the pawn without the other's consent. The pledgor cannot sell because the mortgagee has a superior claim to the pawn's value; the mortgagee cannot sell because the pawn belongs to the pledgor.
3
Recognize the exception
If the pledgor had given prior authorization to the mortgagee to sell, the mortgagee may sell without further consent.
This exception is limited to the case where authorization is given in advance.
4
Learn the concept of tawaqquf
If a sale occurs without permission, the contract is suspended (tawaqquf). It is not automatically void; it awaits ratification by the party whose consent was lacking.
The term 'mawquf' is used for such suspended contracts.
5
Know the consequences of ratification
If the other party ratifies, the sale becomes valid retrospectively, and the sale price replaces the pawn as security for the debt. If not ratified, the sale is nullified and the pawn remains.
Ratification (ijazah) relates back to the time of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] (ولا يبيع الراهن أو المرتهن إلا برضا الآخر) : أي لا يكون للراهن بيع الرهن إلا برضاء المرتهن، وأيضا لا يكون للمرتهن بيع الرهن إلا برضاء الراهن، بأن وكله أو باعه، فأجاز الراهن بيعه. (٢) قوله : ولا يبيع ... الخ ; أي لا يجوز أن يبيع الراهن الرهن إلا برضاء المرتهن ; لأن المرتهن أحق بماليته من الراهن، ولا يقدر الراهن على تسليمه بالبيع، وأيضا لا يجوز للمرتهن أن يبيعه إلا برضاء الراهن ; لأنه ملكه وما رضي ببيعه، هذا إذا لم يكن سلطه الراهن. أما إذا باعه بغير إذن الراهن أو بالعكس توقف [على] إجازة صاحبه فإن أجازه جاز، ويكون الثمن رهنا وإن لم يجزه لا يجوز البيع، وله أن يبطله، ولعبده رهنا، هذا ملخص ما في «غاية البيان». (1) أي يوقف على إجازة الراهن فإن أجازه صح، ويكون الثمن رهنا وإن لم يجز لا يجوز البيع ، وله أن يبطله ويعيده رهنا. ينظر : (الفتاوى الكاملية»(ص٢٤٤).
[2] (And the pledgor or mortgagee does not sell except with the consent of the other): That is, the pledgor may not sell the pawn except with the consent of the mortgagee, and also the mortgagee may not sell it except with the consent of the pledgor, such as by appointing him or selling it and the pledgor approves his sale. (2) His statement: And does not sell... etc.; that is, it is not permissible for the pledgor to sell the pawn except with the consent of the mortgagee, because the mortgagee has a greater right to its value than the pledgor, and the pledgor cannot deliver it upon sale. And also it is not permissible for the mortgagee to sell it except with the consent of the pledgor, because it is his property and he has not consented to its sale. This is if the pledgor did not authorize him to sell it. But if he sells it without the permission of the pledgor or vice versa, it is suspended pending the approval of the other party; if he approves, it is valid and the price becomes a pawn; if he does not approve, the sale is not valid and he has the right to nullify it and return it as a pawn. This is the summary from Ghayat al-Bayan. (1) That is, it is suspended on the approval of the pledgor; if he approves, it is valid and the price becomes a pawn; if he does not approve, the sale is not valid and he has the right to nullify it and return it as a pawn. See al-Fatawa al-Kamiliyya, p. 244.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a plain ruling (ruling statement) from the matn, which the commentator expands. The core rule: neither the pledgor nor the mortgagee may unilaterally sell the pawn. The pledgor cannot sell because the mortgagee has a prior right to the pawn's value (due to his lien). The mortgagee cannot sell because the pawn is owned by the pledgor, who has not consented to its sale. However, if one party does sell without permission, the sale is suspended (موقوف) on the other party's ratification. If the other party ratifies, the sale becomes valid, and the sale price takes the place of the pawn (i.e., the mortgagee's security transfers to the price). If the other party does not ratify, the sale is void, and the property remains as pawn. The commentator clarifies that this applies when there is no prior authorization; if the pledgor had authorized the mortgagee to sell, then the mortgagee may sell without further consent.

Key Terms
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): The sale is suspended; it does not become effective until the other party gives approval.
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): The subsequent approval of the party whose consent was lacking, making the sale valid retroactively.
الثمن رهنا (al-thaman rahnan): The sale price takes the place of the pawn as security for the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is absolutely void from the start.
B) The sale is valid and the mortgagee's right is lost.
C) The sale is suspended pending the mortgagee's approval.
D) The sale is valid only if the price is paid directly to the mortgagee.

Key Takeaway
An unauthorized sale of a pawn is not automatically void; it is suspended and can become valid upon the other party's ratification, which transfers the security to the sale price.

Compelling an Agent to Sell a Pawn When the Pledgor is Absent
Ruling on compulsion of agent to sell when pledgor is absent and debt due

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on compelling an agent to sell a pawn when the pledgor is absent, and explain the qiyas (analogy) to an agent for litigation.

Core Idea
When a pledgor is absent, an agent for sale may be compelled to sell to protect the mortgagee's right, just as an agent for litigation is compelled to litigate.

This lesson examines a specific exception to the general Hanafi principle that an agent cannot be compelled to act. When a pawn's term expires and the pledgor is absent, the agent appointed to sell the pawn is compelled by the judge to sell. The justification is based on qiyas (analogy) to an agent for litigation, where compulsion is allowed to prevent loss of a right. We will explore the ruling, its proof, the method of compulsion, and the scholarly differences within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its exception
State the general principle that an agent is not compelled to act, then note the exception in the matn: when the term expires and the pledgor is absent, the agent is compelled to sell.
2
Trace the qiyas to the litigation agent
The proof is an analogy (qiyas) to an agent for litigation: if the principal is absent and the agent refuses, the judge compels him to litigate to prevent loss of the right.
The illa is the attachment of a right and potential harm.
3
Examine the mechanism of compulsion
The judge imprisons the agent for a period; if he still refuses, the judge sells the pawn on his behalf.
This is the view of the Sahibayn; according to some transmissions of Abu Hanifa, the judge sells directly.
4
Consider the scenario of post-pledge agency
The ruling applies even if the agency was stipulated after the pledge contract, because the mortgagee's right is attached.
5
Apply the ruling to furu‘
In practice, this ruling protects the mortgagee's security interest when the pledgor is unavailable.

If its term expires and his pledgor is absent, the agent is compelled to sell it, like an agent for litigation when his principal is absent and he refused it, and likewise he is compelled if the condition was stipulated after the pledge according to the more correct view. [2] (If its term expires and his pledgor is absent, the agent is compelled to sell it, like an agent for litigation when his principal is absent and he refused it, for the agent is compelled to litigate. The result is that the agent is not compelled to act, except in this case when the pledgor is absent and the agent refuses to sell, for the mortgagee is harmed, so the agent is compelled to sell, just as he is compelled to litigate when the principal is absent, for the principal relied on him and then was absent, so if he does not litigate, the principal is harmed and his right is lost, so the agent is compelled to litigate. [3] (And likewise he is compelled if the condition was stipulated after the pledge according to the more correct view. Know that there are two opinions on compulsion: [1] His statement: The agent is compelled; the manner of compulsion is that the judge imprisons him for days so that he sells, then if he does not sell after imprisonment for days, the judge sells on his behalf. This is apparent according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to the principle of Abu Hanifa it is also like that according to some, because a means for repaying the debt is determined here. And it is said: He does not sell; the property of the debtor is with him for repaying the debt, and the sale is not invalidated by this compulsion, because it is compulsion for a right, so it becomes like choice. Similarly in al-Kifayah. [2] His statement: Like an agent... etc. Its example: There are two men between whom there is a dispute, so the defendant appoints a man for litigation at the request of the plaintiff; then the principal is absent and the agent refuses to litigate against him; then he is compelled, because it involves loss of the right. Unlike an agent for sale, for he is not compelled if he refrains from selling, because the agent like a sale? if he refrains from selling, the principal is not harmed by it, because he can sell by himself. [3] His statement: So the agent is compelled... etc.; that is, even if the agency was stipulated after the contract of pledge, because the evidence is current, which is the attachment of the mortgagee's right, and the loss of his right if he is not compelled. And his statement: Because the absence of evidence, which is that the agency within the contract of pledge takes the place of one of its descriptions and rights. And his statement: Upon the absence of the indicated meaning, which is compulsion. And his statement: When another evidence is found, meaning the attachment of the mortgagee's right and its loss if he is not compelled. This is what was heard from the teacher. Let it be reflected upon. Al-Jalabi said it.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, expanded by the commentator. The matn states that if the term for repayment expires and the pledgor is absent, the agent (who was appointed to sell the pawn) is compelled to sell it, even though generally an agent is not compelled to act. The proof is analogical (qiyas khafi/istihsan) to the case of an agent appointed for litigation: if the principal is absent and the agent refuses to litigate, the judge compels him to litigate because the principal's right would otherwise be lost. Similarly, here the mortgagee's security right would be lost if the agent refuses to sell. The compulsion is executed by the judge imprisoning the agent until he sells, and if he still refuses, the judge sells the pawn on his behalf. This applies even if the agency was stipulated after the pledge contract was made, because the mortgagee's right is attached to the pawn. The commentator notes a difference of opinion: according to Abu Hanifa, some say he does not sell directly, but the judge sells; others say he does. The relied-upon view is that the judge compels and sells if necessary.

Key Terms
الإجبار (al-ijbār): Compulsion by the judge, through imprisonment or direct sale, forcing the agent to perform the sale.
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment by the judge as a means to compel the agent to sell or litigate.
جهة قضاء الدين (jihah qaḍā’ al-dayn): The means or channel for repaying the debt; here, the sale of the pawn is the only feasible way.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The mortgagee is allowed to sell the pawn directly.
D) The agent is fined for refusing.

Key Takeaway
An agent for sale of a pawn is compelled to sell when the pledgor is absent, analogous to an agent for litigation, to protect the mortgagee's right.

8 min
Compelling the Agent to Sell the Pawn when the Pawner is Absent
Ruling on compulsion of agent to sell pawn when pawner absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an agent appointed to sell a pawn can be compelled to sell when the pawner is absent, and explain the qiyas analogy with the agent in litigation, including the illa and the manner of compulsion.

Core Idea
An agent for sale of a pawn is compelled to sell when the pawner is absent, by analogy to litigation agent, to prevent loss to the mortgagee.

The Hanafi school generally does not compel an agent to act. However, this passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah establishes an exception: when the pawner is absent and the appointed agent refuses to sell, the judge may compel the agent to sell to protect the mortgagee's right. The ruling is derived by qiyas (analogy) on the case of an agent in litigation. We will analyze the analogy, the manner of compulsion, and the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the timing of the agency appointment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Parties
The matn states: 'If its term expires and his pawner is absent, the agent is compelled to its sale.' Identify the three parties: pawner (rahin), agent (wakil), and mortgagee (murtahin). The ruling is that the agent is compelled to sell the pawn.
The agent is appointed by the pawner specifically to sell the pawn upon maturity.
2
Trace the Qiyas (Analogy)
The text compares this to the case of an agent in litigation whose principal is absent and refuses to litigate. Identify the asl (original case) and far' (new case). The illa (effective cause) is the potential loss of a right (haqq) if the agent does not act.
Asl: agent in litigation is compelled to litigate when principal is absent to prevent loss of principal's right. Far': agent for sale of pawn is compelled to sell when pawner is absent to prevent loss of mortgagee's right.
3
Analyze the Manner of Compulsion
The commentary explains that the judge first imprisons the agent for a period. If the agent still refuses, the judge sells the pawn on his behalf. Note the intra-Hanafi difference: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the judge sells directly; according to one narration from Abu Hanifa, the judge only imprisons and does not sell.
The reliance is on the two Sahibayn and the later authorities.
4
Examine the Dispute about Timing of Agency
There is a dispute (qawlani) whether compulsion applies if the agency was stipulated after the pawn contract (not within the original rahn). The more correct view (al-ashah) holds that compulsion still applies because the mortgagee's right is attached regardless of when the agency was appointed.
The opposing view argues lack of specific evidence; the response is that another evidence (the mortgagee's right) suffices.
5
Understand the Refutation of the Opposing View
The commentary refutes the claim that absence of evidence for compulsion when agency is after pawn proves absence of the ruling. It argues that the attachment of the mortgagee's right is an independent evidence for compulsion.
This is a typical usul argument: 'adam al-dalil la yadullu 'ala 'adam al-madlul' (absence of evidence does not prove absence of the ruling).

As for according to Abu Hanifa, it is similarly according to some, because the avenue for debt repayment is determined here. And it is said: He does not sell; the debtor's property is with him for debt repayment, and the sale is not invalidated by this compulsion, because it is compulsion that becomes like choice. Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling on compelling an agent to sell the pawned item when the pawner is absent and the mortgagee would suffer loss. The matn states: 'If its term expires and his pawner is absent, the agent is compelled to its sale, like the agent in litigation when his principal is absent and he refuses. And likewise he is compelled if the agency was stipulated after the pawn in the most correct view.' The commentary explains the reasoning by analogy: the agent in litigation is compelled to represent the principal because otherwise the principal's right would be lost. Similarly, when the pawner is absent and the agent refuses to sell, the mortgagee's right to recover the debt would be lost, so the agent is compelled to sell. The commentary then discusses the manner of compulsion: the judge imprisons the agent for days; if he still does not sell, the judge sells on his behalf. There is a difference of opinion (qawlani) regarding compulsion when the agency was stipulated after the pawn contract. The more correct view is that compulsion is still applied because the mortgagee's right is attached, even if the agency was not part of the original pawn contract. The commentary refutes the opposing view by stating that absence of evidence for compulsion does not mean absence of the ruling when another evidence (the mortgagee's right) exists. The passage emphasizes that the agent is not generally compelled to act, except in these specific scenarios where harm would otherwise occur.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): An agent appointed by the pawner (rahin) to sell the pawn upon maturity.
الجبر (al-jabr): Legal compulsion by the judge to force the agent to perform the act (sale or litigation).
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge; a contract where property is given as security for a debt.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The creditor/pawn-holder who holds the pawn as security.
الإجبار (al-ijbar): The act of compelling someone (the agent) by judicial authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the manner of compelling an agent to sell a pawn if he refuses?
A) The judge directly sells the pawn without any prior warning.
B) The judge imprisons the agent for a period; if he still refuses, the judge sells the pawn.
C) The mortgagee is allowed to sell the pawn directly.
D) The agent is only compelled to pay compensation, not to sell.

Key Takeaway
An agent for the sale of a pawn is compelled to sell when the pawner is absent to protect the mortgagee's right, by analogy with the agent in litigation, with the judge using imprisonment and eventual direct sale as enforcement.

The Price Becomes a Pawn: Liability After Trustee Sale
Ruling on the price becoming a pawn after trustee's sale and liability in case of claim of ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the price from the trustee's sale becomes a new pawn and apply the liability options for the claimant.

Core Idea
The price from a trustee's sale substitutes the pawn; its destruction mirrors the pawn's.

When a trustee sells a pawn, the price takes its place as a new pawn. If a third party proves ownership and the pawn is destroyed, the claimant has three liability options: hold the pawner, trustee, or mortgagee. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its qiyas proof.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and ruling
Identify that the trustee ('adl) sells the pawn, and the price becomes a pawn (thaman rahn). The ruling is that the price inherits the pawn's rulings.
The text says 'falthaman rahn' – the price is a pawn.
2
Identify the qiyas proof
The proof is qiyas: the price stands in place of the original pawn (qa'im maqam ma kana maqbudan). The original pawn was held as security; so too the price.
This is a qiyas jaliy: the illah is substitution in possession.
3
Analyze the liability options
If the buyer turns out to be the true owner (mustahiqq) and the pawn is destroyed, the claimant can hold the pawner (rahin), trustee ('adl), or mortgagee (murtahin) liable. Each option has different effects on the sale and recourse.
The text lists three options with consequences.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on compulsion
There is a dispute about whether the trustee can be compelled to sell. The preferred view (asahh) is that compulsion is established because the mortgagee's right would be lost otherwise.
This is tangential but shows the depth of Hanafi discussion.
