والآخر: إنّ الجبر بناء على أنَّ حقَّ المرتهن يضيع، فيجبر كالوكيل بالخصومة إذا غاب الموكل، وإنما كان هذا القولُ أصح؛ لأن عدم الدليل لا يدل على عدم المدلول، خصوصا إذا وجد دليل آخر. (فإن باعَهُ العدلُ فالثمن رهن، فهلكه كهلكه، فإن أوفى ثمنه المرتهن فاستحق): أي الرهن، ففي الهالك): أي إذا هلك الرهن في يد المشتري، (ضمنَ المستحق الراهن قيمته وصح البيع والقبض، أو العدل ثم هو الراهن وصحا، أو المرتهن ثمنه وهو له ورجع المرتهن على راهنِهِ بدينه): أي المستحق إما أن يضمن الراهن قيمة الرهن؛ لأنه غاصب، وحينئذ صح البيع وقبض الثمن؛ لأنَّ الراهن ملكه بأداء الضمان، وإما أن يضمن العدل القيمة؛ لأنه متعد بالبيع والتسليم [1] قوله: فالثمن رهن... الخ؛ أي كان الثمن رهناً؛ لقيامه مقام ما كان مقبوضاً، وإذا تَوى الثمن عند العدل كان مال المرتهن؛ لبقاء عقد الرهن في الثمن؛ لقيامه مقام الرهن المبيع، فيكون هلاك الثمن كهلاك المرهون. وكذلك إذا قتل العبد المرهون، وغرمَ القاتل قيمته؛ لأنَّ المالك يستحق هذا الضمان من حيث المالية، وإن كان بدل الدم فأخذ هذا الضمان، حكم ضمان المال في حق المستحق، فبقي عقد الرهن، وكذلك لو قتله عبد فدفع به؛ لأنه قائم مقام الأول لحماً ودماً، هذا ما يستفاد من «الهداية»).
[٢] قوله: أن يضمن... الخ؛ أي إن شاء المستحق يضمن الراهن القيمة؛ لأنه وكيل من جهته، وعامل له، فيرجعُ عليه بما لحقه من العهدة، وصح البيع وقبض الثمن، فلا يرجع المرتهن عليه بشيء من دينه.
If the trustee sells it, the price is a pawn; its destruction is like its destruction. If the mortgagee pays its price and then [the pawn] is claimed [by a rightful owner], then in the case of destruction, the claimant may hold the pawner liable for its value, and the sale and delivery are valid; or [he may hold] the trustee, then it is the pawner, and both are valid; or the mortgagee [holds] its price and it is his, and the mortgagee has recourse against his pawner for his debt. One [saying]: Compulsion is only established if the agency is binding, i.e., it was in the pawn contract; if it was after, he is not compelled. And the other: Compulsion is based on the fact that the mortgagee's right is wasted, so he is compelled like the agent in litigation when the principal is absent. And this saying is more correct because the absence of evidence does not prove the absence of the result, especially when another evidence exists. (If the trustee sells it, the price is a pawn; its destruction is like its destruction. If the mortgagee pays its price and then [the pawn] is claimed): i.e., the pawn, so in the case of destruction (i.e., if the pawn is destroyed in the buyer's hand), (the claimant may hold the pawner liable for its value, and the sale and delivery are valid; or the trustee [liable], then it is the pawner, and both are valid; or the mortgagee [holds] its price and it is his, and the mortgagee has recourse against his pawner for his debt): i.e., the claimant either may hold the pawner liable for the value of the pawn because he is an usurper; then the sale and receipt of price are valid because the pawner owns it by paying the compensation; or may hold the trustee liable for the value because he transgressed by selling and delivering. [1] His saying: the price is a pawn... etc.: i.e., the price became a pawn because it stands in place of what was held. If the price perishes with the trustee, it is the mortgagee's property because the pawn contract remains in the price, as it stands in place of the sold pawn. Thus the destruction of the price is like the destruction of the pawned item. Similarly, if the pawned slave is killed and the killer pays his value, because the owner deserves this compensation in terms of property, even if it is blood money; taking this compensation, the ruling of property compensation applies in the right of the claimant, so the pawn contract remains. Similarly, if a slave kills him and is handed over [as compensation], because he stands in place of the first in flesh and blood. This is what is derived from al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: that he may hold liable... etc.: i.e., if the claimant wishes, he may hold the pawner liable for the value because he is an agent on his behalf and his worker, so he has recourse against him for what he incurred of liability, and the sale and receipt of price are valid, and the mortgagee has no recourse against him for anything of his debt.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage from the sharh discusses the ruling on the price after the trustee ('adl) sells the pawn. The matn states: 'If the trustee sells it, the price is a pawn; its destruction is like its destruction. If the mortgagee pays its price and then [the pawn] is claimed, then in case of destruction, the claimant may hold the pawner liable for its value, and the sale and delivery are valid; or [he may hold] the trustee, then it is the pawner, and both are valid; or the mortgagee [holds] its price and it is his, and the mortgagee has recourse against his pawner for his debt.' The commentary explains that when the trustee sells the pawn, the price takes the place of the pawn and itself becomes a new pawn in the hands of the trustee. If the buyer is later revealed to be the true owner (mustahiqq) and the pawn has been destroyed (e.g., in the buyer's hand), the claimant has three options: (1) He can hold the pawner (rahin) liable for the value, because the pawner is considered an usurper (ghasib); in this case the sale and receipt of price are valid, and the pawner becomes owner of the price. (2) He can hold the trustee ('adl) liable because he transgressed by selling and delivering; in this case the price belongs to the pawner. (3) He can hold the mortgagee (murtahin) liable for the price, and the price becomes his, and the mortgagee then has recourse against the pawner for his debt. The commentary also draws an analogy: if a pawned slave is killed, the killer's compensation stands in place of the slave and the pawn contract continues in that compensation. This is derived from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
العدل (al-'adl): A trusted third party appointed to sell the pawn and handle the transaction.
الثمن (al-thaman): The price received from the sale of the pawn.
استحق (istihqaq): A claim of ownership by a third party proving that the seller did not own the item.
ضمن (daman): Liability; the obligation to pay compensation for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The price becomes a new pawn in the hands of the trustee, with the same rulings as the original pawn.
C) The price belongs to the pawner unconditionally.
D) The price is forfeited to the state.

Key Takeaway
When a trustee sells a pawn, the price becomes a new pawn with the same rulings; the claimant has a choice of whom to hold liable.

Trustee’s Sale of Pledge and Liability Scenarios
Detailed ruling on sale of pledge by trustee and liability scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal consequences of a trustee's sale of a pledge and determine liability for loss or reclamation under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A trustee’s sale transforms the price into a pledge; liability for loss shifts among pledgor, trustee, and mortgagee based on who is held accountable.

This passage from Al-Hidayah details the intricate rulings when a trustee (عدل) sells a pledged item. The sale generates a new pledge (the price), and if the item is later reclaimed by its true owner, the law provides clear options for liability. Understanding these scenarios is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
State what happens to the price after the trustee sells the pledge: it becomes a pledge in place of the original item.
The price is treated like the original pledge; its destruction is like the destruction of the original.
2
Analyze the destruction scenario
If the mortgagee pays the price and the pledged item is destroyed, the rightful owner has three options for claiming compensation: from the pledgor, the trustee, or the mortgagee.
Each option leads to a different assignment of liability and validity of the sale.
3
Examine the extant scenario
If the pledged item is still in existence, the rightful owner reclaims it from the buyer. The buyer then recovers the price from the trustee, and the trustee recovers from the pledgor.
The sale and receipt of price are valid, and the trustee can still choose to claim from the mortgagee instead.
4
Understand the trustee's option
The trustee has a choice: either to claim the value from the pledgor (validating the sale) or to claim the price from the mortgagee (since the mortgagee paid it).
This option protects the trustee's interests and clarifies the final liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن باعه العدل فالثمن رهن، فهلكه كهلكه، فإن أوفى ثمنه المرتهن فاستحق، ففي الهالك ضمن المستحق الراهن قيمته وصح البيع والقبض، أو العدل ثم هو الراهن وصحا، أو المرتهن ثمنه وهو له ورجع المرتهن على راهنه بدينه. وفي القائم أخذه من مشتريه ورجع هو على العدل بثمنه، ثم هو على الراهن به، وصح القبض ثم هو على الراهن بدينه وحينئذ العدل بالخيار إما أن يضمن الراهن القيمة، وحينئذ صح البيع وقبض الثمن، وإما أن يضمن المرتهن الثمن الذي أداه إليه، وهو له؛ أي ذلك الثمن يكون للعدل، فيرجع المرتهن على راهنه بدينه.
If the trustee sells it, the price is a pledge, its destruction is like its destruction. If the mortgagee pays its price and then it is reclaimed, then regarding the destroyed: the rightful owner guarantees the pledgor its value and the sale and receipt are valid, or (if he guarantees) the trustee, then it is the pledgor and both are valid, or (if he guarantees) the mortgagee its price and it is for him and the mortgagee returns to his pledgor for his debt. And in the extant (case), he takes it from his buyer and he (the buyer) returns to the trustee for its price, then it is upon the pledgor, and the receipt is valid, then it is upon the pledgor for his debt, and then the trustee has the option: either to guarantee the pledgor the value, and then the sale and receipt of the price are valid, or to guarantee the mortgagee the price which he paid to him, and it is for him; i.e., that price belongs to the trustee, so the mortgagee returns to his pledgor for his debt.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the consequences when a trustee (عدل) sells a pledged item (رهن). The price received becomes a pledge in place of the original item, and its destruction is treated like the destruction of the original pledge. If the mortgagee (مرتهن) pays the price to the trustee and the pledged item is later reclaimed by its rightful owner (مستحق), several scenarios arise for who is liable and how the sale stands. The rightful owner has three options: (1) hold the pledgor (راهن) liable for the value of the destroyed item, making the sale and receipt of price valid because the pledgor becomes owner by paying compensation; (2) hold the trustee liable, then the trustee becomes owner of the price; (3) hold the mortgagee liable for the price he paid, meaning the price belongs to the trustee, and the mortgagee must seek his debt from the pledgor. If the pledged item is still in existence (قائم) in the buyer's hands, the rightful owner reclaims it from the buyer. The buyer then recovers the price from the trustee, who in turn recovers it from the pledgor. The trustee has a choice: either claim the price from the pledgor (making the mortgagee's receipt valid), or claim it from the mortgagee (since it becomes clear the price was not rightfully his). The passage is entirely drawn from Hanafi fiqh, specifically from Al-Hidayah, and presents the relied-upon positions without intra-school disputes.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): The trustworthy custodian appointed by both parties to hold the pledged property
مستحق (mustahaqq): The true owner who reclaims the pledged property after it was mistakenly pledged
ضمن (daman): Liability or guarantee for loss or value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Hold the trustee liable for the value
C) Hold the mortgagee liable for the price paid
D) Hold the buyer liable for the price

Key Takeaway
When a trustee sells a pledge, the sale is valid under Hanafi law, and liability for loss or reclamation is distributed among the pledgor, trustee, and mortgagee based on the rightful owner's choice.

Trustee’s Recourse When Agency Is Not Stipulated in Pledge
Ruling on trustee's recourse only against pledgor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the trustee's recourse when agency is not stipulated in the pledge contract, and explain the legal analogy (qiyas) and its basis.

Core Idea
Without stipulated agency, the trustee’s recourse is only against the pledgor, just like an independent agent.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses a practical question: when the trustee (al-‘adl) sells the pledged property and faces a warranty claim (daman), whom can he sue? The answer depends on whether the agency (tawkil) was a condition of the pledge contract. Here we focus on the case where it was not stipulated, and the analogy that determines the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the trustee’s sale of the pledge followed by a warranty claim. The ruling: the trustee can only sue the pledgor (al-rahin), not the mortgagee (al-murtahin), regardless of whether the mortgagee received the price.
This contrasts with the case where agency is stipulated, where the trustee may choose.
2
Locate the operative distinction
The key is whether the agency was a condition in the pledge contract. If not, the agency is considered separate from the pledge, like an independent agency (al-wakalah al-mufrada).
The mortgagee’s right does not attach to this separate agency.
3
Trace the qiyas to independent agency
The ruling is derived by analogy: just as an independent agent who sells by the principal’s order and delivers the price to a third party, then suffers a warranty claim, can only sue the principal (muwakkil), not the third party (qabid) — here the trustee is like the agent, the pledgor is the principal, and the mortgagee is the third party.
The illa (effective cause) is that the mortgagee’s right is not attached to this agency.
4
Identify the illa and apply it
The illa: 'The mortgagee's right is not attached to this agency' (لم يتعلق بهذا التوكيل حق المرتهن). Hence, the trustee has no recourse against the mortgagee.
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy) because the shared meaning is evident.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on compelling the trustee to sell
The passage notes that according to Sarakhsi, the trustee cannot be compelled to sell when agency is not stipulated (this is zahir al-riwaya). But Fakhr al-Islam and Shaykh al-Islam consider the opposite view more correct. This shows that even within the school, there can be methodological differences.
The practical consequence: if the trustee refuses to sell, can the court compel him? The answer depends on which view is adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وقد سلّم له ذلك جاز أن يلزمه بالضمان ، هكذا يفهم من «الهداية» وشروحه. [٢] قوله : إنما يكون ؛ خبر قوله : أي ما ذكر، والمطلب ظاهر، وهذا في التفريق بين الوكالة المشروطة في عقد الرهن والوكالة الغير المشروطة في الرهن الذي ذكره المصنف و الشارح تبعا للكرخي ، حيث ذكره في «مختصره».

وهذا يؤيد قول من لا يرى جبر هذا الوكيل على البيع، وقال شمس الأئمة السِّرَخْسِي : هو ظاهر الرواية ؛ لأنَّ رضاء المرتهن بالرهن بدون التوكيل قد تم ، فصار التوكيل مستأنفاً في ضمن عقد الرهن ، فكان منفصلاً عنه ضرورة، على أن فخر الإسلام وشيخ الإسلام الله قالا قول من يرى جبر هذا الوكيل أصح ؛ لإطلاق
And if agency is not stipulated in the pledge, the trustee has recourse only against the pledgor, whether the mortgagee received its price or not. That is: what was mentioned of the trustee's option between holding the pledgor or the mortgagee liable only applies when the agency was stipulated in the contract of pledge, because then the mortgagee's right is attached to the agency, so the trustee may hold the mortgagee liable because he sold for his right. As for when it [agency] is not stipulated in the pledge, it is like an independent agency: when the agent sells and pays the price to another by the principal's order, then a warranty befalls him, he does not have recourse against the recipient. So here he only has recourse against the pledgor, whether the mortgagee received the price or not. The case of not receiving: that the trustee sold the pledge by the pledgor's order, and the price was lost in the trustee's hand without his negligence, then the pledged property was claimed, then the warranty that befalls the trustee, he has recourse with it against the pledgor. [1] His saying 'And if not stipulated': i.e., if the agency after the pledge contract is not stipulated in the contract regarding what befalls the trustee of warranty, the trustee has recourse with it only against the pledgor, whether the mortgagee received the price or not; because the mortgagee's right is not attached to this agency, so there is no recourse as in the independent agency from the pledge: when the agent sells and pays the price to whom the principal ordered, then a warranty befalls him, he does not have recourse with it against the recipient. Contrary to the agency stipulated in the contract, because the mortgagee's right is attached to it, so the sale is for his right, and he has consented to that, so it is permissible to hold him liable for the warranty. This is understood from al-Hidayah and its commentaries. [2] His saying 'only applies': is the predicate of his saying 'what was mentioned', and the point is clear; and this is in distinguishing between the agency stipulated in the pledge contract and the agency not stipulated in the pledge, which the author and the commentator mention following al-Karkhi, as he mentioned it in his Mukhtasar. And this supports the opinion of those who do not see compelling this trustee to sell. And Shams al-A’imma al-Sarakhsi said: It is the apparent (zahir) of the transmission (riwaya), because the mortgagee's consent to the pledge without the agency has been completed, so the agency becomes initiated within the pledge contract, and it is necessarily separate from it. Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya (with notes from ‘Umdat al-Ri’aya) presents a ruling on the trustee’s right of recourse when he sells the pledged property. The matn states that if the agency (tawkil) was not stipulated in the contract of pledge, the trustee (al-‘adl) can only sue the pledgor (al-rahin), regardless of whether the mortgagee (al-murtahin) has received the price. The sharh clarifies that the thamarah (practical consequence) of this ruling is that the trustee’s option to hold either party liable—discussed in a previous ruling—only applies when the agency was a condition of the pledge. Without such a condition, the agency is treated as an independent (mufrada) agency, where the agent’s recourse after a warranty claim is only against his principal, not the third party who received the price. The text then provides the scenario where the price is lost in the trustee’s hands without his negligence, and the pledged property is later claimed (istihqaq) by a third party. In that case, the trustee can only claim compensation from the pledgor. The marginal notes [1] and [2] further elaborate: note [1] compares the situation to independent agency and refers to al-Hidayah; note [2] explains that this distinction originates from al-Karkhi’s Mukhtasar. The passage then records a dispute among Hanafi scholars: Sarakhsi considers the view that the trustee cannot be compelled to sell (jabr) as the apparent (zahir al-riwaya) ruling, while Fakhr al-Islam and Shaykh al-Islam prefer the view that he can be compelled. The passage ends with the word 'لإطلاق' (for the absolute nature), presumably the beginning of an argument, but the text cuts off here. This demonstrates the Hanafi method of comparing cases through qiyas (analogy) and recognizing ikhtilaf within the school.

Key Terms
العدل (al-‘adl): The trustee or middleman who holds and sells the pledged property.
التوكيل (al-tawkil): Agency; authorization given to the trustee to sell the pledge in case of default.
الرهن (al-rahn): Pledge; a contract where property is given as collateral for a debt.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The apparent transmission; a ruling from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (Zahir al-Riwayah).
الوكالة المفردة (al-wakalah al-mufrada): Independent agency; an agency not connected to another contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the trustee have the option to hold either the pledgor or the mortgagee liable for a warranty claim?
A) When the agency is stipulated in the pledge contract
B) When the agency is not stipulated in the pledge contract
C) When the trustee sells the pledge without the pledgor's order
D) When the mortgagee has not received the price

Key Takeaway
Without stipulated agency, the trustee's recourse is solely against the pledgor, by analogy to an independent agency, because the mortgagee's right is not attached.

Claimant's Option When Pledge Perishes: The Qiyas with Usurpers
Ruling on claimant's option to hold pledgor or mortgagee liable based on qiyas to usurpers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the claimant's two options when a pledge perishes in the mortgagee's hands and a third party claims it, and explain the Hanafi qiyas analogy from the law of usurpers.

Core Idea
Claimant may sue either pledgor or mortgagee – each is a transgressor like a usurper.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi scenario from the law of pledge (rahn): the pledged property is destroyed while in the mortgagee's custody, and then a third party appears claiming full ownership. The text grants the claimant a valuable option – to hold either the pledgor or the mortgagee liable for compensation. We will trace the legal analogy (qiyas) that the Hanafi jurists used, comparing each party to a usurper (ghasib) and the usurper's usurper, and see how the underlying principle of transgression (ta'addi) unifies these cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Ruling
Read the passage carefully. The pledge perishes while in the mortgagee's possession, then a claimant appears. The matn states two possibilities: if the claimant holds the pledgor liable, the debt is satisfied; if he holds the mortgagee liable, the mortgagee can recover from the pledgor. Understand that the claimant has the option (khiyar) to choose whom to sue.
The key is 'al-mustahiqq bi'l-khiyar' – the claimant has the option.
2
Recognize the Analogy to Usurpers
The sharh (commentary) explicitly draws an analogy: the pledgor is like a usurper (ghasib) and the mortgagee like the usurper's usurper (ghasib al-ghasib). In the law of usurpation, the true owner can hold either the first usurper or the second responsible. Confirm that the same logic applies here.
Analogy is made explicit in al-Hidayah.
3
Identify the Illa (Effective Cause)
Both parties have transgressed (ta'addi) against the claimant's right. The pledgor transgressed by delivering property that was not his; the mortgagee transgressed by taking possession of it. This shared illa – being a muta'addi – is what allows the analogy and gives the claimant the option.
Illā: each is a transgressor regarding the owner's right.
4
Apply the Qiyas Panel
Using the qiyas panel from the analytical toolbox: note the asl (usurper case), the far' (pledge case), the illa (transgression), and the derived ruling (claimant's option). See how the Hanafi principle of liability for transference is uniform.
The type is jaliyy (clear) qiyas.
5
Understand the Consequences of Each Choice
If the claimant holds the pledgor liable, the pledgor pays compensation and the debt is considered satisfied (halaka bi daynihi). If he holds the mortgagee liable, the mortgagee recovers the value from the pledgor (as maghrur – deceived person) and also recovers his debt because his possession is nullified. Trace this through the commentary notes.
The principle of 'al-maghrur yarji' ala al-gharr' applies.

Source Text

فإن هلك الرهن مع المرتهن فاستحق وضَمِّنَ الراهن قيمته هلك بدينه، وإن ضَمِّنَ المرتهن رجع على الراهن بقيمته وبدينه
( فإن هلك الرهن مع المرتهن فاستحق وضمن الراهن قيمته هلك بدينه) : أي يكون مستوفياً دينه ، وإن ضَمَّنَ المرتهن ) رجع على الراهن بقيمته وبدينه) : أي المستحق بالخيار بين تضمين الراهن أو المرتهن، فإن ضَمَّنَ الراهن ملكه بأداء الضمان ، فصح الرهن ، وإن ضَمَّنَ المرتهن يرجع على الراهن بالقيمة ؛ لأنـه مغرور من جهة الراهن وبالدين ؛ لأنه انتقض قبضه فيعود حقه كما كان محمد الله في «الجامع الصغير» و «الأصل» ما بيناه ، فتكون الوكالة غير المشروطة في العقدِ كالمشروطة فيه في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام هنـ هناك.

(۱) «الهداية» (٤) : ١٤٣).
If the pledge perishes with the mortgagee, then it is claimed, and he [the claimant] holds the pledgor liable for its value, it is lost against his debt; and if he holds the mortgagee liable, he [the mortgagee] has recourse against the pledgor for its value and for his debt. Commentary: i.e., he becomes a satisfier of his debt. And if he holds the mortgagee liable, he has recourse against the pledgor for its value and for his debt. That is, the claimant has the option between holding the pledgor or the mortgagee liable. If he holds the pledgor liable, he [the pledgor] owns it by paying the compensation, so the pledge is valid. If he holds the mortgagee liable, he [the mortgagee] has recourse against the pledgor for the value because he is deceived (maghrur) by the pledgor, and for the debt because his possession is nullified, so his right returns as it was. As Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Asl. So the agency not stipulated in the contract becomes like that stipulated in all the rulings we mentioned there. [4] [1] His saying 'If it perishes...' etc.: Explanation: If the pledged thing perishes with the mortgagee, i.e., in his possession, then it is claimed, i.e., then a man claims it, he has the option: if he wishes, he holds the pledgor liable, and if he wishes, he holds the mortgagee liable, because each of them is a transgressor (muta'addi) regarding his right. As for the pledgor, by delivering the pledge to the mortgagee; as for the mortgagee, by taking possession. So the pledgor becomes like a usurper (ghasib), and the mortgagee like the usurper's usurper (ghasib al-ghasib). So the claimant may hold liable whichever he wishes. The commentator indicated this by his saying 'the following', i.e., the claimant has the option... etc. This is in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying 'and if he holds liable' etc.: i.e., if the claimant holds the pledgor liable, he owns the pledge between the mortgagee, i.e., he becomes a satisfier of his debt, because the pledgor owned the pledged property by paying the compensation, so the satisfaction is valid. [3] His saying 'and if he holds the mortgagee liable' etc.: for its value or for his debt. As for the value, because he is deceived by the pledgor, and the deceived has recourse against the deceiver for the compensation that befell him, just as the lessee has recourse against the lessor, and the depositee against the depositor. As for the debt, because the mortgagee's possession is nullified, so his right returns as it was. [4] His saying 'because he is deceived': i.e., the pledgor misled him regarding the pledge of another's property and it is as if he is the one who caused the compensation upon him.

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where the pledged property is destroyed while in the mortgagee's possession, and later a third party appears claiming rightful ownership (istihqaq). The matn gives the ruling: if the claimant chooses to hold the pledgor liable, the pledgor pays the value and the debt is considered satisfied (halaka bi daynihi). If the claimant holds the mortgagee liable, the mortgagee can recover both the value and the debt from the pledgor. The sharh explains the reasoning: the claimant has the option because both parties have transgressed (ta'addi)—the pledgor by handing over property that wasn't his, and the mortgagee by taking possession. The analogy is drawn to the relation of a usurper (ghasib) and the usurper's usurper. The marginal notes elaborate: note [1] clarifies the analogy from al-Hidayah; note [2] explains that if the pledgor pays, the pledge is valid and the debt is satisfied because the pledgor owns the property by paying the compensation; note [3] explains that the mortgagee's recourse against the pledgor for the value is based on the principle of the deceived (maghrur) recovering from the deceiver (gharr), using examples from lease and deposit; recourse for the debt is because the mortgagee's possession is revoked, reverting his right to the debt. Note [4] further explains the deception. The passage also includes a cross-reference stating that the agency not stipulated is like the stipulated one in all these rulings—this seems to connect back to the earlier discussion.

Key Terms
المستحق (al-mustahiqq): The claimant; the rightful owner who appears after the pledge is lost and claims it.
الغاصب (al-ghasib): Usurper; one who wrongfully takes possession of another's property.
المغرور (al-maghrur): The deceived person; one who suffers loss due to another's misrepresentation.
الغار (al-gharr): The deceiver; one who causes another to act to their detriment.

Key Takeaway
The claimant whose pledge perishes while in the mortgagee's custody may hold either the pledgor or the mortgagee liable because both have committed an act of transgression.

Ruling on Destruction of Claimed Pledged Property: The Pledgee's Recourse
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pledgee's recourse when claimed property destroyed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the destruction of claimed pledged property and explain why the pledgee can recover both the value and the debt from the pledgor despite an objection.

Core Idea
The pledgee can recover both value and debt from the pledgor because deception arises from delivery, not from ultimate liability.

This passage examines a nuanced scenario in the law of pawn (rahn): when a pawned item is destroyed in the pledgee's hands and then claimed by its true owner. The Hanafi school, based on Imam Muhammad's works, holds that the pledgee can recover both the value and the original debt from the pledgor. An objection by Abu Hazim al-Qadi argues that ultimate liability transfers ownership back to the pledgor, but the established view refutes this on the basis of the timing of ownership transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the claimant's options
Recognize that the claimant (true owner) can choose to sue either the pledgor or the pledgee for the value of the destroyed item.
This choice arises because both parties have transgressed: the pledgor by delivering another's property, the pledgee by taking possession.
2
State the ruling when the pledgee is sued
The pledgee can recover from the pledgor both the value (due to deception) and the debt (because his possession is retroactively invalidated).
This is the position of Imam Muhammad, recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Asl.
3
Analyze Abu Hazim's objection
The objection argues that because the ultimate liability for the value falls on the pledgor, ownership of the item transfers to him, making it as if he pawned his own property. Therefore, the debt should be discharged, and the pledgee should not recover it again.
This objection represents a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes the timing of ownership: the pledgor's ownership via ultimate liability is subsequent to the contract, so at the time of the pawn he did not own the item. The pledgee's recourse is based on deception arising from delivery after the contract.
This is unlike the case where the claimant sues the pledgor directly, where ownership is backdated.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the objection were accepted, the pledgee would lose the right to the debt. The Hanafi position protects the pledgee by allowing recovery of both value and debt.
The mufta bihi is the majority view of Imam Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If the pawned item perishes with the pledgee, then it is claimed, and he (the claimant) makes the pledgor liable for its value, it perishes (covering) his debt. And if he makes the pledgee liable, he (the pledgee) recovers from the pledgor both its value and his debt. That is, he (the pledgor) becomes one who has satisfied his debt. And 'If he makes the pledgee liable' means the claimant has the choice between holding the pledgor or the pledgee liable. If he holds the pledgor liable, he (the pledgor) owns it by paying the liability, so the pledge is valid. And if he holds the pledgee liable, he (the pledgee) recovers from the pledgor the value because he is deceived by the pledgor, and the debt because his possession is invalidated, so his right returns as before. Muhammad (Allah?) stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Asl what we explained, so an agency not stipulated in the contract is like stipulated in it in all the rulings we mentioned there. His statement: 'If it perishes...' etc.; its clarification: If the pawned item perishes with the pledgee, i.e., in his hand, then it is claimed, i.e., then a man claims it, so he has the choice: if he wishes, he holds the pledgor liable, and if he wishes, he holds the pledgee liable, because each of them has transgressed against his right. As for the pledgor, by delivering the pawn to the pledgee; as for the pledgee, by taking possession. So the pledgor becomes like a usurper, and the pledgee like the usurper of a usurper. So the claimant holds liable whichever he wishes. The commentator indicated this by his statement: 'the following', i.e., the claimant has the choice, etc. This is in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'and he made liable...' etc.; i.e., if the claimant holds the pledgor liable, then he (the pledgor) owns it through the pledgee; i.e., he becomes one who has satisfied his debt because the pledgor owned the pawned item by paying the liability, so the satisfaction was valid. His statement: 'And if he holds the pledgee liable...' etc.; by its value or his debt. As for the value, because he is deceived by the pledgor, and the deceived person recovers from the deceiver the liability that befell him, just as the lessee recovers from the lessor, and the depositee from the depositor. And as for the debt, because the pledgee's possession is invalidated, so his right returns as before. His statement: 'because he is deceived' means the pledgor made him other than himself, where he pawned someone else's property and became as if he is the one who caused the liability upon him. An objection was raised against it: Since the ultimate liability for the value is on the pledgor, and ownership of the thing paid as liability is established for the one upon whom the ultimate liability rests, it becomes clear that he pawned his own property. His statement: 'An objection was raised...' etc.; its author is Abu Hazim al-Qadi (may Allah have mercy on him?) who criticized Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. Its explanation: Since the ultimate liability for the value is on the pledgor due to the pledgee's recourse against the pledgor for the value, and ownership of the thing paid as liability is established for the one upon whom the ultimate liability rests, it becomes clear that he pawned his own property. So it becomes as if the claimant had held the pledgor liable initially, and the ruling here should also be that it perishes covering the debt, and the pledgee does not recover his debt from the pledgor. The answer to it: The pledgee recovers from the pledgor the liability due to deception, and deception is due to delivery, as it was mentioned that each of them has transgressed against his right by delivery or by transfer from the pledgee to him, as if he is his agent. And ownership for each (of the delivery and transfer) is subsequent to the contract of pawn. As for delivery, it is obvious because delivery was after the contract, so it becomes clear that he pawned property not his own. As for transfer, because the pledgee is a usurper with respect to the claimant, so when he is made liable, he owns the thing paid, but since the ultimate liability is on the pledgor, his ownership transfers to him, so he owns it from the pledgee, and the pledgee owned it from the moment of possession because he became a usurper of it, so the pledgor owns it later from him, so the pledgor's ownership is subsequent to the contract of pawn, so it is as if he pawned property not his own, unlike the first scenario, because the claimant holds him liable based on possession prior to the pawn, so ownership is attributed to him, so it becomes clear that he pawned his own property. This is in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah deals with a specific scenario in the law of pawn (rahn): when a pawned item is destroyed (halaka) while in the hands of the pledgee (murtahin), and then a third party claims (istahiqqa) that the item was actually his property. The text first states the ruling: the claimant has the option to hold either the pledgor (rahin) or the pledgee liable for the value of the item. If he holds the pledgor liable, the pledgor pays the value and thereby owns the item; this means that the debt for which the item was pawned is considered satisfied (the item 'perishes covering the debt'). If he holds the pledgee liable, the pledgee pays the value to the claimant, but then the pledgee can recover that value from the pledgor (because the pledgor deceived him by pawning property he did not own) and also recover the original debt (because the pledgee's possession is retroactively invalidated, so his right to the debt returns). The commentary elaborates: each party has transgressed (ta'addi) — the pledgor by delivering someone else's property, the pledgee by taking possession — so the claimant can sue either. Then an objection (qila 'alayhi) is raised by Abu Hazim al-Qadi against Imam Muhammad's ruling. The objection argues that when the claimant sues the pledgee, the ultimate liability (qarar al-daman) for the value lies with the pledgor (since the pledgee can recover from him). Consequently, the ownership of the item as paid liability vests in the one on whom the ultimate liability rests, namely the pledgor. Therefore, it becomes evident that the pledgor effectively pawned his own property (since he ends up owning the item), and the ruling should be that the debt is discharged as if the claimant had sued the pledgor directly. The answer (al-jawab) refutes this: the pledgee's recourse against the pledgor is due to deception (ghurur) arising from the delivery (taslim) of the item after the contract. The ownership that the pledgor eventually acquires via the pledgee's recourse is subsequent to the contract of pawn. Therefore, at the time of the contract, he did not own the item — he pawned someone else's property. This is different from the case where the claimant sues the pledgor directly, because there the ownership is backdated to before the contract. The commentary concludes by affirming that the pledgee can recover both the value and the debt from the pledgor. This is the established Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwayah) from Imam Muhammad's works.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract by which property is given as security for a debt; the item is held by the creditor (pledgee) but ownership remains with the debtor (pledgor).
المستحق (al-mustahiq): The true owner of the property that was wrongfully pawned; he has the right to claim the property or its value from the transressor.
الغرور (al-ghurur): Deception that causes harm; here, the pledgor deceives the pledgee by pawning another's property, making the pledgee liable to the claimant.
قرار الضمان (qarar al-daman): The ultimate liability; who is ultimately responsible for paying the value. In this case, although the pledgee pays first, he recovers from the pledgor, so the pledgor bears the ultimate liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling discussed, if a pawned property is destroyed in the hands of the pledgee and then claimed by a third party, what can the claimant do?
A) Only sue the pledgor for the value.
B) Only sue the pledgee for the value.
C) Sue either the pledgor or the pledgee for the value.
D) The claimant has no recourse because the property is destroyed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the pledgee to recover both the value and the debt from a pledgor who pawned another's property, based on deception and invalid possession, despite an objection that ultimate liability transfers ownership.

Pledge Validity When Ownership Arises Later
Commentator defends Imam Muhammad against Abu Hazim's criticism on pledge validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a pledge where the pledgor acquires ownership after the contract, and evaluate the objection of Abu Hazim.

Core Idea
A pledge is valid if ownership is later acquired, because ghurur gives the pledgee recourse against the pledgor.

This passage addresses a subtle issue in the law of pledge (rahn): what if a person pledges property that he does not yet own, but later becomes the owner? Abu Hazim al-Qadi objected that this invalidates the pledge. The commentator defends Imam Muhammad's view that the pledge remains valid, based on the concept of deception (ghurur) arising from the pledgee's possession. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi law balances contractual fairness with ownership requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
First, understand Abu Hazim's argument: since the pledgor bears liability (daman), he must own the property at the time of the contract; otherwise, the pledge is void.
Abu Hazim relies on the principle that liability and ownership coincide.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The commentator answers that the pledgee's recourse is due to ghurur (deception) caused by the pledgee's taking possession, which occurred after the contract. The pledgor's ownership is later, so he pledged what was not his at the time.
The response distinguishes between delivery (taslim) and transfer (intiqal) as grounds.
3
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
The commentator contrasts two cases: (1) delivery-based: possession after contract makes it seem like pledging non-own property; (2) transfer-based: the pledgee becomes a usurper, then ownership transfers to pledgor later. Both validate the pledge.
The objector's analogy to direct guarantee by the rightful owner is rejected because ownership there is retroactive.
4
Extract the Practical Consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the Hanafi view, the pledge is valid and the pledgee can claim the debt from the pledgor. Under the objection, the pledge would be void.
This shows the importance of the ruling for real transactions.
5
Identify the Basis in Hanafi Usul
The ruling rests on istihsan (juristic preference) through the concept of ghurur, not strict analogy. This reflects Hanafi flexibility in mu'amalat.
The proof is rational, not textual, but grounded in the school's principles.

قيل عليه: لما كان قرار الضمان على الراهن والملك في المضمونِ يثبت لمن عليه قرار الضمان ، فتبين أنه رهن ملك نفسه.
[ ١ ] قوله : قيل عليه...الخ ؛ قائله أبو حازم القاضي لله الذي طعن على الإمام محمد بن الحسن . وتقريره : لما كان قرار الضمان على الراهن برجوع المرتهن على الراهن بضمان القيمة، والملك في المضمون يثبتُ من عليه قرارُ الضمان ، فتَبيَّن أنه رهن ملك نفسه، فصار كما إذا ضَمَّنَ المستحق الراهن ابتداء، وينبغي أن يحكم هاهنا أيضاً بأنه مات بالدين، ولا يرجع المرتهن على الراهن بدينه.
والجواب عنه : إنه إنّ المرتهن يرجعُ على الراهن بالضمان بسبب الغرور، والغرور بالتسليم، كما ذكر أنّ كلّ واحدٍ منهما متعد في حقه بالتسليم أو بالانتقال من المرتهن إليه، كأنه وكيل منه ، والملك لكلِّ واحدٍ من التسليم، والانتقال متأخر عن عقد الرهن.
أما بالتسليم فظاهر ؛ لأنّ التسليم كان بعد العقد، فتبين له رهن غير ملكه. وأما بالانتقال ؛ فلأن المرتهن غاصب في حق المستحق، فإذا ضمن ملك المضمون، ولكن لما كان قرار الضمان على الراهن انتقل ملكه إليه، فيملكه من جهة المرتهن، والمرتهن ملكه من حين القبض ؛ لأنه صار غاصبا به، فيملك الراهن بعد ذلك من جهته، فيكون ملك الراهن متأخّراً عن عقد الرهن ، فكأنه رهن غير ملكه بخلاف الوجه الأوّل ؛ لأنّ المستحق يضمنه باعتبار القبض السابق على الرهن، فيستند الملك إليه ، فتبين أنه رهن ملك نفسه. هكذا في شروح «الهداية».
It was said against him: Since the liability is upon the pledgor and ownership of the pledged object is established for the one who bears liability, it becomes clear that he pledged his own property. [1] His statement: 'It was said against him...' etc.; its speaker is Abu Hazim al-Qadi who criticized Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. And its explanation: Since the liability is upon the pledgor by the pledgee's recourse against the pledgor for the guarantee of the value, and ownership of the pledged object is established for the one who bears liability, it becomes clear that he pledged his own property. Thus it is as if the rightful owner made the pledgor liable from the beginning, and it should be ruled here as well that he died with the debt, and the pledgee does not have recourse against the pledgor for his debt. The answer to it is: The pledgee does have recourse against the pledgor for the guarantee due to the deception (ghurur), and deception is by delivery, as mentioned that each of them is an aggressor in his right either by delivery or by transfer from the pledgee to him, as if he is his agent. The ownership for each is by delivery, and the transfer is later than the pledge contract. As for by delivery, it is obvious because the delivery was after the contract, so it turns out that he pledged something other than his own property. As for by transfer, because the pledgee is a usurper in the right of the rightful owner, so when he guarantees the ownership of the pledged object, but since the liability is upon the pledgor, his ownership transfers to him, so he owns it from the side of the pledgee, and the pledgee owned it from the time of possession because he became a usurper by it. So the pledgor subsequently owns it from his side, so the pledgor's ownership is later than the pledge contract, so it is as if he pledged other than his own property, unlike the first face because the rightful owner guarantees the pledgee by virtue of possession earlier than the pledge, so ownership is attributed to him, so it becomes clear that he pledged his own property. This is in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending a ruling of Imam Muhammad. The objector, Abu Hazim, argues that if liability is on the pledgor, then the pledged object must be owned by him, leading to an absurdity. The response distinguishes two scenarios: the pledgee's possession creates a deception, and the pledgor's ownership is acquired later, so the pledge was of property that appeared to be the pledgor's but actually was not at the time of contract. The commentator explains that the pledgee's right to recourse is due to deception in delivery, and the transfer of ownership is subsequent to the contract, so the pledgor effectively pledged something that became his own later, which is valid. The analogy with direct guarantee by the rightful owner is rejected because in that case ownership is retroactive. The relied-upon position is that the pledge is valid and the pledgee can claim the debt. The authority weight is from the commentaries of al-Hidayah, which are based on Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
قرار الضمان (qarar al-daman): The liability or responsibility for guaranteeing the value of the pledged object.
غرور (ghurur): Deception that arises from the pledgee's possession, which grounds the recourse against the pledgor.
متعد (muta'addin): One who transgresses or commits an aggression, here referring to the pledgee and the rightful owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the pledge contract itself creates liability.
B) Because the pledgor committed fraud by misrepresenting ownership.
C) Because the pledgee's possession created deception (ghurur).
D) Because the rightful owner transferred his right to the pledgee.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is valid if the pledgor later acquires ownership, because the pledgee's right to recourse is based on deception arising from possession, not on ownership at contract time.

Ruling on Sale of Pledged Property and Status of Its Price
Opening ruling on disposition of pledged property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sale of pledged property by the pledgor and explain why the price automatically becomes pledged according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Core Idea
A pledgor's sale of his pledge is suspended until ratification or debt payment, and the price becomes pledged automatically.

This passage from the chapter on rahn (pledge) introduces the rules for the pledgor's disposition of the pledged property. The key ruling is that such a sale is not effective immediately but is suspended (mawquf), pending either the pledgee's ratification or the discharge of the debt. Furthermore, once the sale becomes effective, the price received takes the place of the original pledge. The passage also discusses an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the price becomes pledged automatically or only if stipulated, and settles on the view of the matn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The pledgor sells his pledge without permission. Initially the sale is 'mawquf' (suspended) because the pledgee has a right attached to the object.
Mawquf means the contract is valid but its effectiveness depends on subsequent approval.
2
Identify what makes it effective
Two triggering events: ijaza (ratification) by the pledgee, or qada' al-dayn (payment of the debt) by the pledgor.
The commentary compares this to a will exceeding one-third, which is suspended upon heirs' ratification.
3
Note the consequence
Upon effectiveness, the price (thaman) becomes a pledge (rahn) in place of the original object.
The pledgee's security interest transfers to the substitute.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that the price becomes pledged only if stipulated at the time of ratification; the relied-upon position (matn) holds it is automatic.
This is a legitimate difference within the school.
5
Understand the reasoning
The pledgee's right attaches to the financial value (maliyya) of the object, not the specific object itself. Therefore, the right transfers to the substitute (badal) automatically.
Analogies: consumption (istihlak) and sale of a debt slave with creditors' consent.

باب التصرف والجناية في الرهن
(وقف) بيع الراهن رهنه ، فإن أجاز (۳) مرتهنه أو قضى دينه نفذ)، وصار ثمنه رهناً
[١] قوله : باب التصرف ... الخ ؛ لما كان التصرف في الرهن بعد ثبوت الرهن ، وكذلك الجناية على الرهن وجنايته على غيره عقيب مسائل الرهن ؛ لأنّ كل ترتيب يجب طبعاً يجب وضعاً للمناسبة. كذا في «غاية البيان».
(٢) قوله : وقف... الخ ؛ أي إذا باع الراهن رهنه بغير إذن المرتهن، فالبيع موقوف ؛ لتعلق حق الغير وهو المرتهن، فيتوقف على إجازته ، وإن كان التصرف في ملكه كمن ماله، فالوصيّة فيما زادَ على الثلث موقوف على إجازة الورثة ؛ لتعلق حقهم به ، فلذا هذا. [٣] قوله : فإن أجاز...الخ ؛ أي المرتهن نفذ ؛ لأنَّ التوقف كان لحقه وقدره ؛ أي بسقوطه أو قضى دينه ؛ أي إن قضى الراهن الدين نفذ أيضاً ؛ لأن المانع من النفوذ قد زال، والمقتضي لنفاذ البيع موجود ، وهو التصرّف الصادر من الأهل في المحل المملوك، وصار ثمنه رهنا ؛ أي إذا نفذ البيع بالإجازة يكون بدل المرهون، وهو الثمن رهنا، إلا في رواية عن أبي يوسف الله ، وهي أنّه إن شرط عند الإجازة أن يكون الثمن رهناً كان رهناً وإلا فلا ؛ لأنه إذا أجاز بهذا الشرط فما رضي ببطلان حقه عن العين إلا وأن يكون متعلقاً بالبدل. حقه منه ، فأما إذا لم يشترط سقط حقه عن المرهون، والثمن ليس بمرهون، فلا يتعلق والصحيح ما في «الكتاب» ؛ لأنّ حقه تعلّق بالمالية، والبدلُ له حكم المبدل . وأيضاً : إن خروجه بالبيع والبيع أوجب الزوال إلى بدل، فيتعلّق حقه بالبدل، سواء شرط أو لا، كما لو استهلكه إنسان فإنّه يتعلّق حقه بالقيمة ، فصار كالعبد المديون إذا بيع برضاء الغرماء ، ينتقل حقهم إلى البدل ؛ لأنهم رضوا بالانتقال دون السقوط رأسا، فكذا هذا.
Chapter on disposition and injury in the pledge. The pledgor's sale of his pledge is suspended; if the pledgee ratifies it or he pays his debt, it becomes effective, and its price becomes a pledge. [2] - [1] Chapter on disposition and injury in the pledge. (Suspended) is the pledgor's sale of his pledge; if the pledgee ratifies or he pays his debt, it becomes effective, and its price becomes a pledge. [1] His statement: 'Chapter on disposition...' etc.; because disposition in the pledge after the establishment of the pledge, and likewise injury upon the pledge and injury by the pledge upon others, follows the issues of pledge; because every order that is necessary by nature is necessary by positioning for suitability. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. (2) His statement: 'Suspended...' etc.; i.e., if the pledgor sells his pledge without the permission of the pledgee, the sale is suspended (mawquf) due to the attachment of the right of another, which is the pledgee; so it depends on his ratification. Although the disposition is in his own property, like one who bequeaths his property, the will for more than one-third is suspended upon the ratification of the heirs due to the attachment of their right, so similarly here. [3] His statement: 'If he ratifies...' etc.; i.e., the pledgee ratifies, it becomes effective because the suspension was for his right and it ceased; or he pays his debt, i.e., if the pledgor pays the debt, it also becomes effective because the impediment to effectiveness has been removed and the basis for the sale's effectiveness exists, which is the disposition emanating from a qualified person in an owned object. And 'its price becomes a pledge'; i.e., if the sale becomes effective by ratification, the substitute for the pledged object (the price) becomes a pledge. Except in a narration from Abu Yusuf that if he stipulates at the time of ratification that the price be a pledge, it is a pledge; otherwise not, because when he ratifies with this condition, he is not satisfied with the nullification of his right over the specific object unless it is attached to the substitute. As for if he does not stipulate, his right over the collateral falls and the price is not pledged, so it does not attach. The correct position is what is in the book (al-Kitab) because his right is attached to the financial value, and the substitute has the same ruling as the original. Also, its removal by sale, and the sale necessitates transfer to a substitute, so his right attaches to the substitute, whether he stipulates or not, just as if a person consumes it, his right attaches to the value. So it becomes like a slave in debt, if sold with the consent of the creditors, their right transfers to the substitute because they consent to transfer and not to outright fall, similarly here.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on disposition and injury regarding pledged property. The ruling stated is that if the pledgor sells the pledged item without permission, the sale is suspended (mawquf). It becomes effective if the pledgee ratifies the sale or if the pledgor pays off the debt. Once effective, the price received takes the place of the original and itself becomes pledged. The commentary provides justification by analogy with a will exceeding one-third, which is suspended pending heirs' approval. It then discusses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that the price becomes pledged only if so stipulated at ratification, while the correct position (in al-Kitab, i.e., the transmitted text) is that it becomes pledged automatically because the pledgee's right attaches to the financial value, not the specific object. The analogy is given of consumption (istihlak) where the right attaches to the value, and of a debt slave sold with creditors' consent. The relied-upon position is that of the book, which is the Zahir al-Riwayah view.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; the sale is not immediately effective but pending ratification or condition.
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification or approval by the pledgee that makes the suspended sale effective.
بدل (badal): Substitute or compensation; here the price received in place of the pledged item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only if the pledgee stipulates so at the time of ratification.
B) Only after the debt is paid in full.
C) Automatically when the sale becomes effective.
D) It never becomes pledged; the right ends.

Key Takeaway
A sale of pledged property without permission is suspended until ratification or debt payment, after which the price becomes pledged automatically.

Sale of Pledged Property Without Permission: Suspended Transaction
Statement of the ruling on sale by pledgor followed by commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a pledgor's sale of pawn without permission, identify the conditions for its effectiveness, and explain the Hanafi justification using qiyas.

Core Idea
A pledgor's sale of pawn is suspended (mawquf) until the pledgee permits or debt is repaid; the price then automatically becomes a new pledge.

When a person pledges an item as collateral, the pledgee holds a right over that item. What happens if the pledgor sells the pledged property without the pledgee's permission? Is the sale void, or can it be valid later? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that such a sale is suspended (mawquf) and becomes effective once the pledgee's right is removed, with the price substituting as the new pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The act is the pledgor's sale of the pawned item without the pledgee's permission. The initial ruling is that the sale is suspended (mawquf) – not void, not immediately valid.
The term mawquf is distinct from batil (void) and fasid (irregular).
2
Identify the cause of suspension
The suspension is due to the pledgee's right (haqq al-irtihan) over the pawn. Since the sale affects this right, it depends on the pledgee's consent or removal of the right.
Hanafi usul: a contract affecting a third party's right is suspended until that party permits.
3
Identify the conditions for effectiveness
The sale becomes effective (nafidh) if either (a) the pledgee grants permission (ijazah) after the sale, or (b) the pledgor repays the debt, removing the pledgee's right over the pawn.
These are not conditions for validity but for the suspension to lift.
4
Analyze the proof: qiyas on destruction
The Hanafi proof uses qiyas: If a third party destroys the pawn, the pledgee's right transfers to the compensation (qimah). Similarly, when the pawn is sold with subsequent permission, the right transfers to the price. The effective cause (illa) is that the right attaches to the value (maliyyah), not the specific item (ayn).
This qiyas is jali (clear) because the illa is explicit: value-based attachment.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute and the relied-upon view
Abu Yusuf (in one narration) holds that the price becomes a pledge only if the pledgee stipulates this at the time of permission. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that it automatically becomes a pledge. The correct view (al-sahih) according to zahir al-riwayah is the automatic transfer.
The tarjih is based on the principle that the right is in the value, and the substitute takes the ruling of the substituted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التصرف والجناية في الرهن
(وقف) بيع الراهن رهنه ، فإن أجاز (۳) مرتهنه أو قضى دينه نفذ)، وصار ثمنه رهناً
[١] قوله : باب التصرف ... الخ ؛ لما كان التصرف في الرهن بعد ثبوت الرهن ، وكذلك الجناية على الرهن وجنايته على غيره عقيب مسائل الرهن ؛ لأنّ كل ترتيب يجب طبعاً يجب وضعاً للمناسبة. كذا في «غاية البيان».
(٢) قوله : وقف... الخ ؛ أي إذا باع الراهن رهنه بغير إذن المرتهن، فالبيع موقوف ؛ لتعلق حق الغير وهو المرتهن، فيتوقف على إجازته ، وإن كان التصرف في ملكه كمن ماله، فالوصيّة فيما زادَ على الثلث موقوف على إجازة الورثة ؛ لتعلق حقهم به ، فلذا هذا.
[٣] قوله : فإن أجاز...الخ ؛ أي المرتهن نفذ ؛ لأنَّ التوقف كان لحقه وقدره ؛ أي بسقوطه أو قضى دينه ؛ أي إن قضى الراهن الدين نفذ أيضاً ؛ لأن المانع من النفوذ قد زال، والمقتضي لنفاذ البيع موجود ، وهو التصرّف الصادر من الأهل في المحل المملوك، وصار ثمنه رهنا ؛ أي إذا نفذ البيع بالإجازة يكون بدل المرهون، وهو الثمن رهنا، إلا في رواية عن أبي يوسف الله ، وهي أنّه إن شرط عند الإجازة أن يكون الثمن رهناً كان رهناً وإلا فلا ؛ لأنه إذا أجاز بهذا الشرط فما رضي ببطلان حقه عن العين إلا وأن يكون متعلقاً بالبدل. فأما إذا لم يشترط سقط حقه عن المرهون، والثمن ليس بمرهون، فلا يتعلق حقه منه، والصحيح ما في «الكتاب» ; لأنّ حقه تعلّق بالمالية، والبدلُ له حكم المبدل . وأيضاً : إن خروجه بالبيع والبيع أوجب الزوال إلى بدل، فيتعلّق حقه بالبدل، سواء شرط أو لا، كما لو استهلكه إنسان فإنّه يتعلّق حقه بالقيمة ، فصار كالعبد المديون إذا بيع برضاء الغرماء ، ينتقل حقهم إلى البدل ؛ لأنهم رضوا بالانتقال دون السقوط رأسا، فكذا هذا.
Chapter on disposition and tort regarding the pledge. (Suspended) The pledgor's sale of his pawn: if the pledgee permits or he repays his debt, it becomes effective, and its price becomes a pledge. [1] His words: Chapter on disposition... etc. Because disposition regarding the pawn after the establishment of the pawn, and likewise the tort upon the pawn and the pawn's tort upon others, follow the issues of pawn; because every order that is naturally necessary is also conventionally necessary for suitability. [2] His words: Suspended... etc.; i.e., if the pledgor sells his pawn without the permission of the pledgee, the sale is suspended due to the attachment of the right of another, namely the pledgee, so it depends on his permission. [3] His words: if he permits... etc.; i.e., the pledgee, it becomes effective because the suspension was due to his right and it was removed, or he repays his debt; i.e., if the pledgor repays the debt it also becomes effective, because the impediment to effectiveness has been removed and the prerequisite for effectiveness of the sale exists, i.e., the disposition issuing from a qualified person in owned property. And its price becomes a pledge; i.e., if the sale becomes effective by permission, the substitute of the pawn, i.e., the price, becomes a pledge, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf, namely that if he stipulated at the time of permission that the price be a pledge, it is a pledge, otherwise not; because if he permitted with this stipulation, he did not consent to the cancellation of his right from the specific item unless it becomes attached to the substitute. But if he did not stipulate, his right from the pawn falls, and the price is not a pledge, so his right does not attach. The correct view is what is in the book (zahir al-riwayah), because his right attaches to the value, and the substitute has the ruling of the substituted. Also, if it exits by sale, and the sale necessitates transfer to a substitute, then his right attaches to the substitute, whether he stipulates or not, just as if someone had destroyed it, his right attaches to the value. So it is like the case of a slave who is indebted when sold with the consent of creditors: their right transfers to the substitute, because they consented to transfer, not absolute cancellation. Similarly here.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from a Hanafi fiqh work on the chapter of rahn (pledge). The matn (base text) states: 'The sale of the pawn by the pledgor is suspended. If the pledgee permits or the pledgor repays the debt, the sale becomes effective and the price becomes a pledge.' The commentator explains the rationale: the sale is suspended because the pledgee has a right over the pawn. Once that right is removed (by permission or debt repayment), the sale becomes effective. The price then becomes a new pledge in place of the original item. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to a narration from Abu Yusuf, the price becomes a pledge only if the pledgee stipulates it at the time of permission. However, the correct view (al-sahih) as recorded in the zahir al-riwayah is that the price automatically becomes a pledge, because the creditor's right is attached to the value, not to the specific item. The commentator supports this with analogy: if a third party destroys the pawn, the creditor's right transfers to the value (compensation). Similarly, if a slave is sold with the creditors' consent, their right transfers to the price. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract by which property is held as security for a debt; the pawned item.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a transaction that is not immediately enforceable due to a third party's right.
إجازة (ijazah): Permission or ratification given by the pledgee for the sale of the pawn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the price when a sale of pawned property becomes effective after the pledgee's permission?
A) The price is paid to the pledgor and the pledge is released.
B) The price automatically becomes the new pledge unless the pledgee stipulates otherwise.
C) The price becomes a new pledge only if the pledgee stipulates it.
D) The price is forfeited to the state.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats a pledgor's sale without permission as suspended, not void, and the price automatically becomes the new pledge upon effectiveness.

Effect of Pledgee's Annulment of Sale: Two Narrations
Discussion of two narrations on whether pledgee may annul the sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two narrations on the pledgee's rescission and explain why the soundest view holds that the sale remains suspended.

Core Idea
The pledgee's right is only retention (habs), not ownership, so his rescission cannot annul the sale.

In the Hanafi school, there is a dispute about whether the pledgee (mortgagee) can annul a sale of the pawn that he did not permit. This passage presents two narrations: one that grants the pledgee annulment power, and the sounder view that denies it. Understanding the basis of each view is crucial for applying the correct ruling in pledge transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Read the passage and locate the two conflicting narrations regarding the pledgee's rescission.
The first narration is attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani; the second (sounder) is preferred.
2
Understand the rationale of the first narration
The first narration treats the pledgee's right as akin to ownership, giving him the power to annul the sale.
This is an analogy (qiyas) to ownership.
3
Understand the rationale of the sounder narration
The sounder view holds that the pledgee's right is only retention (habs), not ownership, so his rescission does not affect the sale.
The right of retention is not nullified by the contract; the sale remains suspended.
4
Note the practical consequence for the buyer
The buyer may either wait for the pawn to be redeemed (and thus the sale becomes effective) or raise the matter to the judge for annulment due to inability to deliver.
The judge has the authority to annul, not the pledgee.
5
Compare to the absconding slave case
The commentator draws an analogy: just as a buyer of a runaway slave may wait or seek judicial annulment, here the buyer has the same options.
This qiyas strengthens the sounder view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يجز وفسخ لا ينفسخ في الأصح ، وصبَرَ المشتري إلى فك الرهن، أو رفع إلى القاضي ليفسخ ). اعلم أن المرتهن إذا فسخ ينفسخ في رواية) ، والأصح أنه لا ينفسخ ؛ لأنَّ حقه في الحبس لا يبطل بانعقاد هذا العقد، فبقي موقوفاً ، فالمشتري إن شاءَ صبر إلى فك الرهن ، أو رفع الأمر إلى القاضي ; ليفسح البيع. 
[1] قوله : وإن لم يجز، وفسخ ... الخ ; أي وإن لم يجز المرتهن البيع وفسخ ، ففيه روايتان : في رواية : الفسخ ، حتى لو افتك الراهن رهنه لا سبيل للمشتري عليه ; لأنَّ الحق الثابت للمرتهن بمنزلة الملك ، فصار كالمالك له أن يجيز وله أن يفسخ. وفي رواية وهي أصح الروايتان لا ينفسخُ بفسخِهِ أشار إليه المصنف بقوله : لا ينفسخ في الأصح ; لأنه لو ثبت حق الفسخ له ثبت ضرورة صيانة حقه ، وحقه في الحبس وهو لا يبطل بانعقاد هذا العقد، فبقي موقوفاً ، فالمشتري إن شاء صبر إلى فك الرهن إذا عجز البائع عن التسليم على شرف الزوال، أو رفع الأمر إلى القاضي. والمقتضى أن يفسخ ; لفواتِ القدرة على التسليم، وولاية الفسخ للقاضي لا له ، وصار كما إذا أبق العبد المشترى قبل القبض، فإنّ المشتري بالخيار إن شاءَ صبر حتى يرجع الأبق، وإن شاءَ رفع الأمر إلى القاضي، ينفسخ بحكم العجز عن التسليم وكذلك هذا (٢).
And if he does not permit and rescinds, according to the soundest view it is not annulled, and the buyer waits until the redemption of the pawn, or raises the matter to the judge to annul. Know that if the pledgee rescinds, in one narration it is annulled, but the soundest view is that it is not annulled; because his right of retention is not nullified by the conclusion of this contract, so it remains suspended. So the buyer, if he wishes, may wait until the pawn is redeemed, or raise the matter to the judge to annul the sale. [1] His words: And if he does not permit and rescinds... etc.; i.e., if the pledgee does not permit the sale and rescinds, there are two narrations: in one narration, rescission is effective, to the extent that if the pledgor redeems his pawn, the buyer has no recourse against him; because the right of the pledgee is akin to ownership, so he becomes like an owner, having the right to permit or rescind. In the other narration, which is the sounder of the two, it is not annulled by his rescission, as indicated by the author (al-musannif) by his words: 'it is not annulled according to the soundest view'; because if the right of rescission were established for him, it would be established necessarily for safeguarding his right, but his right is the right of retention (habs), which is not nullified by the conclusion of this contract, so it remains suspended. So the buyer, if he wishes, may wait until the pawn is redeemed if the seller is unable to deliver, as it is on the verge of being lost, or raise the matter to the judge. The necessity is that it be annulled due to the inability to deliver, but the authority of annulment belongs to the judge, not to him. It is like the case of a slave that absconds before delivery: the buyer has the option, if he wishes, to wait until the slave returns, or if he wishes, to raise the matter to the judge, and it is annulled by virtue of inability to deliver. Similarly here.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the pledgee's rescission (faskh) of a sale that he did not permit. The matn states that if the pledgee does not permit the sale and rescinds, according to the soundest view (al-asahh) the sale is not annulled; the buyer may either wait for the pawn to be redeemed or raise the matter to the judge for annulment. The commentator expands on two narrations: In one narration (attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani), the pledgee's rescission is effective, and the buyer loses all rights. This is based on the analogy that the pledgee's right is like ownership, so he can annul the sale. In the stronger narration, the rescission is not effective because the pledgee's only right is the right of retention (habs), which is not extinguished by the sale; therefore, the sale remains suspended. The buyer cannot be forced to accept an invalid delivery and thus has the option to wait or seek judicial annulment due to inability to deliver. The commentator compares this to the case of a slave who runs away before delivery: the buyer can wait or go to the judge to annul. The relied-upon position is that the pledgee's rescission does not annul the sale; the authority to annul lies with the judge.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or rescission of a contract by one party or by judicial decree.
الأصح (al-asahh): The soundest view among multiple narrations within the Hanafi school.
حبس (habs): The right of the pledgee to retain possession of the pawn as security for the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the soundest view (al-asahh), what happens if the pledgee rescinds a sale of the pawn that he did not permit?
A) The sale is automatically annulled.
B) The sale is not annulled; the buyer may wait or seek judicial annulment.
C) The buyer must immediately return the pawn to the pledgor.
D) The pledgee must pay compensation to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The pledgee's rescission does not annul the sale; the sale remains suspended until judicial resolution.

Validity of Manumission, Tadbir, and Istilad by a Pledgor
Ruling statement on dispositions of pawned property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pledgor's manumission of pawned property and explain why it is valid without the pledgee's permission, distinguishing it from a sale.

Core Idea
A pledgor's manumission, tadbir, and istilad are valid because they arise from ownership and do not transfer property to another.

The pledgor remains the owner of the pawned property. While a sale requires the pledgee's consent because it transfers ownership, acts like manumission merely limit or terminate ownership without transferring it. This lesson examines the validity of such acts and the reasoning behind it, drawn from the Hanafi text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts
The passage mentions i'taq (manumission), tadbir (declaring freedom upon death), and istilad (acknowledging motherhood). All involve the owner's disposition of his slave.
2
State the ruling
The matn declares these acts are valid (sahih) regardless of whether the pledgor is wealthy or insolvent. The commentary confirms effectiveness (nifadh).
3
Understand the reasoning
The acts issue from a qualified person (the owner) in its proper place (his property). The pledgee's right does not prevent them because these acts do not transfer ownership to a third party.
4
Contrast with sale
A sale of pawned property is suspended (mawquf) and needs the pledgee's permission because it would transfer ownership to a buyer, eliminating the security.
The commentator explicitly contrasts with sale: 'unlike the sale whose effectiveness is prevented.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح إعتاقه وتدبيره واستيلاده رهنه ، فإن فعلها غنياً
[٢] قوله : صح إعتاقه ... الخ ; أي نفذ سواء كان موسراً أو معسراً; لصدوره من أهله في محله، وهو ملكه، فلا يلغو تصرُّفه بعدم إذن المرتهن، وامتناع النفاذ في البيع
And his manumission, tadbir, and istilad of his pawn are valid, even if he does them while wealthy. [2] His words: His manumission is valid... etc.; i.e., it is effective whether he is wealthy or insolvent; because it issues from a qualified person in its proper place, and it is his property, so his disposition is not rendered void by the lack of permission of the pledgee, unlike the sale whose effectiveness is prevented.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This is a ruling statement on the validity of certain acts of disposition performed by the pledgor on the pawned property. The matn declares that if the pledgor frees the slave (manumission), makes the slave mudabbar (free upon the owner's death), or makes a female slave the mother of his child (istilad), these are valid acts, even if he is wealthy (i.e., even though the pledgee's right exists). The commentator explains that these acts are effective because they come from a person who is legally competent to do them (the owner) and on his own property. The lack of permission from the pledgee does not invalidate these acts, unlike the sale of the pawn which is suspended and cannot become effective without permission because it would transfer ownership entirely. The reason is that manumission, tadbir, and istilad are acts that limit or terminate ownership but do not transfer it to another person, so the pledgee's right of retention does not prevent them.

Key Terms
إعتاق (i'taq): Manumission; freeing a slave by the owner's declaration.
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration that a slave will be free upon the owner's death.
استيلاد (istilad): Acknowledging a child born to a slave woman, thereby making her umm walad (free upon owner's death).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the pledgor's manumission of a pawned slave valid without the pledgee's permission?
A) Because manumission takes priority over all other rights.
B) Because the pledgor is the owner, and manumission does not transfer ownership to another person.
C) Because the pledgee has no right over the slave.
D) Because the manumission is only valid if the pledgor is wealthy.

Key Takeaway
A pledgor's manumission, tadbir, and istilad are valid because they arise from ownership and do not transfer ownership; the pledgee's security is preserved through the property's value.
