Effect of Mortgagee's Rescission on Sale
Intra-Hanafi ruling on mortgagee's rescission of a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred Hanafi ruling on the effect of a mortgagee's rescission on a sale and explain the reasoning behind the two narrations, including the analogy to a runaway slave.

Core Idea
The mortgagee's right to retain the pawn does not give him the power to nullify a sale; only the judge can nullify due to inability to deliver.

This passage addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: if a pledgor sells the mortgaged property without the mortgagee's consent, and the mortgagee then rescinds the sale, does that rescission nullify the sale? The answer depends on whether the mortgagee's right is akin to ownership or merely a right of retention. The preferred view (al-asah) is that rescission does not nullify; the sale remains suspended, and the buyer must either wait or approach the judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the topic is the ruling when a mortgagee rescinds a sale of the pawn made without his permission. Two narrations exist within the Hanafi school.
2
State the two narrations and their proofs
The minority narration says rescission nullifies because the mortgagee's right is akin to ownership. The preferred narration says it does not nullify because his right is merely retention.
The minority is attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani in some transmissions.
3
Analyze the preferred view's reasoning
Understand that the right of retention (habs) is not invalidated by the contract, so the sale remains suspended. The mortgagee cannot nullify; only the judge can.
Key distinction: retention vs. ownership.
4
Examine the analogy to the runaway slave
The preferred view draws an analogy: like a purchased slave that runs away before delivery, the buyer cannot nullify but can wait or go to the judge. Here the pawn is like the slave.
Analogy: asl = runaway slave before delivery; far' = mortgagee's rescission; illa = inability to deliver.
5
Determine the practical outcome (thamarah al-khilaf)
In the minority view, the buyer has no recourse after the mortgagee's rescission. In the preferred view, the buyer can wait or raise the matter to the judge.
The mufta bihi follows the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يجز وفسخ لا ينفسخ في الأصح ، وصَبَرَ المشتري إلى فك الرهن، أو رفع إلى القاضي ليفسخ . اعلم أن المرتهن إذا فسخ ينفسخ في رواية، والأصح أنه لا ينفسخ ؛ لأنَّ حقه في الحبس لا يبطل بانعقاد هذا العقد، فبقي موقوفاً ، فالمشتري إن شاءَ صبر إلى فك الرهن ، أو رفع الأمر إلى القاضي؛ ليفسح البيع. وفي رواية : الفسخ، حتى لو افتك الراهن رهنه لا سبيل للمشتري عليه ؛ لأنَّ الحق الثابت للمرتهن بمنزلة الملك، فصار كالمالك له أن يجيز وله أن يفسخ. وفي رواية وهي أصح الروايتان لا ينفسخُ بفسخِهِ؛ لأنه لو ثبت حق الفسخ له ثبت ضرورة صيانة حقه، وحقه في الحبس وهو لا يبطل بانعقاد هذا العقد، فبقي موقوفاً، فالمشتري إن شاء صبر إلى فك الرهن إذا عجز البائع عن التسليم على شرف الزوال، أو رفع الأمر إلى القاضي. والمقتضى أن يفسخ؛ لفواتِ القدرة على التسليم، وولاية الفسخ للقاضي لا له، وصار كما إذا أبق العبد المشترى قبل القبض، فإنّ المشتري بالخيار إن شاءَ صبر حتى يرجع الأبق، وإن شاءَ رفع الأمر إلى القاضي، ينفسخ بحكم العجز عن التسليم. وكذلك هذا.
And if he does not permit and rescinds, it is not nullified in the most correct view. The buyer waits until the pawn is redeemed, or raises the matter to the judge to nullify it. Know that if the mortgagee rescinds, it nullifies in one narration, but the most correct is that it does not nullify, because his right of retention is not invalidated by the conclusion of this contract, so it remains suspended. So the buyer may either wait until the pawn is redeemed or raise the matter to the judge to nullify the sale. In one narration: rescission, so that if the pledgor redeems the pawn, the buyer has no claim on him, because the right established for the mortgagee is akin to ownership, so he becomes like an owner—he may authorize or rescind. In a narration that is the most correct of the two narrations, it is not nullified by his rescission, because if the right of rescission were established for him, it would be established necessarily to protect his right, and his right is retention, which is not invalidated by the conclusion of this contract, so it remains suspended. So the buyer may either wait until the pawn is redeemed if the seller is unable to deliver due to imminent loss, or raise the matter to the judge. The requirement is that he nullify due to inability to deliver; the authority to nullify belongs to the judge, not to him. And this is like when a purchased slave runs away before delivery—the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he waits until the slave returns, and if he wishes, he raises the matter to the judge, who nullifies due to inability to deliver. And so is this.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the mortgagee's rescission of a sale made by the pledgor without his permission. The matn states the preferred ruling that the rescission does not nullify the sale. The commentator expands on two narrations: in one (attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani), the rescission nullifies because the mortgagee's right is like ownership; in the other, which is the relied-upon view (al-asah), it does not nullify but leaves the sale suspended. The reasoning is that the mortgagee's right is merely retention, not ownership, and that right is not invalidated by the contract. Thus the buyer must wait and cannot unilaterally nullify; only the judge can nullify due to inability to deliver. The commentator draws an analogy to a purchased slave who runs away before delivery, where the buyer has a similar option.

Key Terms
الراهن (al-rāhin): The person who gives the pawn (pledgor).
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The person who holds the pawn (mortgagee).
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, here by the mortgagee.
الحبس (al-habs): The mortgagee's right to retain possession of the pawn until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is automatically nullified.
B) The sale remains suspended; the buyer can wait or go to the judge.
C) The buyer must immediately return the item to the mortgagee.
D) The mortgagee must accept the sale.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's rescission does not nullify the sale; only the judge can nullify it due to inability to deliver, protecting the buyer's rights.

Validity of Manumission of a Pledged Slave by a Wealthy Pledgor
Ruling on manumitting a pawned slave and its effect on debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on manumitting a pawned slave and apply the distinction between immediate and deferred debt to determine the mortgagee's recourse.

Core Idea
Manumission of a pawned slave is valid, but the mortgagee's right is protected by substituting value.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that a pledgor's act of freeing a pawned slave is effective because he owns it, yet it does not deprive the mortgagee of security. The commentary explains the consequences for wealthy vs. insolvent pledgors and immediate vs. deferred debts, ensuring the mortgagee's right is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is manumission (i'taq), tadbir, or istilad of a pawned slave. The ruling: it is valid and executed regardless of the mortgagee's permission.
Validity stems from the pledgor owning the slave.
2
Understand the reason for validity
The pledgor is the owner and the act issues from a competent person in its proper place (ownership). Pawn does not transfer ownership, only possession as security.
Contrast with sale/gift, which require delivery and are invalid.
3
Distinguish from sale and gift
Sale and gift are ineffective because they require the ability to deliver possession, which is blocked by the pawnholder's right. Manumission does not require physical delivery.
This highlights the unique nature of manumission.
4
Apply the rule for a wealthy pledgor with immediate debt
If the debt is due, the mortgagee demands payment from the pledgor; if unpaid, the mortgagee can take the (now free) slave in satisfaction because it is of the same type (if the debt is of the same genus).
The mortgagee's right shifts from the slave as collateral to the value.
5
Apply the rule for a wealthy pledgor with deferred debt
If the debt is deferred, the court takes the slave's current value from the pledgor and makes it a new pawn until the term ends. At maturity, the mortgagee collects from this new pawn.
If the value exceeds the debt, the excess is returned; if less, the mortgagee can claim the remainder.

وصح إعتاقه وتدبيره واستيلاده رهنه ، فإن فعلها غنياً ففي دينه حالاً أخذ دينه، وفي مؤجَلِهِ قيمته للرهن بدله إلى محل أجله . قوله: صح إعتاقه ... الخ؛ أي نفذ سواء كان موسراً أو معسراً؛ لصدوره من أهله في محله، وهو ملكه، فلا يلغو تصرُّفه بعدم إذن المرتهن، وامتناع النفاذ في البيع والهبة؛ لانعدام القدرة على التسليم، وتمامه في (الهداية)، ومثل الاعتاق الوقف. وفي «الإسعاف» وغيره: لو وقف المرهون بعد تسليمه أجبره القاضي على دفع ما عليه إن كان موسراً، فإن كان معسراً أبطل الوقف وباعَه فيما عليه، هكذا في «رد المحتار». قوله: ففي دينه حالاً ...الخ؛ يعني إذا كان الدين حالاً طالب المرتهن الراهن بعد العتق بالدين إذا كان موسراً؛ لأنه إذا طولبَ بالرَّهن كان له أن يأخذه بدينه إذا كان من جنس حقه، فيكون إيفاء واستيفاء، فلا فائدة فيه. قوله: وفي مؤجله...الخ؛ يعني لو كان الدِّينُ مؤجلاً يؤخذ من المعتق قيمة الرهن، وتجعل رهناً مكان المرهون إذا كان معسراً؛ لأنَّ سبب الضمان قد تحقق، وفي التضمين فائدة، وفي حصول الاستيثاق من الوجه الذي ينساه ويحبسها إلى حلول الأجل، فإذا حلَّ اقتضاه بحقه إذا كان بجنسه؛ لأنَّ للغريم أن يستوفي حقه من مال غريمه إذا ظفر بجنس حقه، وإن كان فيه فضل ردَّه لانتهاء حكم الرهن بالاستيفاء، وإن كان أقل من جنسه رجع بالزيادة؛ لعدم ما يسقطه. كذا في «تكملة البحر».
And his manumission, tadbir, and istilad of his pawn are valid. If he does them while wealthy, then regarding his immediate debt, he takes his debt; and regarding his deferred debt, his value is taken for the pawn as substitute until the term of the deferred debt. The commentator says: 'His manumission is valid' means it is executed whether he is wealthy or insolvent, because it issues from a competent person in its proper place, namely his ownership. So his disposition is not invalidated by lack of permission from the mortgagee, but it is ineffective in sale and gift due to inability to deliver. This is complete in al-Hidayah. Similar to manumission is waqf. In al-Is'af and others: if the pawn is made waqf after delivery, the judge forces him to pay what is due if he is wealthy; if insolvent, the judge nullifies the waqf and sells it for what is due. So in Radd al-Muhtar. The commentator says: 'Regarding his immediate debt' means if the debt is due, the mortgagee demands the pledgor after manumission for the debt if he is wealthy, because if he demanded the pawn, he could take it for his debt if it is of the same type, so it would be payment and receipt, so no benefit. The commentator says: 'Regarding his deferred debt' means if the debt is deferred, the value of the pawn is taken from the manumittor and made a pawn in place of the pawned item if he is insolvent, because the cause of guarantee has occurred, and there is benefit in taking a pledge, and in obtaining security from the aspect that he forgets and holds it until the term arrives. When it arrives, he demands his right if it is of the same type, because a creditor can collect his right from his debtor's property if he finds property of the same type. If there is excess, he returns it because the ruling of pawn ends with collection; if it is less, he demands the remainder because there is nothing to drop it. So in Takmilat al-Bahr.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that a pledgor's manumission (i'taq), tadbir, or istilad of a slave he has pawned is valid and effective, regardless of whether he is wealthy or insolvent, because he is the owner. However, the effectiveness is limited: sale and gift are not valid because they require delivery which is impossible while the pawn is in the mortgagee's possession. For a wealthy pledgor, if the debt is due, the mortgagee simply demands payment and can take the pawn in satisfaction; if the debt is deferred, the court takes the value of the pawn and makes it a new pawn until the term. The commentary explains the wisdom behind these rules, analogizing to cases of waqf and ensuring the mortgagee's right is protected.

Key Terms
الإعتاق (al-iʿtāq): Manumission (freeing a slave).
التدبير (al-tadbīr): Declaring a slave to be free upon the owner's death.
الاستيلاد (al-istīlād): A master impregnating his female slave, making her a mother of a child (umm walad) and free upon his death.
الموسر (al-mūsir): A wealthy person with ability to pay debts.

Manumission of a pawned slave is valid; the mortgagee's security is protected by substituting the slave's value or demanding immediate payment.

Manumission of a Pledged Slave by an Insolvent Pledgor
Ruling on manumission by insolvent pledgor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on manumission of a pawned slave by an insolvent pledgor and explain the slave's obligation to pay via sa'y.

Core Idea
An insolvent pledgor's manumission is valid; the freed slave must pay the lesser of his value or the debt.

In Hanafi fiqh, a pledgor who is insolvent may still validly manumit a pawned slave. Since the mortgagee's right is attached to the slave, the freed slave must work and earn (sa'y) to compensate the mortgagee. The amount owed is the lesser of the slave's market value or the debt owed, ensuring fairness for both the creditor and the newly freed slave. This ruling balances the rights of ownership with the security interest of the mortgage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the manumission of a pawned slave by an insolvent pledgor. The Hanafi ruling: the manumission is valid (sahih).
2
Determine the consequence for the mortgagee
The mortgagee cannot demand payment from the insolvent pledgor; instead, he pursues the freed slave.
3
Specify the slave's obligation (sa'y)
The slave must engage in earning (sa'y) to pay the mortgagee the lesser of his market value or the outstanding debt.
The slave works and pays until the amount is fulfilled.
4
Distinguish the wealthy pledgor case
If the pledgor were wealthy (mawsūr), a different ruling applies (as per the earlier text): the mortgagee can demand payment directly from the pledgor.
This contrasting case reinforces the condition of insolvency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن فعلها معسراً ففي العتق سعى العبد في أقل من قيمته ومن دينه . قوله: وإن فعلها معسراً، ففي العتق ... الخ؛ لأنَّ حق المرتهن كان متعلقاً به، فإذا أعتقه الراهن معسراً، فليس للمرتهن أن يطالب الراهن؛ لأنه معسر، بل يتبع العبد فيسعى في أقل من قيمته ومن دينه، كما في تعليق الهداية. ومعنى سعايته أن يعمل ويكتسب ويؤدي إلى المرتهن حتى يوفي حقه. وإن كان الراهن موسراً، فكما مر.
And if he does them while insolvent, then regarding manumission, the slave works to pay the lesser of his value and his debt. The commentator says: 'And if he does them while insolvent, then regarding manumission' because the mortgagee's right was attached to him. So if the pledgor manumits him while insolvent, the mortgagee cannot demand from the pledgor because he is insolvent, but he pursues the slave, and the slave works to pay the lesser of his value and his debt, as in the commentary of al-Hidayah. The meaning of his working is that he labors, earns, and pays to the mortgagee until he fulfills his right. If the pledgor is wealthy, the ruling is as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage covers the scenario where an insolvent pledgor manumits a pawned slave. Since the pledgor cannot pay the debt, the mortgagee's right attaches to the freed slave himself. The slave must engage in earning (sa'y) to pay the lesser of two amounts: either the slave's market value or the debt owed. This ensures that the mortgagee does not suffer loss if the slave's value is less than the debt, and the slave is not burdened beyond his worth if the debt is larger. The commentary clarifies that the mortgagee cannot pursue the insolvent pledgor but must seek payment from the slave's earnings.

Key Terms
السعي (al-saʿy): The slave's obligation to work and earn to pay off a debt or value.
المعسر (al-muʿsir): An insolvent person who does not have the means to pay debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An insolvent pledgor's manumission is valid, but the slave must pay the lesser of his value or the debt through earning (sa'y).

Freeing a Mortgaged Slave by an Insolvent Debtor: The Obligation of Sa'aya
Commentary on the ruling of freeing a mortgaged slave by an insolvent debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mortgagor manumits a mortgaged slave while insolvent, calculate the amount the slave must work for (sa'aya), and distinguish the rules for tadbir and istilad.

Core Idea
An insolvent master's manumission frees the slave, but the slave must work to compensate the mortgagee—for the lesser of value or debt.

When a mortgagor manumits a mortgaged slave, the usual obligation is for the mortgagor to restore the security. But if he is insolvent, a unique device—sa'aya—shifts the burden to the slave, who benefits from freedom. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the calculation of the required work, and the special cases of tadbir and istilad, drawing on the Hanafi tradition from al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Guided Path
1
Classify the scenario by the master's wealth and the debt's status
If the master is wealthy, the mortgagee simply takes the debt (if due) or takes the slave's value as a substitute mortgage (if deferred). If he is insolvent, the slave must work (sa'aya). Identify whether the debt is due or deferred.
2
Calculate the amount for sa'aya using the two-thing rule
For a simple manumission by an insolvent master, the slave works for the lesser of two amounts: his current market value and the outstanding debt. This is derived by qiyas to a jointly owned slave manumitted by one insolvent co-owner.
Example: value=100, debt=80 → slave works for 80.
3
Refine the calculation using al-Jami' al-Saghir's three-thing criterion
According to al-Jami' al-Saghir, the slave works for the least of three things: (1) value on the day of manumission, (2) the amount guaranteed by the debt, and (3) the amount detained by the debt. This ensures precision when the guaranteed and detained amounts differ.
The commentary explains each: value because the slave detained that much from the mortgagee; guaranteed amount because the slave is liable to that extent; detained amount because sa'aya concerns the increase detained.
4
Distinguish tadbir and istilad
If the slave is a mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death) or an umm walad (mother of master's child), the slave works for the entire debt without the right of recourse against the master, because their earnings belong to the master.
Rationale: The master still owns their earnings, so they are not independent.
5
Understand the slave's right of recourse
After the slave works and pays, if the master later becomes wealthy, the slave can reclaim the amount from the master, because he was compelled to pay the master's debt and did not volunteer.
This is likened to the case of a person who lends to a mortgagor to redeem a mortgage.

Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah
For a due debt immediately, he takes his debt; for a deferred debt, its value is taken as a substitute mortgage until its term. If he did it while insolvent, in manumission the slave works for the lesser of his value and his debt.
For a due debt immediately, he takes his debt. For a deferred debt, 'its value as a mortgage substitute until its term': meaning he takes its value so that it becomes a mortgage in place of the mortgaged item until the time of the term's maturity. Its benefit appears if the value is of a different genus than the debt, such as if the value is dirhams and the debt is grain and he has no ability to pay the debt immediately, then the dirhams become a mortgage until the term.
[3] (And if he did it while insolvent, then in manumission the slave works for the lesser of his value and his debt) and the gift; due to lack of ability to deliver, and its completion is in al-Hidayah. And similar to manumission is endowment. In al-Is'af and others: if he endows the mortgaged item after its delivery, the judge forces him to pay what is due if he is wealthy; if he is insolvent, the judge annuls the endowment and sells it for what is due. This is in Radd al-Muhtar.
[1] His saying: 'For a due debt immediately...' etc.; meaning if the debt is due, the mortgagee demands the debtor after manumission for the debt if he is wealthy; because if he demands the mortgage, he can take it for his debt if it is of the same genus as his right, so it becomes payment and receipt, so there is no benefit.
[2] His saying: 'And for a deferred debt...' etc.; meaning if the debt is deferred, the value of the mortgage is taken from the manumitter, and it is made a mortgage in place of the mortgaged item if he is insolvent; because the cause of liability has been realized, and there is benefit in making liable, and in obtaining security from the aspect that he forgets, and he keeps it until the term's maturity. When it matures, he collects his debt if it is of the same genus; because the creditor can collect his right from the debtor's property if he finds his right's genus. And if there is surplus, he returns it because the mortgage's ruling ends with collection; if it is less than his genus, he claims the excess due to the absence of what would drop it. As in Takmilat al-Bahr.
[3] His saying: 'And if he did it while insolvent, then in manumission...' etc.; because the mortgagee's right was attached to it. And he returns to his master if he becomes wealthy; and in the two cases (tadbir and istilad) he works for the entire debt and has no recourse. And he returns to his master if he becomes wealthy; and in the two cases he works for the entire debt and has no recourse). For the mortgagor if he manumits while insolvent, if the debt is less than the value, the slave works for the debt; if the value is less, he works for the value; because he works (sa'a) because when the mortgagee cannot collect his right from the mortgagor, he takes it from the one who benefits from manumission, and the slave only benefits to the extent of his monetary worth. Then he returns what he worked for to the master if the master becomes wealthy; because he paid his debt, and he was compelled in it by legal ruling, so he returns to him what he bore for him. In tadbir and istilad, he works for the entire debt; because the earnings of the mudabbar and umm walad belong to the master, so they work for his entire debt, and there is no recourse. If recourse to the manumitter is impossible due to his insolvency, he returns to him because he is the one who benefits from this manumission. The apparent wording of the author (al-Musannif) is that he remains in the lesser of the two mentioned things, but what is transmitted elsewhere is that he works for the lesser of three things.
He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If the mortgagor manumits the mortgaged slave while insolvent, he looks at three things: the value on the day of manumission, what was guaranteed by the debt, and what was detained by it (the debt). He works for the lesser of these things. As for the value: because by manumission he has detained from the mortgagee's right this amount, so sa'aya is not incumbent upon him except for this amount, like a jointly owned slave if one of the co-owners manumits him while insolvent.
As for what is guaranteed by the debt if it is less: because the slave is guaranteed to the extent of the debt by manumission, and what arises from connected increase after taking possession is not guaranteed; because sa'aya is in respect of the increase, so if it is detained by the debt, it is not possible to impose the extent on the slave in respect of the increase. If the detained amount is less than the guaranteed amount and his value, he works to its extent. If you wish further clarification, refer to Takmilat al-Bahr.
[1] His saying: 'And he returns to his master...' etc.; its summary is that when the manumitted slave works and pays, he returns with the sa'aya to his master if he becomes wealthy; because he paid his debt and was compelled in it, and the slave was not a volunteer, so it is like the one who lent the mortgage.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from Husn al-Dirayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, expands on the ruling of a mortgagor manumitting a mortgaged slave while he is insolvent. The text is an expansion_probe, clarifying the original matn. The author first distinguishes two scenarios: if the debt is due immediately and the mortgagor is wealthy, the mortgagee simply takes his debt; no further action is needed. If the debt is deferred, the mortgagee takes the value of the slave as a substitute mortgage until the debt matures. The benefit of this appears when the value is of a different genus than the debt (e.g., dirhams for grain). If the mortgagor is insolvent at the time of manumission, the slave must work (s'aayah) to pay the lesser of his value or the debt. The reasoning is that the mortgagee's right was attached to the slave, and since the mortgagor cannot pay, the slave, who benefits from freedom, must compensate. The slave then has recourse against the master if the master later becomes wealthy. However, for tadbir (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and istilad (mother of the master's child), the slave works for the full debt without recourse because their earnings belong to the master. The passage also cites al-Jami' al-Saghir, which indicates the slave works for the lesser of three things: value at the time of manumission, the amount guaranteed by the debt, and the amount detained by the debt. The commentary explains each: value because the slave has detained that much from the mortgagee; guaranteed amount because the slave is liable to that extent; detained amount because the sa'aya is in respect of the increase detained. The ruling is mufta bihi and from Zahir al-Riwayah via al-Jami' al-Saghir. The passage emphasizes that the slave is not a volunteer but compelled, so he can recover from the master.

Key Terms
سعاية (sa'aya): The slave's effort to earn money to pay off a debt or compensation in exchange for freedom.
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is held as security for a debt.
معسر (mu'sir): Insolvent, unable to pay debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when an insolvent mortgagor manumits a mortgaged slave, the slave must work for the lesser of three things. Which of the following is NOT one of those three things?
A) The value of the slave on the day of manumission
B) The amount guaranteed by the debt
C) The amount detained by the debt
D) The original purchase price of the slave

Key Takeaway
When an insolvent master frees a mortgaged slave, the slave must compensate the mortgagee by working (sa'aya) for the least of three amounts: value, guaranteed amount, or detained amount.

Freeing a Pledged Slave by an Insolvent Pledgor: The Slave's Obligation and Recourse
Ruling with proof on sa'y of freed pledged slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pledged slave freed by an insolvent pledgor and explain the proof via analogy to a jointly-owned slave.

Core Idea
A freed slave works for the least of three claims to compensate the creditor.

This lesson examines the consequences when a pledgor (mortgagor) frees a pledged slave while insolvent. The slave must work (sa'y) to compensate the mortgagee for the loss of security. The amount is determined by balancing the slave's benefit from emancipation against the creditor's rights. We will trace the legal reasoning, including an analogy to a jointly-owned slave, and note an important intra-Hanafi difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and general ruling
The pledgor frees the pledged slave while insolvent (cannot pay the debt). The slave must work to offset the creditor's loss. If the debt is less than the slave's value, he works for the debt; if the value is less, he works for the value. He later recovers from the master if the master becomes wealthy.
The slave benefits from emancipation only to the extent of his value, so his obligation is limited.
2
Understand the proof: analogy to a jointly-owned slave
The ruling is derived by qiyas (analogy) to a jointly-owned slave freed by one insolvent co-owner. In that case, the freed slave works for the value of the freed portion. Similarly, the pledged slave works for the amount he deprived the creditor of (the value or debt, whichever is less).
The 'illa (effective cause) is that the slave benefits from emancipation while the creditor loses security.
3
Distinguish mudabbar and umm walad
For a mudabbar (slave promised freedom at master's death) and umm walad (slave mother of master's child), their earnings belong to the master. Therefore, they work for the entire debt and have no recourse against the master, because the master is the ultimate beneficiary.
This is because the master retains ownership of their earnings during his lifetime.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference: two vs. three factors
The base text (matn) implies the slave works for the lesser of two: debt and value. However, the relied-upon view from Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir is that three factors are considered: value at emancipation, amount secured by the debt, and amount for which the slave is detained. The slave works for the least of these three.
The third factor (detained amount) accounts for cases where the value or secured amount changes after possession.
5
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Suppose a slave is valued at 100, the debt is 80, and the amount detained (the original loan) is 70. The slave works for 70 (the least of the three). He then works to pay 70 to the creditor, and if the master later becomes wealthy, he can reclaim 70 from him.
This example illustrates the three-factor calculation from al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Book of Rahn: Its definition, conditions, what is valid with it, and disposition therein. ...and he has recourse against his master if he is wealthy, and in the case of the two sisters (mudabbar and umm walad) he works for the entire debt with no recourse. So if the pledgor frees (the slave) while insolvent: if the debt is less than the value, the slave works for the debt; if the value is less, he works for the value; because he works since the mortgagee cannot recover his right from the pledgor, so he takes it from one who benefits from the emancipation, and the slave only benefits to the extent of his value. Then he has recourse for what he worked against the master if the master becomes wealthy, because he paid off his debt while compelled by law, so he recovers from him what he bore. In the case of tadbir and istilad, he works for the entire debt because the earnings of the mudabbar and umm walad belong to the master, so they work for his entire debt with no recourse. If recourse against the freeer is impossible due to his insolvency, he has recourse against him (the original master) because he is the one who benefits from this emancipation. The apparent wording of the author is that he (the slave) remains within the lesser of the two mentioned things, but what is transmitted in other works is that he works for the lesser of three things. He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When the pledgor frees the pledged slave while insolvent, three things are considered: the value on the day of emancipation, what is secured by the debt, and what is detained (by the debt). He works for the least of these. As for the value, because through emancipation he deprived the mortgagee of this amount, so he is not obliged to work except for this amount, like a jointly-owned slave when one co-owner frees him while insolvent. As for what is secured by the debt if it is less, because the slave is secured to the extent of the debt by emancipation, and what arises from subsequent connected increase does not become secured; because the work (sa'y) concerns the increase, so if the detained (debt) is less than the secured amount and its value, he works to its extent. If you want further clarification, refer to Takmilat al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'and he has recourse against his master...' etc.; the summary is that when the freed slave works and pays, he has recourse for the work (sa'y) against his master if the master becomes wealthy, because he paid his debt while compelled, and the slave was not a volunteer, so it is like a loan of the pledge (i.e., the slave lent his work). Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the consequences of a pledgor freeing a pledged slave while insolvent. The author begins by stating the general rule: the slave works (saa) to pay off the debt or the value, whichever is less, and then can seek reimbursement from the master later. The reasoning is that the mortgagee cannot collect from the pledgor due to insolvency, so he collects from the slave who benefits from emancipation. The slave's benefit is limited to his value, so he only works up to that amount. For a mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and umm walad (slave mother of master's child), their earnings belong wholly to the master, so they work for the entire debt and have no recourse. The author then notes an intra-Hanafi difference: the matn (base text) implies the lesser of two things (debt and value), but the correct view from Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir is the lesser of three: value, secured amount, and detained amount. The commentator explains each: value because the slave deprives the mortgagee of that much; secured amount because the slave secures the debt; detained amount because the debt detains the slave. The analogy to a jointly-owned slave freed by one co-owner is used as proof. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the slave's recourse against the master is valid because he was compelled to pay under Shari'a, similar to a borrower of the pledge who pays the debt and then reclaims.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge or collateral: a property held as security for a debt in Hanafi law.
السعاية (al-sa'y): The work a freed slave performs to compensate the creditor for the debt secured by the slave.
المدبر والمستولدة (al-mudabbar wa al-mustawlada): A mudabbar is a slave who will become free upon the master's death; an umm walad is a female slave who becomes free upon the master's death because she bore his child.

Key Takeaway
When a pledged slave is freed by an insolvent pledgor, the slave must work for the least of three values to compensate the creditor, with recourse against the master if he later becomes wealthy.

Destruction and Borrowing of the Pledged Property
Rulings on destruction and borrowing of pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of three scenarios involving a pledge: destruction by pledgor, destruction by outsider, and borrowing with permission, and distinguish these from the slave working case.

Core Idea
Destruction or lending of a pledge shifts liability: pledgor pays, outsider compensates mortgagee, borrowing drops all liability.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents three key rulings on what happens when a pledged property (rahn) is destroyed or lent to a third party. Each scenario has a distinct legal consequence rooted in the mortgagee's right of possession and priority. The commentator also clarifies an important distinction from the case of a jointly-owned slave working for freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage covers: (1) pledgor destroys the pledge, (2) outsider destroys the pledge, (3) the pledge is borrowed with permission. Label each scenario clearly.
2
Determine the ruling for pledgor destruction
When the pledgor destroys the pledge, the ruling is analogous to freeing a slave while wealthy. If the debt is due, he must pay the debt immediately; if deferred, he must pay the value now, which becomes a new pledge.
This ensures the mortgagee's security is not lost by the pledgor's act.
3
Determine the ruling for outsider destruction
If an outsider destroys the pledge, the mortgagee is the proper claimant. The compensation becomes a new pledge held by the mortgagee until the debt is paid.
The mortgagee's right of possession gives him priority to claim.
4
Determine the ruling for borrowing with permission
If the mortgagee or pledgor lends the pledge to a third party with the other's permission, the lender's liability drops. If the borrower destroys it, no one is liable to each other. Both parties may reclaim the pledge and return it to the original arrangement.
Liability is based on possession; lending transfers possession without liability.
5
Note the distinction from the slave working case
The commentator contrasts this with a jointly-owned slave (mustas'a) where one partner frees his share while insolvent and the slave works to pay. There, the slave pays for his own freedom, not for another's liability, so the rulings differ.
This highlights the principle that payment for oneself vs. for another changes the legal outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(1) ينظر: تكملة البحر» (ص ٣٠٤).

And his destroying his pledge is like his freeing it while wealthy. And if an outsider destroys it, the mortgagee holds him liable for its value, and that becomes a pledge with him. And a pledge that the mortgagee or the pledgor borrows (i.e., lends to a third party) with the other's permission: its liability drops, and if it perishes with the borrower, it perishes without anything (no liability). Each of them (mortgagee and pledgor) may return it as a pledge. If the pledgor dies before returning it, the mortgagee has priority over creditors. (And his destroying his pledge is like his freeing it while wealthy): meaning if the pledgor destroys the pledge, it is as if he freed it while wealthy: i.e., if the debt is due, the debt is taken from him; if it is deferred, the value is taken to be a pledge until the term arrives. 'And an outsider destroys it, the mortgagee holds him liable, and that' (i.e., the compensation) 'becomes a pledge with him.' 'And a pledge that the mortgagee or the pledgor borrows' with the other's permission, its liability drops, and it perishes without anything. Each may return it as a pledge. If the pledgor dies before returning, it perishes with its borrower... This differs from the case of a slave working (mustas'a) when he is between two partners and one frees his share while insolvent, and he works for the other share and pays but without recourse, because he pays a guaranteed obligation due on him, as he works to complete the emancipation according to them, and to achieve emancipation according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). Here, he works for a liability on someone else after his complete emancipation, so they differ. [1] His statement: 'And an outsider destroys...' etc.: meaning if an outsider destroys it, the mortgagee guarantees its value, and the compensation becomes a pledge with him, i.e., the mortgagee is the plaintiff in requiring its value, so it becomes a pledge with him because he is more entitled to the physical pledge while it exists, similarly in reclaiming what has taken its place. What is obligatory for this destroyed item is its value on the day it perished due to destruction, unlike the mortgagee's liability, and its explanation has preceded. [2] His statement: 'And a pledge borrowed...' there is leniency, because lending (i'arah) is the ownership of benefits without compensation, and the mortgagee does not own the benefits, so how can he make another own them? However, since it is treated like a loan in terms of no liability and the ability of the lender to reclaim, it is called a loan. This is what my brother Chalabi stated in his gloss. [3] His statement: 'Its liability drops' from the mortgagee, because liability is based on possession, and it has ceased. [4] His statement: 'And each of them' i.e., the pledgor and mortgagee may return the borrowed item, for the contract of pledge remains (as per references).
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings regarding the disposition of a pledged property (rahn). First, if the pledgor (raahin) destroys the pledge, it is treated exactly as if he had freed a slave (who is the pledge) while wealthy. That means if the debt is due, the debtor (pledgor) must immediately pay off the debt; if the debt is deferred, he must pay the value of the pledge now, which then becomes a new pledge held by the mortgagee until the original debt's term expires. Second, if a third party (ajnabi) destroys the pledge, the mortgagee (murtahin) is the one who claims compensation from the outsider, and that compensation becomes a new pledge in place of the destroyed property. Third, if either the mortgagee or the pledgor lends the pledge to someone else with the other's permission, the lender's liability (daman) for the pledge drops, meaning if the borrower destroys it, neither the mortgagee nor the pledgor is liable to each other. Both the pledgor and mortgagee have the right to reclaim the pledge from the borrower and return it to the original pledge arrangement. If the pledgor dies before returning it, the mortgagee has priority over other creditors regarding that pledge. The commentator clarifies a nuance: this is different from the case of a slave working (mustas'a) who is jointly owned and partially freed, because there the slave's payment is for his own freedom, whereas here the compensation is for a liability on another.

Key Terms
الإتلاف (al-itlaf): Destruction of the pledged property by the pledgor or an outsider.
الإعارة (al-i'arah): Lending the pledged property to a third party with the permission of both parties (pledgor and mortgagee).
الضمان (al-daman): Liability for the pledged property; it drops when borrowed with permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The borrower (if borrowed)

Key Takeaway
Destruction or lending of a pledge shifts liability: pledgor pays his debt, outsider compensates mortgagee, and borrowing with permission drops all liability because possession ends.

Destruction of Pawn by Pledgor or Third Party
Ruling with proof by analogy on pawn destruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the analogy between destroying a pawn and freeing a slave while wealthy to determine the ruling when a pledgor or third party destroys the pawn.

Core Idea
Destroying the pawn does not cancel the debt; it substitutes the value as a new pawn.

This passage presents a ruling with proof by analogy (qiyas). It compares the pledgor destroying the pawn to a wealthy person freeing a slave, and then handles the case of a third party destroying it. Understanding this analogy reveals the Hanafi principle that liability attaches to the value, and the mortgagee's right continues through the substitute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Analogy
The text states that if the pledgor destroys the pawn, it is like freeing a slave while wealthy. Identify the two scenarios being compared.
The analogy is explicitly drawn in the opening phrase.
2
Understand the Analogous Case (Freeing a Slave While Wealthy)
In Hanafi law, if a wealthy person frees a slave, the slave is free but the owner must pay the slave's value. This establishes the principle of substituting value when a right is destroyed by someone capable of paying.
The key is the actor's capability to pay and the property being subject to another's right.
3
Apply the Illa (Effective Cause)
The illa is that both involve destroying property that is subject to another's right while the actor is capable of paying. Confirm that the illa applies to the pawn case.
The pledgor has the ability to pay the debt or value.
4
Derive the Ruling for the Pledgor
Apply the analogy: if the debt is due, the debt is taken from the pledgor (like taking the slave's value). If the debt is deferred, the pawn's value is taken and becomes a new pawn until the term expires.
Note the two sub-scenarios based on whether the debt is current or deferred.
5
Analyze the Third Party Case
If a third party destroys the pawn, the mortgagee guarantees the value from the third party, and that guarantee itself becomes a new pawn. This is different from the pledgor case because the liability shifts to the third party.
The mortgagee is the proper plaintiff to claim compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإتلافه رهنه كإعتاقه غنيّاً: أي إن أتلف الراهن الرهن فكما أعتقه غنياً؛ أي إن كان الدين حالاً أخذ منه الدين، وإن كان مؤجّلاً أخذ قيمته؛ ليكون رهناً إلى زمان حلول الأجل. وأجنبي أتلفه ضمنه مرتهنه وكان: أي الضمان رهناً معه.
And his destroying his pawn is like his freeing a slave while wealthy: i.e., if the pledgor destroys the pawn, it is as if he freed a slave while wealthy; i.e., if the debt is due immediately, the debt is taken from him, and if it is deferred, its value is taken to be a pawn until the term expires. And if a third party destroys it, the mortgagee guarantees it, and the guarantee becomes a pawn with it.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof by analogy. It first states that if the pledgor (raahin) destroys the pawn (rahn), the ruling is like that of a wealthy person freeing a slave: if the debt is due immediately, the creditor takes the debt from the pledgor; if deferred, the value of the pawn is taken and held as a pawn until the debt matures. This is because the pledgor's destruction is treated as an admission of the debt, similar to freeing a slave while being wealthy (ghani) which makes the slave free but the value is taken. Second, if a third party (ajnabi) destroys the pawn, the mortgagee (murtahin) guarantees the value (i.e., the third party pays the value to the mortgagee), and that guaranteed amount becomes a new pawn in place of the destroyed one. The mortgagee has the right to claim compensation because he has the primary right to the pawn's value. This follows the Hanafi position that the mortgagee is the proper plaintiff in such cases.

Key Terms
إتلاف (itlāf): Destruction of property by an act that causes total loss
غنيّاً (ghaniyyan): Wealthy person who has sufficient funds to pay the value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pledgor destroys the pawn while the debt is deferred, what is the ruling according to the text?
A) The debt becomes immediately due and must be paid.
B) The value of the pawn is taken and held as a pawn until the term expires.
C) The pawn is considered released and no liability.
D) The pledgor must pay double the value.

Key Takeaway
Destroying the pawn does not eliminate the security; the value substitutes for the pawn, preserving the mortgagee's rights.

Lending the Pawn: Liability Drops but Contract Persists
Ruling on lending a pawn and its effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a pawn is lent with permission, explain the reasoning (qiyas to i'arah), and apply the rule to determine the mortgagee's priority upon the pledgor's death.

Core Idea
Lending a pawn with permission removes liability for its destruction, but does not invalidate the pawn contract.

This lesson examines a specific scenario within pawn (rahn) transactions: what happens when the mortgagee permits the pawn to be used by the pledgor or another? The text rules that the guarantee of the pawn drops, yet the pawn obligation remains. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying pawn laws.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The ruling involves a pawn (rahn) that is lent (i'arah) by the mortgagee to the pledgor, or by one of them with the other's permission.
The permission is key; without it, the ruling differs.
2
State the ruling on liability
The text says 'its guarantee drops' (saqaṭa ḍamānuh). If the pawn is destroyed in the borrower's hands, it is lost without compensation.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
The basis is qiyas to i'arah (lending): the mortgagee's right of possession that created guarantee is removed by lending.
The guarantee is tied to possession; lending transfers possession without liability.
4
Note the right to reclaim as pawn
Either party may demand the return of the pawn as a pawn, preserving the original contract.
This shows the pawn contract remains intact.
5
Apply the rule to death scenario
If the pledgor dies before returning the lent pawn, the mortgagee has priority over other creditors.
The mortgagee's right persists despite the lending.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورهَن أعارَهُ مرتهنه راهنه أو أحدهما بأذن صاحبه آخر سقط ضمانه، فهلكه مع مستعيره هلك بلا شيء، ولكل منهما أن يرده رهناً، فإن مات الراهن قبل رده فالمرتهن أحق به من الغرماء
And a pawn which the mortgagee lent to the pledgor, or one of them lent with the permission of the other, its guarantee drops, so if it is destroyed with the borrower, it is destroyed without anything (i.e., no liability). And each of them may return it as a pawn; if the pledgor dies before returning it, the mortgagee has more right to it than the (other) creditors.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal effects of lending a pawn (rahn). If the mortgagee (murtahin) lends the pawn to the pledgor (raahin), or if one of them lends it to the other with his permission, the guarantee (daman) that existed due to the pawn being in the possession of the mortgagee drops. This means that if the pawn is destroyed while in the borrower's hands, no compensation is due from anyone—it is lost without liability. However, each party (the pledgor and the mortgagee) has the right to demand the return of the pawn as a pawn (i.e., to restore the pawn status). If the pledgor dies before returning it, the mortgagee has priority over other creditors in taking that pawn to satisfy his debt. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the lending (i'arah) removes the mortgagee's possession that gave rise to guarantee, but the pawn contract (rahn) remains valid. The lender's right to reclaim it as pawn is because the pawn contract persists. The marginal note clarifies that the term 'i'arah (lending) is used loosely; in actuality, the mortgagee does not own the usufruct, but the ruling treats it as a loan in terms of no liability and the right to reclaim.

Key Terms
إعارة (i'ārah): Lending of property for use without compensation
سقط ضمانه (saqaṭa ḍamānuh): The liability for destruction drops off

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pledgor dies before returning the lent pawn, who has priority?
A) All creditors equally.
B) The mortgagee (creditor) over other creditors.
C) The heirs of the pledgor.
D) The borrower who received the pawn.

Key Takeaway
Lending a pawn with permission removes liability for destruction but does not dissolve the pawn; the mortgagee retains priority upon the pledgor's death.

Mortgagee Liability for Pawn Use with Permission
Ruling on liability when pawn is used with permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mortgagee liability when a pawn is used with permission and identify the critical factor of timing.

Core Idea
Timing of destruction determines liability: before or after work, liable; during work, not liable.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in pawn (rahn) law: when the mortgagee (murtahin) either permits someone else to use the pawn or borrows the pawn himself for work, the timing of destruction determines liability. This distinction reflects the Hanafi principle that liability attaches to possession (yad) unless the loss occurs as an incidental part of a permitted activity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text covers two situations: (1) mortgagee permits another to use the pawn, (2) mortgagee borrows the pawn from the pledgor for work. Both are treated identically.
The permission (idhn) and borrowing (i'arah) both give the mortgagee control over the pawn.
2
Determine the timing of destruction
The ruling hinges on when the pawn is destroyed: before the work begins, after it ends, or during the actual work.
The key phrase is 'حال عمله' (during his work).
3
Apply liability rule
If destruction occurs before or after the work, the mortgagee is liable (daman) and must compensate. If during the work, no liability arises.
Liability is like that of a regular pawn (ka al-rahn) — meaning the pawn itself is not lost, but the mortgagee's responsibility is tied to possession.
4
Understand the rationale
During the work, the destruction is incidental to the permitted act, so liability is suspended. Before or after, the mortgagee's possession is not actively engaged in the permitted purpose, so he bears risk.
This is an application of the maxim: liability follows possession unless the loss is inherent to the permitted use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومرتهن أذن باستعمال رهنه أو استعاره من راهنِهِ لعمل، إن هلك قبلَ عمله أو بعده ضَمِنَ كالرّهن، ولو هلك حال عمله لا
And a mortgagee who permitted the use of his pawn, or borrowed it from his pledgor for work: if it is destroyed before his work or after it, he is liable like the pawn; but if it is destroyed during his work, he is not liable.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on two scenarios: first, when the mortgagee (murtahin) gives permission to someone else to use the pawn; second, when the mortgagee borrows the pawn from the pledgor (raahin) for work. In both cases, the ruling is the same: if the pawn is destroyed before the work begins or after it is completed, the mortgagee is liable (daman) and must pay compensation, as the pawn was in his control during those times. However, if it is destroyed during the actual performance of the work, the mortgagee is not liable because the destruction occurred in the course of an activity that was permitted and not under the mortgagee's control. The marginal note clarifies that 'permission' (idhn) is different from 'borrowing' (i'arah), but they share the same ruling on liability. The reasoning is that liability attaches when the destruction is not incidental to the permitted activity.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or destruction
حال عمله (ḥāli 'amalih): During the actual performance of the work for which the pawn was borrowed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the mortgagee borrows the pawn for work and it is destroyed during the work, what is the ruling?
A) The mortgagee must pay full compensation.
B) The mortgagee is not liable.
C) The pledgor must pay.
D) The mortgagee and pledgor share liability.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's liability for a pawn used with permission depends entirely on timing: liable before or after work, but not during work.

Borrowing Something to Pawn It
Ruling on borrowing an object specifically to pawn it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on borrowing an object for the purpose of pawning it and identify the scope of the borrower's authority.

Core Idea
Borrowing an object to pawn it is valid; the borrower may pawn it for any amount.

This short passage from a Hanafi legal text addresses a practical question: may someone borrow an item specifically to use it as collateral for a loan? The answer affirms the validity of this arrangement and grants the borrower wide discretion in setting the pawn's value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm
Recognize that the passage rules on the validity (sahih) of borrowing an item with the intention of pawning it. This is a form of isti'arah (borrowing for use).
2
Determine the borrower's authority
The text states the borrower 'may pawn it for whatever he wishes', indicating no restriction on the pawn amount relative to the debt or other factors.
This reflects the Hanafi view that the pawn contract does not require value equivalence.
3
Recognize implicit permission
By lending the object for pawning, the lender consents to the borrower's use of the item as collateral, including the amount set.
The lender's consent is a condition for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is valid to borrow something to pawn it; he may pawn it for whatever he wishes.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: it is legally valid (sahih) to borrow an object (i'arah) with the intention of using it as a pawn (rahn) for a loan. The borrower may then pawn it for any amount he desires, whether small or large, because the pawn contract does not require the pawn's value to equal the debt. The marginal note explains that the borrower may pawn it for a little or much, as payment.

Key Terms

Rulings on Mortgagee's Use of Pledge and Borrowing to Pledge
Rulings on mortgagee's use of pledge and borrowing to pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the liability of the mortgagee when using the pledged property (before, during, after work) and apply the rules for borrowing property to pledge it, including the effect of restrictions.

Core Idea
Mortgagee's liability for loss depends on timing: not liable during permitted work, liable otherwise.

This lesson examines two interconnected scenarios in Hanafi fiqh: first, the mortgagee's use of the pledged property with permission or through a borrowing contract, where liability hinges on when the loss occurs. Second, the borrowing of an item specifically to pledge it, where the scope of permission and compliance with restrictions determines liability and debt satisfaction. Understanding these rulings is essential for correctly structuring rahn transactions and avoiding unjust enrichment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage covers two distinct cases: (1) mortgagee using/borrowing the pledge, and (2) borrowing an item to pledge it. Note the common theme of liability for loss.
The commentary splits the first scenario into two sub-cases: permission vs. borrowing, but the ruling is unified.
2
Analyze liability for loss in the first scenario
Focus on the three time points: before work, during work, after work. The text rules: liable if before or after, not liable if during the permitted work.
The rationale: during work, the use is in progress; the risk is on the owner by nature of the work.
3
Examine borrowing to pledge: absolute vs. restricted permission
If the lender gives absolute permission, the borrower may pledge for any amount/type/location. If restricted, the borrower must adhere; violation triggers liability.
Restrictions can be on amount, type of pledge, mortgagee, or town.
4
Trace the liability chain when restrictions are violated
The lender can choose to hold the borrower liable (which perfects the pledge as the borrower owns the property by paying the value) or hold the mortgagee liable (mortgagee recovers from the pledgor).
The pledge contract is completed only if the borrower is held liable; otherwise, it is invalid?
5
Determine debt satisfaction when borrower complies
If the property perishes with the mortgagee, the debt is satisfied if its value equals or exceeds the debt; if less, only part is satisfied. The borrower owes the lender the value up to the debt amount, not the full value.
The borrower becomes a 'payer' of the debt with his property, so he can recover from the pledgor the amount paid.

Book of Rahn / Its definition, conditions, what is valid by it, and disposal in it. Before repayment, the mortgagee has more right to it than creditors; because the ruling of rahn remains in it, because the hand of 'ariyah (loan) is not binding, and its being non-guaranteed does not indicate that it is not mortgaged. [1] His saying: 'And the mortgagee... to his saying: 'no' includes two issues: first: the mortgagee being permitted by the pledgor to use the pledge without a contract of borrowing between them. Second: his being a borrower of the pledge from him; because they are unified in ruling, which is that the mortgagee is liable if it perishes before and after the work, and not liable if it perishes during the work. It is permissible to differentiate between them: permission is a permissibility, so it does not establish the permissibility of lending, and permission is for another person, unlike borrowing, because it necessitates permissibility of lending and permission for others who are not differentiated, so reflect. [2] His saying: 'so he pledges with whatever he wishes' etc.; meaning the borrower pledges with whatever he wishes, little or much, if absolute. If restricted, he is bound by what is specified of amount, type, mortgagee, and town. If he violates, the lender guarantees his borrower, and his pledge is completed between him and his mortgagee, or him (the mortgagee), and he (the lender) returns with what he guaranteed and his debt against his pledgor. If he complies and it perishes with the mortgagee, then he has taken all his debt if its value is like the debt or more, and he guarantees the borrower the amount of debt paid from it, not the value; or part of his debt if it is less, and the remainder of his debt is against his pledgor. [1] His saying: 'And if restricted'; i.e., the lender restricted the type between him; i.e., between the borrower who is the pledgor. (2) His saying: 'Then if he violates...' etc.; i.e., if the borrower violates, he is liable. Then if the lender wishes, he guarantees the borrower; and if he wishes, he guarantees the mortgagee. If he guarantees the borrower, the pledge contract is completed because he owns it by paying the guarantee, so it becomes clear that he pledged his own property. And if he guarantees the mortgagee, the mortgagee returns with what he guaranteed and the debt against the pledgor, as explained in the case of entitlement of the pledge before. His saying: 'Then if he complies...' etc.; that he pledges with the amount he commanded, and it perishes with the mortgagee. If its value is like the debt or more, the mortgagee's debt against the pledgor is dropped due to complete satisfaction by perishing, and the equivalent is owed to the owner against the pledgor; because he became a payer of his debt with his property to this extent, which necessitates recourse, not by taking possession itself because it is with his consent. And if its value is less than the pledge, it goes... (1) i.e., he guarantees the mortgagee because he also is a wrongdoer, so the pledgor becomes like a usurper and the mortgagee like a usurper's usurper. See: 'Durar al-Hukkam' p.258. (2) that he pledged with the amount he commanded; see 'Durar al-Hukkam' p.258. (3) See: 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba' p.599. (4) See: 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba' p.599.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings from a Hanafi fiqh commentary on the topic of rahn (pawn/pledge). It begins by affirming the mortgagee's priority over the pledged property before repayment, reasoning that the ruling of rahn persists even if the property is in the hands of a borrower (عاري) because the borrower's possession is not binding—thus the property remains subject to the mortgage. The passage then addresses a scenario where the mortgagee is permitted to use the pledged property or borrows it from the pledgor for work. The ruling distinguishes three moments of loss: if the property perishes before the work begins or after it is completed, the mortgagee is liable (guarantees), similar to any other borrower; but if it perishes during the work itself, he is not liable. This is because during the work, the property is in use with permission, and the risk is considered part of the work's nature. The next ruling validates borrowing an item specifically for the purpose of pawning it. The borrower may then pledge it for whatever amount or duration he wishes if the lender gives absolute permission. However, if the lender restricts the amount, type of pledge, mortgagee, or location, the borrower must comply. Violating these restrictions makes the borrower and/or mortgagee liable. The passage details the consequences: if the borrower violates, the lender can either hold the borrower liable (which perfects the pawn as the borrower then owns the property by paying the guarantee) or hold the mortgagee liable (in which case the mortgagee recovers from the pledgor). If the borrower complies with restrictions and the property perishes with the mortgagee, the debt is considered satisfied if the property's value equals or exceeds the debt; if less, only part is satisfied. The footnotes reference key Hanafi sources like 'Majma' al-Anhur', 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba', and 'Durar al-Hukkam', indicating the authority of these rulings within the school.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is held as security for a debt; the property remains with the creditor (mortgagee) until debt repayment.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor who receives the pledged property as security; the mortgagee.
عارية (ʿāriyah): A loan of property for use, where the borrower is not liable for accidental loss unless it is a loan of fungibles or with conditions. In this text, the mortgagee borrows the pledged property as a loan for use (عارية) from the pledgor.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee; in this context, the obligation to compensate if the property perishes while in one's possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, he is liable regardless of when it perishes.
B) No, he is not liable because the loss occurred during the permitted work.
C) Only if he exceeded the terms of use.
D) Only if the work was for his own benefit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mortgagee's liability for loss of the pledged property during a permitted use is suspended only if the loss occurs while the work is in progress; otherwise, he is liable.

Rulings on Pledging a Borrowed Item with Conditions
Rulings on liability when a borrowed item is pledged with owner's conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings when a borrower violates or complies with conditions set by the owner for pledging a borrowed item, and calculate the liability in different scenarios.

Core Idea
Violating conditions voids the owner's permission; the borrower becomes liable for the debt satisfied, not the market value.

This passage from the Hanafi school addresses a specific transaction combining borrowing (ariyah) and pledging (rahn). The owner may allow the borrower to pledge the item but with restrictions. Understanding the consequences of compliance or violation is crucial for determining liability when the pledge is lost. We will break down the rules into clear scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions and the violation
The owner specifies amount, type, mortgagee, and city. If the borrower violates any, the owner can hold the borrower or the mortgagee liable.
2
Analyse the scenario of compliance
If the borrower complies and the pledge perishes with the mortgagee, the borrower's liability is limited to the debt satisfied, not the value.
3
Apply the value-debt comparison
The text provides three numerical examples: value equal to debt, value more than debt, value less than debt. Determine what the mortgagee recovers and what the borrower owes the owner.
4
Consider the owner's redemption right
The owner can redeem the pledge by paying the debt, even without the pledgor's consent, and reclaim from the pledgor.

وإن قيد تقيد بما عين من قدر وجنس ومرتهن وبلد، فإن خالف ضمّن المعير مستعيره، ويتم رهنه بينه وبين مرتهنه، أو إياه، ورجع هو بما ضمّن وبدينه على راهنه، فإن وافق وهلك مع مرتهنه فقد أخذ كل دينه إن كانت قيمته مثل الدين أو أكثر، وضمن مستعيره قدر دين أوفاه منه لا القيمة، أو بعض دينه إن كانت أقل وباقي دينه على راهنه. فإن كانت قيمته عشرة والدين عشرة، فقد أخذ المرتهن كل الدين، ويضمن المستعير الدين الذي أوفاه وهو عشرة للمعير، وإن كانت قيمته خمسة عشر والدين عشرة، فقد أخذ المرتهن كل الدين، فيضمن المستعير الدين الذي أوفاه: أي العشرة، ولا يضمن القيمة؛ لأنه قد وافق فليس بمتعد، وإن كانت القيمة عشرة والدين خمسة عشر فقد أخذ المرتهن بعض الدين وهو عشرة، وباقي الدين على الراهن، ويضمن المستعير قدر ما أوفاه من الدين وهو العشرة. ولا يمتنع المرتهن إذا قضى المعير دينه، وفك رهنه، ويرجع على الراهن بما أدى، فلو هلك مع الراهن قبل رهنه أو بعد فكه لا يضمن.
And if he (the owner) restricted (the borrowing) he is bound by what was specified of amount, type, mortgagee, and city. So if he (the borrower) violates, the owner holds the borrower liable, and his pledge is completed between him and his mortgagee, or him. And he (the owner) reclaims what he was held liable for and his debt from the pledgor. If he (the borrower) complies and it perishes with the mortgagee, he has taken all his debt if its value equals the debt or more, and the borrower is liable for the amount of debt he fulfilled from it, not the value, or part of his debt if it (the value) is less, and the remainder of his debt is against the pledgor. So if its value is ten and the debt is ten, the mortgagee has taken the entire debt, and the borrower is liable for the debt he fulfilled, which is ten, to the owner. If its value is fifteen and the debt is ten, the mortgagee has taken the entire debt, so the borrower is liable for the debt he fulfilled, i.e., the ten, and he is not liable for the value because he complied and was not a transgressor. If the value is ten and the debt is fifteen, the mortgagee has taken part of the debt, which is ten, and the remainder of the debt is against the pledgor, and the borrower is liable for the amount he fulfilled of the debt, which is the ten. And the mortgagee is not prevented if the owner pays his debt and redeems his pledge, and he (the owner) reclaims from the pledgor what he paid. So if it (the pledge) perishes with the pledgor before his pledge or after its release, he (the owner) is not liable.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the Hanafi school regarding the case where a person borrows an item (عارية) and then, with the owner's permission, pledges it as collateral for a debt, but the owner imposes specific conditions on the pledge, such as the amount of debt for which it can be pledged, the type of debt, the identity of the mortgagee (lender), or the city where the pledge can be made. If the borrower (المستعير) violates any of these conditions, the owner (المعير) has the option to hold either the borrower or the mortgagee (المرتهن) liable for compensation. If the owner holds the borrower liable, the pledge contract is perfected between the borrower and the mortgagee, meaning the borrower effectively becomes the owner of the pledge at the moment of liability (by paying compensation) and it is revealed that his own property was pledged. If the owner holds the mortgagee liable, the mortgagee can reclaim from the pledgor (الراهن) both what he paid as compensation and his original debt, as in the case of a prior claim on the pledge. If the borrower complies with the conditions and the pledge perishes in the hands of the mortgagee, then the amount of debt satisfied depends on the value of the pledge relative to the debt. If the value equals or exceeds the debt, the mortgagee's entire debt is considered collected through the destruction of the pledge, and the borrower owes the owner the amount of debt satisfied (not the market value). If the value is less than the debt, only that portion is discharged, and the remainder remains against the pledgor. The passage then addresses the owner's right to redeem his property by paying off the debt, even without the pledgor's consent; the mortgagee cannot refuse because the owner is not a volunteer (متبرع) but acts to free his own property. In such a case, the owner can recover from the pledgor what he paid. However, if the pledged item perishes while still in the possession of the pledgor (after redemption or before the pledge), the owner bears no liability.

Key Terms
المعير (al-mu'īr): The owner who lends his property (عارية) to the borrower.
المستعير (al-musta'īr): The borrower who takes the item on loan and then pledges it.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The person who receives the pledged item as collateral for a debt.
الراهن (al-rāhin): The pledgor; in this context, the borrower who hands over the borrowed item as pledge.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage; here, the owner holds the borrower liable for violating conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the borrower (musta'ir) complies with the owner's conditions and the pledged item perishes with the mortgagee, and the value of the item is more than the debt, what is the borrower liable for?
A) The market value of the item.
B) The amount of debt satisfied (not the value).
C) Half of the debt.
D) Nothing, because he complied.

Key Takeaway
The borrower's liability when complying is limited to the debt satisfied, not the market value, because he is not a transgressor.

Pledgee Must Release Pledge When Lender Pays Debt
Ruling with rational proof (istihsan) on pledge redemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the pledgee must release the pledged property when the lender pays the debt, and explain the rationale that the lender is not a volunteer (ghayr mutabarri').

Core Idea
The pledgee must release the pledge when the lender pays because the lender reclaims his own property.

This lesson focuses on a specific scenario in the law of pledge (rahn): the original lender (al-mu'ir) pays the debt to redeem the pledged property. The Hanafi school obliges the pledgee to release the pledge in this case, based on the principle that the lender is not acting as a volunteer but is reclaiming his own property. We will examine the ruling, the rationale, and contrast it with the case of a stranger who pays the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the scenario
Recognize the four parties: the pledgor (rahin, debtor), pledgee (murtahin, creditor), lender (mu'ir, original owner of the pledged item), and borrower (musta'ir, who borrowed from the lender and then pledged it). The scenario: the lender pays the debt to redeem the pledge.
2
State the ruling
The text states: the pledgee is not prevented from taking (i.e., cannot refuse) when the lender pays the debt and redeems the pledge. The pledgee must release the pledged property. This is wajib (obligatory) upon the pledgee.
The ruling is from al-Hidayah, a core text of the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the rationale: the lender is not a volunteer
The commentary explains that the lender is 'ghayr mutabarri' (not a volunteer) because he is striving to free his own property. He has a right and ownership in the pledged property, so the pledgee cannot refuse his payment.
Contrast this with a stranger (ajnabi) who pays the debt – the stranger is a volunteer, and the pledgee may refuse his payment.
4
Analyze the numerical examples
The text gives three examples of the relationship between the value of the pledged item and the debt. In all cases, the borrower (musta'ir) guarantees only the amount he paid (the debt), not the full value, because he acted with consent and is not a transgressor.
The borrower's liability is limited to what he actually paid to satisfy the debt.
5
Apply the distinction to a hypothetical
Now contrast with a stranger paying the debt. Based on the rationale, if a stranger pays, the pledgee may refuse because the stranger is a volunteer. The lender's special status (owner) is the key distinguishing factor.
This distinction arises from istihsan (juristic preference), not from strict analogy (qiyas).

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the rights and obligations of the pledgee, pledgor, and lender when a pledged item is redeemed. The main text states that when the original lender (المعير) pays off the debt and redeems the pledge, the pledgee (المرتهن) cannot refuse to release the pledged property. The lender then has the right to claim reimbursement from the pledgor (الراهن) for what he paid. If the pledged property perishes while in the possession of the pledgor—either before the pledge was made or after redemption—the lender does not incur any liability for the loss. The text then gives three numerical examples to clarify the calculation: when the value of the pledged item equals the debt, when it exceeds the debt, and when it is less. In each case, the borrower (المستعير) who returned the item guarantees only the amount he paid to satisfy the debt, not the full value, because he acted with consent. The commentary expands on this, emphasizing that the pledgee is forced to hand over the pledged property because the lender is not a volunteer (غير متبرع); he is seeking to free his own property. This contrasts with a stranger who repays the debt, as the stranger is a volunteer and the creditor may refuse his payment. The commentator cites al-Hidayah as authority. The second commentary note clarifies that the borrower does not become a repayer by the mere fact of the item's destruction, so no liability arises.

Key Terms
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The pledgee; the creditor who holds the pledged property as security for the debt.
المعير (al-mu'ir): The lender; the person who gave the borrowed item to be used as pledge.
الراهن (al-rahin): The pledgor; the debtor who gives the pledge as security.
المستعير (al-musta'ir): The borrower; the person who borrowed the item and then returned it to the lender for pledge.
غير متبرع (ghayr mutabarri'): Not a volunteer; one who acts to protect his own interest, not out of charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The pledgee may keep the pledge until the lender proves ownership.

Key Takeaway
The pledgee must release the pledged property when the lender pays the debt, as the lender is reclaiming his own property and is not a volunteer.
