A) The mortgagee automatically takes possession.
B) The executor sells the pledge and pays the debt.
C) The pledge is returned to the pledgor's heirs.
D) The debt is canceled.

Key Takeaway
The executor must sell the pledge upon the pledgor's death to pay the debt; the mortgagee does not gain ownership automatically.

Appointing an Executor to Sell the Pawn
Ruling on appointing executor to sell pawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the judge appointing an executor to sell a pawn and explain the prohibition of preferential treatment of creditors using qiyas.

Core Idea
The judge must appoint an executor to sell the pawn; the executor cannot favor one creditor over others.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah expands on a ruling from al-Hidāyah regarding the appointment of an executor when a pawner dies without one. The author clarifies the judge's duty and then examines a critical sub-issue: an executor pawning part of the estate to one creditor. Understanding this ruling is essential for ensuring fair distribution among creditors and preventing injustice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Determine the core ruling: when a deceased pawner has no executor, the judge appoints an executor to sell the pawn to satisfy debts.
The judge acts as a supervisor for Muslims' rights.
2
Analyze the role of the judge
Understand that the judge appoints ex officio because the deceased is unable to appoint, and the debt must be paid.
The appointment is wajib (obligatory) as a means to fulfill the debt.
3
Examine the sub-issue of preferential treatment
Study the problem: if the executor pawns part of the estate to one creditor among several, the pawn is invalid and other creditors can reject it.
This is because it constitutes īthār (preferential treatment) through constructive payment.
4
Trace the qiyas analogy
The author uses qiyas: the executor's preferential treatment resembles the deceased's own acts during terminal illness, which are also invalid.
The common factor (jāmi') is nullifying the rights of other creditors.
5
Note the exceptions
If the executor pays the creditors before they reject, it becomes valid. If there is only one creditor, the pawn is valid.
These exceptions remove the obstacle of injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم يكن له وصى نُصب وصي يبيعه
[١] قوله: نصب وصي... الخ ؛ أي نصب القاضي له وصياً يبيعُ الرهن ؛ لأنّ القاضي نُصبَ ناظراً لحقوق المسلمين إذا عجزوا عن النظر لأنفسهم، وقد تعيَّن النظر في نصب الوصي ؛ ليؤدي ما عليه لغيره، ويستوفي حقوقه من غيره، ولو كان على الميت ديـن فـرهن الوصي بعض التركة عند غريم له من غرمائه لم يجز، وللآخرين أن يردّوه ؛ لأنه إيثار لبعض الغرماء بالإيفاء الحكمي ، فأشبه الإيثار بالإيفاء الحقيقي، والجامع ما في كل واحد منهما من إبطال حق غيره من الغرماء.
ألا ترى أن الميت بنفسه لا يملك ذلك بمرض موته ، فكذا من قام مقامه وإن قضى دينهم قبل أن يردّوه جاز ؛ لزوال المانع ، ووصول حقهم، ولو لم يكن للميت غريم آخر جاز الرهن اعتباراً بالإيفاء الحقيقي، وبيع في دينه ما لا يباع فيه قبل الرهن، فكذا بعده وإذا ارتهن الوصي بدين للميت على رجل جاز ؛ لأنه استيفاء، فيملكه وله أن يبيعه. هذا تلخيص ما في «الهداية»(۱).
If he does not have an executor, an executor is appointed to sell it. [1] His statement: 'appoint an executor...' etc.; meaning the judge appoints for him an executor to sell the pawn; because the judge is appointed as a supervisor for the rights of Muslims when they are unable to look after themselves. And the consideration in appointing an executor becomes determined so that he may fulfill what is due from him and claim his rights from others. If the deceased had a debt and the executor pawned part of the estate to a creditor from among his creditors, it is not permissible, and the others may reject it; because it is a preferential treatment of some creditors through constructive payment (al-ifā' al-ḥukmī), so it resembles preferential treatment through actual payment (al-ifā' al-ḥaqīqī), and the commonality between them is the nullification of the rights of other creditors. Do you not see that the deceased himself does not possess that [right] during his fatal illness? So also his deputy. And if he pays off their debt before they reject it, it is valid, due to the removal of the obstacle and the arrival of their right. And if the deceased had no other creditor, then the pawn is valid, considering the actual payment. And he may sell in his debt what could not be sold before the pawn; likewise after it. And if the executor takes a pawn for a debt owed to the deceased from a person, it is valid; because it is a claim, so he owns it and may sell it. This is a summary of what is in al-Hidāyah.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling from al-Hidāyah concerning the appointment of an executor to sell a pawned item when the pawner dies without an executor. The author explains that the qāḍī (judge) appoints an executor because he is the guardian of the rights of Muslims. The executor's role includes fulfilling obligations and claiming rights. The commentary then addresses a sub-issue: if the executor pawns part of the deceased's estate to one of several creditors, this is invalid (jā'iz) and other creditors can reject it. The reasoning is that it constitutes a preferential treatment (īthār) of one creditor through a constructive payment (al-ifā' al-ḥukmī), which is analogous to actual preferential payment (al-ifā' al-ḥaqīqī) and nullifies the rights of others. The deceased himself could not do this during his fatal illness, so his deputy also cannot. However, if the executor pays off the creditors before they reject, it becomes valid because the obstacle (ẓulm) is removed. If there is only one creditor, the pawn is valid. Additionally, the executor may sell the pawned item for the debt, as the pawner's living counterpart may sell with permission. Similarly, if the executor takes a pawn for a debt owed to the deceased, it is valid as a claim (istīfā') and he may sell it. The passage relies on qiyās (analogy) between the living pawner and the deceased's executor, and between constructive and actual preferential payment.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): An executor appointed by the deceased in his will or by the judge to manage the estate and fulfill debts.
رهن (rahn): A contract of pledge where property is given as security for a debt.
إيثار (īthār): Preferential treatment of one creditor over others, which is invalid during terminal illness (marad al-mawt) as it harms other creditors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a deceased person has multiple creditors and the executor pawns part of the estate to one of them without the consent of the others, what is the ruling?
A) The pawn is valid because the executor has authority over the estate.
B) The pawn is invalid, and the other creditors may reject it.
C) The pawn is valid only if the pawned item is sold immediately.
D) The pawn is valid only if the other creditors consent later.

Key Takeaway
The judge must appoint an executor to sell a pawn when the pawner dies without one, and the executor must not preferentially treat any creditor to the detriment of others.

Ruling on Pledged Grape Juice That Becomes Wine Then Vinegar
Ruling that a pledged item remains despite change if value stays same

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a pledge when the pledged item transforms in substance, and apply the rule that for measured goods (muqaddarāt) the pledge remains valid as long as the quantity is unchanged, regardless of value changes.

Core Idea
A pledge remains valid if the quantity of the measured good stays the same, even if its substance changes.

What happens when a pledged item changes its very nature—from grape juice to wine to vinegar? Does the pledge dissolve, or does it persist? In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi ruling from al-Hidāyah that answers precisely this question. The key insight: for goods measured by volume or weight, the debt is secured by the quantity, not the value. Even a dramatic transformation like fermentation does not void the pledge if the quantity remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and apparent problem
Read the passage: grape juice worth ten dirhams was pledged for a debt of ten dirhams. Then it became wine (impure, non-saleable) and eventually vinegar (pure, saleable), still worth ten. The question: does the pledge remain valid?
The transformation from juice to wine appears to change the legal status of the object, potentially voiding the pledge.
2
Recall the general rule for pledgeable items
The passage states the general principle: 'Whatever is fit for sale is fit for pledge.' Since vinegar is saleable, it is pledgeable. But the objector raises a point: if that were the only consideration, why mention the transformation at all?
This reveals that the general rule is not enough—we need a rule specific to measured goods.
3
Apply the rule for measured goods (muqaddarāt)
The commentator clarifies that grape juice and vinegar are measured goods (by volume or weight). For such goods, the debt is tied to quantity (qadr), not value. A decrease in value (e.g., due to transformation) does not reduce the debt; only a decrease in quantity does. This is supported by analogy with a broken vessel and by consensus (ijma').
Thus, the transformation does not affect the pledge as long as the quantity of vinegar equals the original quantity of juice.
4
Determine the practical ruling and options
Since the quantity (and thus the value) remained the same, the pledge remains valid for the full debt. The pawner, however, is given an option (khiyār) if the value decreases, but that does not reduce the debt. The mention of 'vinegar' also indicates the pawner is Muslim (the ruling might differ for a non-Muslim pawner).
The key takeaway: for measured goods, substance change without quantity change does not void the pledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المتفرقات
( عصير قيمته عشرة رُهِنَ بها فتخمّر وتخلّل، وهو يعدلُها) : أي الخل يعدلُ عشرة، (بقي رهناً بها، فالحاصل أنَّ ما هو محل للبيع محل" للرهن، وما ليس محلا للبيع ليس محلاً للرهن
[ ١ ] قوله : فصل ؛ هذا الفصل بمنزلة المتفرّقة المذكورة في أواخر الكتب، فلذا أخره استداركاً لما فات فيما سبق. [٢] قوله : إنّ ما هو محل للبيع محل ... الخ ؛ أقول : لقائل أن يقول : لو كان مدار المسألة المذكورة على هذا القدر من التعليل لما ظهر فائدة قوله : وتخلل في وضع مسألة ، بل كان يكفي أن يقال : رهن بها فتخمّر بقي رهناً بها ؛ لكفاية التعليل المذكور بعينه في إثبات هذا المعنى العام، فتأمل.
وقال صاحب «العناية») : ولقائل أن يقول : ما يرجع إلى المحل فالابتداء والبقاء فيه سواء، فما بال هذا تخلف عن ذلك الأصل، وقال : ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنه كذلك فيما يكون المحل باقياً، وهاهنا يتبدَّلُ المحل حكماً بتبدل الوصف، فكذلك تخلف عن

وإنّما يوجب الخيار على ما ذكرنا ؛ لأن الغاية فيه مجرد الوصف، وفوات كلّ
شيء من الوصف في المكيل والموزون لا يوجب سقوط شيء من الدين بالإجماع، فيكون الحكم فيه أنه إن نقص شيء من القدر سقط بقدره شيء من الدين، وإلا فلا ،
وأشار بقوله : وتخلل إلى أنّ المرهون عند مسلم، والراهن فلو كان ذمياً.
Section on Miscellaneous Matters. (Grape juice worth ten was pledged for it, then it became wine and then vinegar, and it [the vinegar] equals it) i.e. the vinegar equals ten. (It remains a pledge for it. The conclusion is that whatever is fit for sale is fit for pledge, and whatever is not fit for sale is not fit for pledge.) [1] His statement: Section; this section is like the miscellaneous matters mentioned at the ends of books, so he delayed it as a completion for what was missed previously. [2] His statement: Indeed, whatever is fit for sale... etc. I say: A questioner may say: If the basis of the mentioned issue were this level of reasoning, then the benefit of his statement 'and it became vinegar' in posing the issue would not appear; rather it would have been sufficient to say: 'pledged it, then it became wine, it remains a pledge for it' because the same reasoning suffices to establish this general meaning. So ponder. And the author of al-Inayah said: A questioner may say: What pertains to the object, the beginning and the continuation are the same, so why does this [case] differ from that principle? He said: It can be answered that it is so when the object remains; but here the object is legally changed with the change of description, so likewise it differs from that principle. I say: His statement 'and it became vinegar' after his statement 'worth ten, pledged it...' indicates that the consideration is the increase and decrease in value; but it is not so; rather the consideration is the measure (quantum), because grape juice and vinegar are among measured goods (muqaddarāt); they are either measured by volume or weight. In them, a decrease in value does not cause a reduction in the debt, as in the case of a broken vessel. It only gives an option (khiyār) as we mentioned; because the objective here is merely the description. And loss of any part of the description in measured goods does not cause a reduction in the debt by consensus. So the ruling is that if any part of the measure decreases, then a corresponding part of the debt falls; otherwise no. And by his statement 'and it became vinegar' he indicated that the pawned item is with a Muslim and the pawner could be a non-Muslim.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from al-Hidāyah regarding a unique case: grape juice worth ten dirhams was pledged for a debt of ten. It then became wine (and thus legally prohibited and impure), and later turned into vinegar, still worth ten. The question is whether it remains a valid pledge. The general principle is that anything eligible for sale is eligible for pawn. Since vinegar is saleable, it remains a pledge. However, an objector asks: if the principle is simply 'whatever is saleable is pawnable', why mention the transformation? The answer is that the object has changed in description, so the principle's application is not obvious. The author of al-'Ināyah clarifies that the object itself changes legally (i.e., it becomes a different substance in legal terms), so the case is distinct from one where the object remains. The commentator then argues that the true basis is not value but quantity (qadr). Since juice and vinegar are measured goods (mikyal or mawzūn), a change in value alone does not affect the debt; only a change in quantity (e.g., evaporation) reduces the debt proportionally. The loss of value due to transformation gives the pawner an option (khiyār) to reclaim the item or demand compensation, but does not automatically reduce the debt. The mention of 'vinegar' also indicates that the ruling assumes the pawner is Muslim; if the pawner is a non-Muslim, the ruling might differ (though not elaborated here).

Key Terms
عصير (ʿaṣīr): Grape juice, which is pure and permissible to consume and sell.
مقدرات (muqaddarāt): Goods that are measured by volume (makīl) or weight (mawzūn), where the value depends on the quantity.
خيار (khiyār): Option for the pawner to void the contract or seek compensation if the pledged item's value decreases significantly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The pledge remains valid for the full debt of ten.
C) The debt is reduced to five because the value decreased.
D) The pawner may choose to redeem the pledge by paying five.

Key Takeaway
For measured goods, the pledge persists through substance changes as long as the quantity remains and the final product is saleable; the debt is tied to quantity, not value.

Ruling on Produce of the Pawn (Nama' al-Rahn)
Ruling on produce (offspring, milk, wool, fruit) of pawned property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the ownership and pawn status of the produce of a pawned item, and apply the method of partial redemption when the original pawn perishes.

Core Idea
The growth of a pawn belongs to the pledgor and is also part of the pawn; it perishes without liability.

When an item is pawned, any natural increase—offspring, milk, wool, fruit—raises questions of ownership and security. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling: such growth belongs to the pledgor but becomes part of the pawned property. This lesson unpacks the ruling, including what happens if the growth is destroyed or if the original pawn is lost while the growth remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two central rulings
Determine the ownership of the growth and its status as part of the pawn.
The growth belongs to the pledgor but is also pawned along with the original.
2
Understand liability for destruction
Note that the growth perishes without compensation if destroyed naturally.
This is because it is not separately intended in the contract.
3
Master the partial redemption method
When the original is lost, the remaining growth is redeemed by paying a proportional share of the debt.
The debt is divided based on the original's value at pawn and the growth's value at redemption.
4
Work through the example calculation
Follow the text's example: debt=10, original value=10, growth value=5. Compute shares.
Original's share is 2/3 of debt (drops), growth's share is 1/3 (redeemed).
5
Apply to a new scenario
Practice with different numbers to internalize the proportional division.
Always use original's value at pawn and growth's value at redemption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونماء الرهن كولدِهِ ولبنه وصوفه و ثمره لراهنه ، وهو رهن مع أصله، ويهلك بلا شيء، فإن هلك أصله فك بقسطه يقسمُ الدِّينُ على قيمته يومَ فكه ، وقيمة أصله يوم قبضه، وتسقط حصة أصله، وفُك بقسطه  كما إذا كان الدين عشرة ، وقيمة الأصل يوم القبض عشرة، وقيمةُ النَّماء يومَ الفك خمسة ، فثلثا العشرة حصة الأصل فيسقط، وثلث العشرة حصة النماء، فيفك به.
And the growth of the pawn, like its offspring, milk, wool, and fruit, belongs to the pledgor, and it is pawned with its origin, and it perishes without liability. If its origin perishes, it is redeemed by its share: the debt is divided by its value on the day of its redemption and the value of its origin on the day of its taking possession, and the share of the origin is dropped, and it is redeemed by its share. For example, if the debt is ten, the value of the origin on the day of taking possession is ten, and the value of the growth on the day of redemption is five, then two-thirds of the ten is the share of the origin, so it drops, and one-third of the ten is the share of the growth, so it is redeemed by it.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about the produce (offspring, milk, wool, fruit) of a pawned item. According to the Hanafi school, such produce belongs to the pledgor (raahin), but it becomes part of the pawn along with the original asset. It is destroyed without any compensation due (because it is not specifically intended in the contract). If the original pawn perishes while the produce remains, the pledge is partially released: the debt is apportioned according to the value of the original at the time of taking possession and the value of the produce at the time of redemption. The share of the original falls away, and the share of the produce is redeemed by paying that portion of the debt. An example is given: debt=10, original value at possession=10, produce value at redemption=5. The original's share = 10*(10/15)=6.66? Actually, the text says 'two-thirds of the ten is the share of the origin' (because total value 15, origin 10, so origin's share is 2/3 of debt, 6.66, but text says drops; then produce's share is 1/3 of debt, 3.33, redeemed). This ensures fairness.

Key Terms
نَماء (namaa'): Increase or growth of the pawn, including offspring, milk, wool, fruit.
رهن (rahn): Pawn or pledge; a security for a debt.
فك بقسطه (fakk bi-qistihi): Redemption of the pawn proportionally; the share of the remaining part is redeemed by paying its portion of the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mortgagee
B) The pledgor
C) Both equally
D) The loss is shared proportionally

Key Takeaway
The natural growth of a pawn belongs to the pledgor, is part of the pawn, and, if the original perishes, the growth is redeemed proportionally.

Increase in Pledge and Debt: The Hanafi Dispute
Dispute among Hanafi imams on validity of increase in pledge and debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on increasing the pledge and the debt, and explain the reasoning behind the internal dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Increasing the pledge is valid; increasing the debt is not, because it creates commonality in the pledge.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah presents a key internal dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the pawn contract (rahn). After a pledge has been made, can the debtor add more collateral or take an additional loan secured by the same item? The ruling affects everyday financial transactions, and the disagreement among the great Hanafi imams reveals important principles of analogy and contractual stability. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply analogical reasoning (qiyas) and recognize the limits of contractual modifications.

Guided Path
1
State the positions of the Hanafi imams
Identify what Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abu Yusuf hold regarding increase in the pledged property and increase in the debt.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad agree; Abu Yusuf differs on debt increase.
2
Examine the analogy for debt
Understand why Abu Yusuf analogizes debt to price in a sale: just as a price can be increased by mutual consent, so can a debt secured by a pledge.
This leads to his view that increasing the debt is valid.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) refutes Abu Yusuf by pointing out that increasing the debt creates commonality (shuyu‘) in the pledge, which is not permissible because a pledge must be specifically tied to a single obligation.
Shuyu‘ leads to uncertainty and potential conflict of interest.
4
Compare with Zufar and al-Shafi'i
Note their stricter view: both increase in pledge and increase in debt are invalid, analogizing the entire transaction to a sale where no change can be made after the contract.
The Hanafi school rejects this strict analogy on the basis of the unique nature of pawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والزيادة في الرهن تصح، وفي الدين لا هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله وعند أبي يوسف له : يجوز الزيادة في الدين أيضاً ، فإنَّ الدِّينَ بمَنْزِلَةِ الثَّمن ، والزِّيادة ... قلنا : الزّيادةُ في الدِّينِ توجبُ الشَّيوع في الرهن، وعند زفر والشافعي : لا تجوز في شيء منهما، كما لا تجوز في المبيع والشَّمنُ عندهما ، وقد مر في «البيوع».
And increase in the pledge is valid, and in the debt it is not. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And according to Abu Yusuf, increase in the debt is also permissible, because the debt is like the price. ... We said: increase in the debt necessitates commonality (shuyu') in the pledge. And according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible in either of them, just as it is not permissible in the sold item and the price according to them, and it has passed in the chapter of sales.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage discusses whether an increase in the pledged property or the debt is valid in a pawn contract. The Hanafi school has internal disagreement. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, increasing the pledge (e.g., adding another item as collateral) is valid, but increasing the debt (taking an additional loan against the same pledge) is not valid. Abu Yusuf holds that increasing the debt is also valid because the debt is analogous to the price in a sale—just as a price can be increased, so can a debt. The default Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is preferred. Zufar and al-Shafi'i go further and forbid any increase in either the pledge or the debt, analogizing to a sale where neither the object nor the price can be increased after the contract. The text notes that this ruling has already been discussed in the chapter on sales.

Key Terms
زيادة في الرهن (ziyadah fi al-rahn): Adding additional property to the pledge.
زيادة في الدين (ziyadah fi al-dayn): Incurring an additional debt that is also secured by the same pledge.
شيوع (shuyu'): Commonality or undivided share; here it means that the pledge would become shared between the original and new debt, which is problematic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why is increasing the debt secured by an existing pledge invalid?
A) Because the debt is like a price and cannot be increased
B) Because it creates commonality (shuyu‘) in the pledge
C) Because the pledge must be re-registered
D) Because the debtor's consent is not obtained

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position is that increasing the pledge is valid but increasing the debt is invalid, due to the problematic commonality (shuyu‘) created in the pledged asset.

Substitution of a Pawn (Istibdal al-Rahn)
Ruling on the status of a substitute pawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on substituting a pawn and explain why the substitute remains a trust until the original pawn is returned.

Core Idea
A substitute pawn is a trust until the original is returned; only one pawn can be in liability at a time.

This lesson clarifies a common scenario in pawn transactions: what happens when the borrower offers a different item as collateral? The Hanafi ruling draws a distinction between the liability (daman) of the original pawn and the trust status of the substitute, ensuring clarity in the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original pawn and its liability
The original pawn entered into the mortgagee's liability (daman) through possession and the existence of the debt. Both remain until the pawn is returned.
2
Understand the substitution delivery
The pawnor delivers another item of equal value as a substitute. Both parties agree to this substitution.
3
Determine the status of the original pawn
The original pawn remains a pawn until it is returned to the pawnor. It does not automatically exit liability because possession and debt persist.
4
Determine the status of the substitute
The substitute is held as a trust (amana) by the mortgagee. He is a trustee, not a pawnholder, so he is not liable for loss without negligence.
5
Identify the condition for the substitute to become a pawn
The substitute becomes a pawn only after the original pawn is returned to the pawnor. Then the substitute enters into liability, replacing the original.
The parties agreed to only one pawn in liability at a time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن رهن عبداً يعدل ألفاً بألف ، فدفع عبداً كذلك رهناً بدل الأول فهو رهن حتى يرده إلى راهنه ، ومرتهنه أمين في الآخر حتى يجعله مكان الأول، بأن يرد الأول إلى الراهن، فحينئذ يصير الثاني مضمونا. قوله : حتى يجعله مكان الأول... الخ ؛ لأنّ الأوّلَ إنّما دخل في ضمانه بالقبض والدين وهما باقيان، فلا يخرج عن الضمان إلا بنقض القبض، ما دام الدين باقيا، وإذا بقي الأول في ضمانه لا يدخلُ الثاني في ضمانه ؛ لأنهما رضيا بدخول أحدهما فيه، لا بدخولهما ، فإذا ردّ الأوّل دخلَ الثاني في ضمانه. كذا في «الهداية».
If he pawns a slave worth a thousand for a thousand, then delivers a similarly valued slave as a pawn in place of the first, then the first remains a pawn until he returns it to the pledgor, and the mortgagee is a trustee over the other until he puts it in place of the first. That is, he returns the first to the pledgor, then the second becomes a security. The commentator said: 'until he puts it in place of the first...' because the first entered into his liability by possession and debt, and both remain, so it does not exit from liability except by annulling possession, as long as the debt exists. And if the first remains in his liability, the second does not enter into his liability; because they agreed to the entry of one of them, not both. So if he returns the first, the second enters into his liability. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage outlines the ruling when a pawn is substituted with another item. If a person pawns a slave and later delivers another slave of equal value as a substitute, the original slave remains a pawn until it is returned to the pledgor. The mortgagee holds the substitute as a trust (amanah), meaning he is not liable for its loss unless he is negligent. The substitute becomes a valid pawn only after the original is returned. The reasoning from al-Hidayah is that the original entered into the mortgagee's liability (daman) through possession and the existence of the debt. Both factors persist, so the original cannot exit liability unless possession is annulled by returning it. Since the parties agreed to have only one pawn at a time, the second cannot become a pawn while the first is still in liability. Therefore, the second remains a trust until the first is returned, after which it becomes a pawn.

Key Terms
استبدال (istibdal): Substitution of one pawn for another; replacing the collateral.
أمين (ameen): Trustee; the mortgagee holds the substitute as a trust, not as a pawn, meaning he is not liable for loss without negligence.
ضمان (daman): Liability; the state of being responsible for the pawn's value if lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, what must happen for a substitute pawn to become a valid pawn?
A) It must be delivered to the mortgagee
B) The original pawn must be returned to the pawnor
C) The debt must be repaid
D) The creditor must accept it

Key Takeaway
A substitute pawn is held in trust until the original pawn is returned, due to the principle that liability (daman) is attached to possession and debt, and only one pawn can be in liability at a time.
